Two Powerful Agents vs Two Effective Agents
Razi distinguishes between shared effectiveness and shared power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two effective agents meeting on one effect and two powerful agents sharing one object of power.

Core Idea
Shared power does not mean shared effectiveness.

Razi continues his critique by showing that the original proof only rules out two effective agents acting on one effect, not two powerful agents. According to Ahl al-Sunna, God and the servant can both be powerful over the same thing, but only one is effective. This lesson clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the terms: اجتماع مؤثرين (meeting of two effectives) and اجتماع قادرين (meeting of two powerful agents).
2
Identify the proof's limitation
The original proof only proves the impossibility of two effective agents sharing one effect. Why does it not prove the impossibility of two powerful agents sharing one object of power?
3
Understand the Sunni position
Razi states the Sunni view: God is powerful over all possibles but not effective in all; the servant is powerful over some but not effective. They can both be powerful over the same thing, but only one is effective.
Effectiveness requires power plus will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفى قوله إذا ثبت أنه قادر على جميع الممكنات وجب أن لا يوجد شيء من الممكنات إلا بقدرته ففيه نظر لأنه لا يلزم من كونه قادرا على جميع الممكنات كونه مؤثرا فى جميعها وإلا لزم منه وجود جميع الممكنات وذلك أن القدرة وحدها لا تكفى فى وجود التأثير بل يحتاج معها إلى الإرادة، والدليل الذي ذكره يدل على امتناع اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد ولم أيدل على امتناع اجتماع قادرين على مقدور واحد ، بل الصحيح عند أهل السنة أن الله تعالى قادر على كل الممكنات وغير مؤثر فى كلها والعبد قادر على البعض وغير مؤثر ، فهما إذا قادران على شيء واحد ، أن المؤثر فيه أحدهما دون الآخر ، وإنما كان ذلك كذلك لكون المؤثر محتا جامع القدرة مع إلى الإرادة والقادر هو الذى له القدرة فقط من حيث هو قادر ، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يمتنع أن يكون في ممكن مؤثر غير الله تعالى إلا أن بعض ذلك بغير ماذكره وفى عبارته عند قوله أو يقع بواحد منهما وهو محال لأن المانع من وقوعه بذاك فمالم يوجد وقوعه بهذا لا يمتنع وقوعه بذاك ، موضع نظر إذ كان من الواجب أن يقول فمالم يوجد وقوعه بهذا وجب وقوعه بذاك إذ ذلك مؤثر خال عن الموانع ، وباقى الكلام هكذا فلو لم يقع بهذا وذلك وقع بذلك وبهذا وهو محال .
And in his statement: When it is established that He is powerful over all possibles, it must be that nothing of the possibles exists except by His power, there is consideration in it. For it does not follow from His being powerful over all possibles that He is effective in all of them; otherwise it would necessitate the existence of all possibles, because power alone is insufficient for the occurrence of effect, rather will is needed with it. And the proof he mentioned indicates the impossibility of two agents meeting on one effect, but it does not indicate the impossibility of two powerful agents meeting on one object of power. Rather, according to Ahl al-Sunna, God is powerful over all possibles but not effective in all of them, and the servant is powerful over some but not effective, so they are two powerful agents over one thing, but the effective one is one of them, not the other. And that is so because the effect (al-mu'aththir) must combine power with will, while the powerful agent is the one who has power only insofar as he is powerful. On this basis, it is not impossible that there is in a possible an effective other than God, except that some of that is not as he mentioned. And in his statement when he said 'or it occurs by one of them, and it is impossible because the preventer of its occurrence by that prevents its occurrence by this, it is not impossible that it occurs by that' there is a point of consideration, for he should have said: 'as long as its occurrence by this has not occurred, it must occur by that, since that is an effective free from preventers.' And the rest of the speech is like this: 'So if it did not occur by this and that, it would occur by that and this, and it is impossible.'
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage critiques an argument that proceeds from God's power over all possibles to the conclusion that nothing exists except by His power. Razi points out several logical flaws. First, power alone does not entail influence (ta'thīr); will is also needed; otherwise all possibles would exist. Second, the argument correctly proves that two influences cannot meet on one effect, but it does not prove that two powerful agents cannot share an object of power. According to Sunni doctrine, God and the servant can both be powerful over the same possible, but only one is effective (the one with both power and will). Razi also criticizes the phrasing of the original argument: the condition for one agent to act is not merely the absence of prevention from the other; rather, if one does not act, the other (if effective) must act. He corrects the logical formulation.

Key Terms
مؤثر
قادر
اجتماع مؤثرين
اجتماع قادرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God and the servant are both powerful over an action, then both must be its effective causes.
Correction: Power alone does not make one effective; effectiveness requires will. Only God is truly effective, though both have power.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say the original proof actually rule out?
A) The meeting of two powerful agents on one object of power.
B) The meeting of two effective agents on one effect.
C) The meeting of God's power and the servant's power on the same action.
D) The possibility of any action occurring without God's power.

Key Takeaway
The original proof only rules out two effective agents on one effect, not two powerful agents sharing one object of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a good deed, do you inwardly credit your own power and effort, forgetting that your ability itself is a gift from the sole Effective Agent? What would change in your heart if you truly felt that your power is only capacity, not creation?
?
Have you ever prayed earnestly for something, yet quietly doubted because you saw your own weakness? What does this lesson teach you about the relationship between your powerlessness and God's complete effectiveness?
?
If both you and God are 'powerful' regarding your next action, but only God is effective, how does that reshape your reliance on Him in your next difficult choice?

'Abbad's Argument and Razi's Answer on Knowledge and Power
Razi reports 'Abbad's claim and answers with a reductio and distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes 'Abbad's claim that divine foreknowledge removes contingency and power.

Core Idea
God's knowledge follows events, not the other way around.

'Abbad argued that if God knows something will happen, it becomes necessary, and therefore cannot be within power. Razi exposes a fatal consequence of this view and then clarifies the relationship between knowledge, occurrence, and power. Let's see how he preserves divine power while affirming foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read 'Abbad's claim and Razi's answer in Arabic and translation. Notice the two parts: the reductio and the positive argument.
2
Understand the reductio
See how Razi turns 'Abbad's logic against him: if everything known is necessary or impossible, then God has no power at all.
3
Distinguish relative vs intrinsic
Razi says the necessity is only relative to knowledge; the event remains possible in itself. This saves contingency.
4
Grasp the order of dependence
Knowledge depends on occurrence, which depends on power. The later (knowledge) does not invalidate the earlier (power). The marginal note adds that the later also does not cause the earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عباد فإنه زعم أن ما علم الله أنه يكون فهو واجب وماعلم أنه لا يكون فهو ممتنع والواجب والمتنع غير مقدور والجواب: أن هذا يقتضى أن لا يكون لله تعالى مقدور أصلا لان كل شيء فهو أما معلوم الوجود أ و معلوم العدم ثم نقول إنه وإن كان واجبا نظراً إلى العلم لكنه ممكن في نفسه فكان مقدورا ، ولأن العلم بالوقوع تبع الوقوع الذي هو تبع القدرة والمتأخر لا يبطل المتقدم (1) . (۱) أقول : المتأخر لا يبطل المتقدم لا يوجبه أيضا بل المتقدم هو الذى يوجب المتأخر إذا كان المتقدم بالعلية ، وأصل هذا الجواب مامر في المذهب المتقدم
As for 'Abbad, he claimed that what God knows will happen is necessary and what He knows will not happen is impossible, and the necessary and the impossible are not within power. The answer: This entails that God has no object of power at all, because everything is either known to exist or known not to exist. Then we say: even if it is necessary with respect to knowledge, it is possible in itself, so it is within power. And because knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence, which follows power, and the later does not invalidate the earlier. (1) I say: The later does not invalidate the earlier nor does it necessitate it; rather, the earlier is what necessitates the later if the earlier is by way of causation. And the basis of this answer is what has passed in the earlier school.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents 'Abbad's objection: God's foreknowledge makes events necessary or impossible, so they cannot be objects of power. Razi first points out a reductio: if all known events are necessary/impossible, then God has no power at all. Then he argues that the necessity is relative to knowledge, not intrinsic; the thing remains possible in itself and thus within power. Furthermore, knowledge is dependent on the occurrence, which is dependent on power; the later (knowledge) does not negate the earlier (power). The marginal note adds that the later does not cause the earlier; rather, the earlier (if it is a cause) causes the later. The basis of this answer is from the earlier school (likely Ash'ari).

Key Terms
عباد
معلوم الوجود
معلوم العدم
العلم تبع الوقوع
المتأخر لا يبطل المتقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge makes events necessary or impossible, so they are not within power.
Correction: God's knowledge does not change the intrinsic possibility of events; it follows the occurrence which follows power.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to 'Abbad's claim that God's knowledge makes events impossible to be within power?
A) He agrees but says power is still real.
B) He says events are possible in themselves and knowledge does not change that.
C) He says God's power only applies to unknown events.
D) He says the events are not really known by God.

Key Takeaway
Razi preserves both divine foreknowledge and power by distinguishing between relative necessity and intrinsic possibility, and by showing that knowledge follows power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you anticipate a future event that you know will happen, does that knowledge make you feel powerless? How does Razi's distinction between relative necessity and intrinsic possibility free you from that feeling in relation to God's knowledge?
?
Consider a past mistake you know God knew would happen. Does knowing He foreknew it make you feel like you had no choice? Confront that feeling: if the mistake was possible in itself and within your power, what does that demand of your repentance and trust in God's forgiveness?
?
If you truly believed that God's knowledge follows His power and does not constrain it, how would your daily reliance on Him change? Would you pray differently? Act differently? What stops you from living that belief today?

Abu Ali and Abu Hashim on Shared Power: Objection and Refutation
Razi reports and answers the view that God cannot have power over the exact act of the slave due to conflict of motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that conflicting motives preclude divine power over human acts and Razi's refutation based on the non-absoluteness of deterrents.

Core Idea
Conflicting motives do not automatically prevent an act; a stronger cause can override a deterrent.

This passage presents a key objection from the Mu'tazilite theologians Abu Ali and Abu Hashim against the Ash'ari view of shared power. They argue that if God and the servant both have power over the same act, conflicting motives would lead to a contradiction. Razi provides a crisp refutation, and a marginal note clarifies the conditions for shared power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, then answer, then marginal note.
2
Identify the objection
Understand why Abu Ali and Abu Hashim think God cannot have power over the exact act of the servant. They rely on the principle that existence follows complete motives and non-existence follows deterrents.
3
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi denies that a deterrent absolutely prevents existence; it does so only if no other independent cause is present. This opens the door for God's will to override the servant's deterrent.
4
Examine the marginal note
The note clarifies that shared power is possible when the act is considered abstractly, not attributed to any agent. When attributed, it becomes exclusive in that regard.
5
Reflect on the broader debate
Consider how this argument fits into the qadar debate on divine and human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما أبو على وأبو هاشم وأتباعهما فقد زعموا أن الله تعالى قادر على مثل مقدور العبد لكنه غير قادر على نفس مقدوره ، لأن المقدور من شأنه أن يوجد عند توفر دواعي القادر وأن يبقى على العدم عند توفر صارفه فلو كان مقدور العبد مقدورا الله تعالى لسكان إذا أراد الله تعالى وقوعه وكره العبد وقوعه يلزم أن يوجد لنحقنى الداعي وأن لا يوجد لتحقق الصارف وهو والجواب : أن البقاء على العدم عند تحقق الصارف ممنوع مطلقا بل ذلك إنما يجب إذا لم يقم مقامه سبب آخر مستقل ، وهذا أول المسألة (٢) عمال . (٢) أقول : إنما يمكن كون المندور مشتركا إذا أخذ غير مضاف إلى أحدهما بعد الإضافة إلى أحدهما امتنع الاشتراك فيه من حيث تلك الإضافة والمقدور غير المصاف ممكن إضافته إلى كل واحد على سبيل البدل ، والمراد من كون مقدور أحدهما مقدور الآخر
As for Abu Ali and Abu Hashim and their followers, they claimed that God is powerful over the like of the servant's object of power but not over the exact object of power, because the object of power is such that it exists when the motives of the powerful agent are complete and remains non-existent when its deterrent is complete. If the servant's object of power were God's object of power, then when God wills its occurrence and the servant dislikes its occurrence, it would have to exist due to the realization of the motive and not exist due to the realization of the deterrent – and that is impossible. The answer: The remaining non-existent when the deterrent is realized is not absolutely granted; rather, that is only necessary if no other independent cause replaces it. And this is the beginning of the matter. (2) I say: Shared power is only possible when taken as not added to either; after addition to one, sharing in it from that addition is impossible. The object of power not added is possible to be added to each on the basis of substitution, and the meaning of one's object of power being the object of power of the other...
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i and their followers. They argue that God cannot have power over the exact same act as the servant because if God wills it and the servant wills against it, it would both exist (due to God's motive) and not exist (due to the servant's deterrent). Razi's answer is that the principle that existence is prevented by a deterrent is not absolute; it holds only if there is no other independent cause that can make it exist despite the deterrent. The marginal note adds a clarification: shared power over an object is possible when the object is considered abstractly; when it is attributed to one agent, it becomes exclusive to that agent in terms of that attribution.

Key Terms
أبو علي
أبو هاشم
دواعي القادر
صارفه
المقدور المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of a deterrent absolutely prevents existence, even if a stronger cause wills it.
Correction: Razi argues that a deterrent only prevents existence if no independent cause is present; a stronger cause like God's will can overcome the deterrent.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute Abu Ali and Abu Hashim's argument that conflicting motives preclude shared power?
A) By saying that the act would exist due to God's motive regardless.
B) By denying that the deterrent is absolute when another independent cause exists.
C) By claiming that God and the servant cannot have conflicting motives.
D) By stating that power is not about existence.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection by denying the absoluteness of deterrents, asserting that a stronger cause can override, and clarifies that shared power is possible when the act is considered abstractly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you struggle to abandon. What deterrent (fear, desire, habit) do you treat as absolute, forgetting that Allah's motive can override it? Will you turn to Him tonight and ask Him to overpower that deterrent with His will, rather than relying on your own resolve?
?
When you do something good, do you attribute it entirely to yourself, or do you acknowledge that Allah also has power over that act? How would your humility and gratitude change if you truly saw every good deed as a shared power between you and Allah?
?
The lesson says non-existence is not absolute when a stronger cause intervenes. What situation are you convinced will never change because of a deterrent in your heart? Will you let this lesson dismantle that conviction and pray for Allah's overriding cause to bring change?

The Ash'ari Consensus on Divine Attributes
Razi states the Ash'ari consensus and the need to clarify the point of dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that God is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, and living by life, and why Razi emphasizes the need to uncover the actual point of dispute with opponents.

Core Idea
The dispute over attributes hinges on defining the actual point of disagreement.

Razi begins by stating the consensus of his school that God's attributes are real and distinct. He then warns that the most critical task is to identify precisely where the disagreement lies, as many differing positions are only verbal.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening consensus
Read the Arabic passage where Razi states the Ash'ari agreement on the three attributes. Reflect on the phrase 'contrary to the philosophers and Mu'tazila.'
2
Identify the key task
Note Razi's emphasis on 'uncovering the point of dispute' (الكشف عن محل النزاع). Ask yourself why this is important before entering into details.
3
Anticipate the different views
Consider that there are multiple groups that reject or reinterpret these attributes. This lesson sets the stage for the discussion that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اتفق أصحابنا على أنه تعالى عالم بالعلم قادر بالقدرة حى بالحياة خلافا للفلاسفة والمعتزلة ) وأهم المهمات في هذه المسألة الكشف عن محل النزاع فنقول : أما نفاة الأحوال فقد زعموا أن العلم نفس العالمية والقدرة نفس القادرية وهما صفتان زائدتان على الذات، واعترف أبو على وأبو هاشم هذا الزائد إلا أنهم قالوا لا يسمى هذه الأمور علما وقدرة بل عالمية وقادرية فيكون الخلاف في الحقيقة لفظيا، بل ذهب أبو هاشم إلى أنه أحوال والحال لا تعلم ولكن تعلم الذات عليها . وعندنا أن هذه الأمور معلومة في نفسها وقول أبي هاشم باطل قطعا لأن ما لا يتصور في نفسه استحال التصديق بثبوته لغيره ، وأما أبو على الجبائي فإنه سلم فيها أنها معلومة فعلى هذا لا يبقى بينه وبين نفاة الاحوال منا خلاف معنوى البتة ، وأما مثبتو الحال منا فقد زعموا أن عالمية الله تعالى صفة معللة لمعنى قائم به وهو العلم وهو لا يتحقق الخلاف بينهم وبين المعتزلة فى المعنى ، وأما نحن فلا نقول ذلك، لأن الدلالة مادلت إلا على إثبات
Issue: Our scholars agreed that He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, living by life, contrary to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila. The most important of the important matters in this issue is to uncover the point of dispute. We say: As for the rejectors of states (nafāt al-ahwāl), they claimed that knowledge is the same as the state of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) and power is the same as the state of being powerful (al-qādiriyya), and they are two attributes additional to the essence. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim acknowledged this addition, except that they said these things should not be called knowledge and power but rather the state of being knowing and the state of being powerful, so the disagreement is in fact verbal. Rather, Abu Hashim went to the view that they are states (ahwāl) and the state is not known, but the essence is known through it. According to us, these things are known in themselves, and Abu Hashim's statement is absolutely false because that which cannot be conceived in itself is impossible to affirm its existence for something else. As for Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, he conceded that they are known, so on this no real substantive difference remains between him and the rejectors of states among us. As for the affirmers of states among us, they claimed that God's being knowing is an attribute caused by a meaning subsisting in Him, which is knowledge, and thus there is no real difference between them and the Mu'tazila in meaning. As for us, we do not say that, because the evidence only establishes...
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage introduces a major topic in kalam: the reality of divine attributes. Razi states the consensus of his school (Ash'aris?) that God is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, etc., opposing philosophers and Mu'tazila. He then analyzes the point of dispute. The 'rejectors of states' (probably earlier Mu'tazila) consider the attributes identical to the states of being knowing/powerful, but admit they are additional. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim admit the addition but call them by different names, making the dispute verbal. Abu Hashim goes further, saying these are 'states' that cannot be known in themselves, only through the essence. Razi rejects this: if something is not conceivable in itself, it cannot be affirmed for something else. Abu Ali concedes they are known, so no real difference between him and the Ash'ari rejectors of states. Then the 'affirmers of states' among the Ash'aris say that God's being knowing is caused by a meaning (science), but Razi disagrees with them too, stating that the proof only establishes the attributes. The text breaks off, but the lesson covers the delineation of the various positions.

Key Terms
مسألة
الصفات
العلم
القدرة
الحياة
محل النزاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the most important preliminary task according to Razi in this issue?
A) To prove God's existence
B) To uncover the point of dispute
C) To list all divine attributes
D) To refute the philosophers

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on attributes is a starting point; the real work is in clarifying where the debate actually lies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about Allah's attributes, do you first clarify the exact point of disagreement, or do you assume you already know what the other person means? How can Razi's emphasis on 'uncovering the point of dispute' change the way you approach such conversations?
?
Razi states that the Ash'ari position on attributes is 'contrary to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila.' Have you ever truly internalized that your belief about Allah's attributes places you in opposition to other theological systems? How does that shape your commitment to learning and defending this belief?
?
The passage calls 'uncovering the point of dispute' the most important matter. Is your own heart more interested in winning arguments or in understanding the truth? What would it look like for you to prioritize clarity over victory in your next theological discussion?

The Rejectors of States and the Verbal Dispute
Razi details the view of nafāt al-ahwāl and notes the verbal nature of the dispute with Abu Ali and Abu Hashim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of the 'rejectors of states' (nafāt al-ahwāl) and why Razi considers the disagreement with them to be largely verbal.

Core Idea
What appears as a doctrinal dispute may be merely a difference in terminology.

Razi introduces the first group of opponents: those who reject the theory of states (ahwāl). He explains that they acknowledge attributes as additional to the essence but call them by different names, making the conflict essentially verbal. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim both admit the extra entity, but Abu Hashim takes it further by calling it a 'state'.

Guided Path
1
Examine the nafāt al-ahwāl view
Read how Razi describes those who reject states: they equate knowledge with 'the state of being knowing' (al-ʿālimiyya) and consider both as additional to the essence.
2
Understand the verbal twist
Notice that Abu Ali and Abu Hashim agree on the addition but object to calling it by the names 'knowledge' and 'power'. Instead they use 'worldly' and 'powerly' forms. This reduces the disagreement to words.
3
Reflect on implications
If the dispute is merely verbal, then these opponents may not truly deny the reality of attributes. Razi will later show that a real difference appears with Abu Hashim's theory of states.

Source Text

مسألة: اتفق أصحابنا على أنه تعالى عالم بالعلم قادر بالقدرة حى بالحياة خلافا للفلاسفة والمعتزلة ) وأهم المهمات في هذه المسألة الكشف عن محل النزاع فنقول : أما نفاة الأحوال فقد زعموا أن العلم نفس العالمية والقدرة نفس القادرية وهما صفتان زائدتان على الذات، واعترف أبو على وأبو هاشم هذا الزائد إلا أنهم قالوا لا يسمى هذه الأمور علما وقدرة بل عالمية وقادرية فيكون الخلاف في الحقيقة لفظيا، بل ذهب أبو هاشم إلى أنه أحوال والحال لا تعلم ولكن تعلم الذات عليها . وعندنا أن هذه الأمور معلومة في نفسها وقول أبي هاشم باطل قطعا لأن ما لا يتصور في نفسه استحال التصديق بثبوته لغيره ، وأما أبو على الجبائي فإنه سلم فيها أنها معلومة فعلى هذا لا يبقى بينه وبين نفاة الاحوال منا خلاف معنوى البتة ، وأما مثبتو الحال منا فقد زعموا أن عالمية الله تعالى صفة معللة لمعنى قائم به وهو العلم وهو لا يتحقق الخلاف بينهم وبين المعتزلة فى المعنى ، وأما نحن فلا نقول ذلك، لأن الدلالة مادلت إلا على إثبات
Issue: Our scholars agreed that He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, living by life, contrary to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila. The most important of the important matters in this issue is to uncover the point of dispute. We say: As for the rejectors of states (nafāt al-ahwāl), they claimed that knowledge is the same as the state of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) and power is the same as the state of being powerful (al-qādiriyya), and they are two attributes additional to the essence. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim acknowledged this addition, except that they said these things should not be called knowledge and power but rather the state of being knowing and the state of being powerful, so the disagreement is in fact verbal. Rather, Abu Hashim went to the view that they are states (ahwāl) and the state is not known, but the essence is known through it. According to us, these things are known in themselves, and Abu Hashim's statement is absolutely false because that which cannot be conceived in itself is impossible to affirm its existence for something else. As for Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, he conceded that they are known, so on this no real substantive difference remains between him and the rejectors of states among us. As for the affirmers of states among us, they claimed that God's being knowing is an attribute caused by a meaning subsisting in Him, which is knowledge, and thus there is no real difference between them and the Mu'tazila in meaning. As for us, we do not say that, because the evidence only establishes...

Commentary
This passage introduces a major topic in kalam: the reality of divine attributes. Razi states the consensus of his school (Ash'aris?) that God is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, etc., opposing philosophers and Mu'tazila. He then analyzes the point of dispute. The 'rejectors of states' (probably earlier Mu'tazila) consider the attributes identical to the states of being knowing/powerful, but admit they are additional. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim admit the addition but call them by different names, making the dispute verbal. Abu Hashim goes further, saying these are 'states' that cannot be known in themselves, only through the essence. Razi rejects this: if something is not conceivable in itself, it cannot be affirmed for something else. Abu Ali concedes they are known, so no real difference between him and the Ash'ari rejectors of states. Then the 'affirmers of states' among the Ash'aris say that God's being knowing is caused by a meaning (science), but Razi disagrees with them too, stating that the proof only establishes the attributes. The text breaks off, but the lesson covers the delineation of the various positions.

Misconception: Thinking that the Mu'tazila completely reject divine attributes as additional to the essence.

Checkpoint
How do Abu Ali and Abu Hashim differ from the nafāt al-ahwāl?
A) They deny any additional entity
B) They accept the addition but rename it as 'worldly' and 'powerly'
C) They claim attributes are identical to the essence
D) They affirm the theory of states

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite opponents of Ash'ari attributes may actually agree on the extra entity, differing only in terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you discuss Islam with others who differ, do you focus on the words they use or the reality they intend? Can you recall a time when an argument dissolved once you realized you both meant the same thing? How might you change your approach to future disagreements knowing that many disputes in theology are merely verbal?
?
The rejectors of states and their opponents agreed on the core reality (an extra attribute) but argued over labels. Is there a theological position you hold with great certainty that could, upon reflection, be more about terminology than truth? What would it mean for your faith if such a position turned out to be a verbal dispute?
?
This lesson reveals that even great scholars like Abu Ali and Abu Hashim might have been disagreeing over words. How does that challenge your pride in your own understanding? Are you willing to concede that some of your strongly held beliefs might be overly attached to specific phrasing rather than the underlying meaning? Commit to one concrete step this week to seek understanding over winning an argument.

Abu Hashim's Theory of States and Its Refutation
Razi presents and refutes Abu Hashim's view that attributes are unknown states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's distinctive theory that attributes are 'states' (ahwāl) that are not known in themselves, and Razi's decisive refutation of that theory.

Core Idea
A state that cannot be conceived in itself cannot be affirmed for another.

Razi now addresses the specific view of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, who introduced the theory of states (ahwāl). According to Abu Hashim, attributes like knowledge are not just renamed entities; they are 'states' that cannot be known directly but only known through the essence they qualify. Razi rejects this as logically impossible, because something completely inconceivable cannot be a real property.

Guided Path
1
Analyze Abu Hashim's claim
Read that Abu Hashim says attributes are 'states' (ahwāl) that are not known in themselves; only the essence is known through them. Consider what this implies about the reality of the attribute.
2
Understand Razi's objection
Razi argues that if the attribute cannot be conceived in itself, it cannot be affirmed to exist for something else. This is a logical principle: you cannot attribute something that has no conceivable existence.
3
Assess the force of the refutation
Reflect on why this is a 'decisive' (qaṭʿan) refutation. Without conceivability, no attribution is possible. This eliminates the theory of states as an alternative.

Source Text

According to Razi, why is Abu Hashim's view that attributes are unknown states false?

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's theory of unknowable states collapses because a real attribute must be conceivable on its own.

?

?

?
If Razi's logical test were applied to your own understanding of Allah's attributes, would they pass or fail? Are there attributes you believe in but cannot conceive? What will you do to remedy that?

Abu Ali's Concession and the Ash'ari Affirmers of States
Razi discusses Abu Ali's concession and contrasts the Ash'ari affirmers of states with his own view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Ali's admission that attributes are known removes any real disagreement, and how the Ash'ari 'affirmers of states' differ from Razi's own position.

Core Idea
When opponents concede the knowability of attributes, the dispute becomes empty; but even some Ash'aris err by introducing a causal link.

Razi continues by noting that Abu Ali al-Jubba'i conceded that attributes are known in themselves, thus eliminating any substantive difference between him and the Ash'ari rejectors of states. Then, Razi turns to the 'affirmers of states' among the Ash'aris, who claim that God's being knowing is caused by knowledge as a separate meaning. Razi distances himself from this view, stating that the proof only establishes the attributes, not a causal theory.

Guided Path
1
Examine Abu Ali's concession
Read that Abu Ali admits the attributes are known. Why does this make the dispute with the rejectors of states entirely verbal? Razi says no real substantive difference remains.
2
Introduce the Ash'ari affirmers of states
The 'affirmers of states' among the Ash'aris hold that God's being knowing is an attribute caused by a meaning (knowledge) subsisting in God. Razi says this position is actually similar to the Mu'tazila, not different.
3
Understand Razi's own view
Razi rejects the causal claim. He says the evidence only establishes the attribute, not a causal relationship between the attribute and the state. Try to grasp why he insists on stopping where the proof stops.
The passage ends abruptly, but the point is clear: don't go beyond what evidence affirms.

?

?

?

Have you ever held a position that you thought was defending the truth, only to realize it actually compromised it? How can you ensure that your beliefs are truly grounded in revelation and evidence, not just in the desire to be right?

The Ash'ari Consensus and Initial Dispute with Mu'tazila
Al-Razi states Ash'ari consensus and introduces Mu'tazila views on attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that God knows by a real knowledge and is powerful by a real power, and how some Mu'tazila (deniers of modes) and Abu Ali/Hashim approached this, potentially reducing the dispute to a verbal one.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari-Mu'tazila debate on attributes may be partly verbal but rests on deeper metaphysical assumptions.

Al-Razi begins by stating the Ash'ari consensus that God's attributes are real and additional to the essence. He then examines the Mu'tazila's denial of attributes and the nuanced views of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim, suggesting that some disagreements may be merely linguistic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Al-Razi structures his presentation: first the consensus, then the dispute.
2
Identify the three groups mentioned
Al-Razi mentions deniers of modes (Nafat al-Ahwal), Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, and the Ash'ari position. Identify their claims about the attributes.
3
Reflect on the 'verbal dispute'
Al-Razi suggests that the disagreement might be verbal because both sides affirm an additional attribute, but differ on terminology. Ask yourself: does this resolve the theological issue?
This connects to later lessons where deeper disagreements emerge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اتفق أصحابنا على أنه تعالى عالم بالعلم قادر بالقدرة حي بالحياة خلافا للفلاسفة والمعتزلة. وأهم المهمات في هذه المسألة الكشف عن محل النزاع فنقول: أما نفاة الأحوال فقد زعموا أن العلم نفس العالمية والقدرة نفس القادرية وهما صفتان زائدتان على الذات، واعترف أبو علي وأبو هاشم هذا الزائد إلا أنهم قالوا لا يسمى هذه الأمور علما وقدرة بل عالمية وقادرية فيكون الخلاف في الحقيقة لفظيا، بل ذهب أبو هاشم إلى أنها أحوال والحال لا تعلم ولكن تعلم الذات عليها. وعندنا أن هذه الأمور معلومة في نفسها وقول أبي هاشم باطل قطعا لأن ما لا يتصور في نفسه استحال التصديق بثبوته لغيره، وأما أبو علي الجبائي فإنه سلم فيها أنها معلومة فعلى هذا لا يبقى بينه وبين نفاة الأحوال منا خلاف معنوي البتة، وأما مثبتو الحال منا فقد زعموا أن عالمية الله تعالى صفة معللة لمعنى قائم به وهو العلم وهو لا يتحقق الخلاف بينهم وبين المعتزلة في المعنى، وأما نحن فلا نقول ذلك، لأن الدلالة ما دلت إلا على إثبات أمر زائد على الذات فأما على الأمر الثالث فلا دليل عليه البتة لا في الشاهد ولا في الغائب (1). (1) أقول: أكثر هذا الكلام نقل المذاهب. وقوله: في إبطال قول أبي هاشم أن ما لا يتصور في نفسه استحال التصديق بثبوته في غيره فيه نظر، لأنه إن كان المراد أن ما لا يتصور بانفراده استحال التصديق بثبوته فذلك غير مسلم لأن النسب لا تتصور بانفرادها وقد يصدق بثبوتها لغيرها، وإن كان المراد أن ما لا يتصور أصلا فهو حق وقوله: الخلاف بين أبي علي وأبي هاشم وبين أصحابنا لفظي فيه نظر، لأن الزائد عندهم ليس موجودا ولا معدوما وهو محلول العلم وهو معلول العلم الذي ليس زائدا على الذات، وعند أصحابنا أن العلم زائد وهو موجود. والباقي ظاهر.
Issue: Our companions agree that He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, living by life, contrary to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila. The most important things in this issue are to uncover the point of dispute. We say: As for the deniers of modes, they claimed that knowledge is identical to being-knowing and power is identical to being-powerful, and both are attributes additional to the essence. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim acknowledged this additional, but they said that these things are not called knowledge and power, rather being-knowing and being-powerful, so the dispute is in reality verbal. Rather Abu Hashim held that they are modes, and the mode is not known, but the essence is known through it. In our view, these things are known in themselves, and the statement of Abu Hashim is absolutely invalid, because that which is not conceivable in itself is impossible to affirm its existence for another. As for Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, he conceded that they are known, so on this no substantive dispute remains between him and the deniers of modes from us. As for the affirmers of modes from us, they claimed that God's being-knowing is an attribute caused by a meaning subsisting in Him, which is knowledge, and no dispute in meaning occurs between them and the Mu'tazila. And we do not say that, because the evidence only leads to affirming something additional to the essence; as for the third thing, there is no evidence for it at all, neither in the witnessed nor the absent. (1) I say: Most of this speech is transmission of schools. His statement: 'in invalidating the statement of Abu Hashim that what is not conceivable in itself is impossible to affirm its existence for another' - there is consideration in it. Because if he means that what is not conceivable by itself is impossible to affirm its existence, that is not accepted, because relations are not conceivable by themselves yet they may be affirmed to exist for others. And if he means that what is not conceivable at all, that is correct. His statement: 'the dispute between Abu Ali and Abu Hashim and our companions is verbal' - there is consideration in it. Because the additional in their view is neither existent nor non-existent, and it is a solved (?) science, and it is an effect of science which is not additional to the essence. According to our companions, knowledge is additional and existent. The rest is clear.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage states the Ash'ari position that God is knowing by a real knowledge, powerful by a real power, etc., contra Mu'tazila and philosophers. It then analyzes the dispute: (1) Deniers of modes (among Mu'tazila) say knowledge is the same as being-knowing, both additional. (2) Abu Ali and Abu Hashim concede the additional nature but rename them as being-knowing / being-powerful, making it a verbal dispute. (3) Abu Hashim says these are modes, which are not known directly but the essence is known through them. Al-Razi says modes are known in themselves, rejecting Abu Hashim. (4) Abu Ali al-Jubba'i concedes they are known, so no substantive dispute with Ash'ari deniers of modes. (5) Among Ash'aris, affirmers of modes say being-knowing is caused by knowledge—this agrees with Mu'tazila in meaning, but Al-Razi rejects that, saying evidence only proves one extra attribute (knowledge) not a third thing. The marginal note critiques Al-Razi's reasoning on conceivability and verbal nature of the dispute.

Key Terms
عالم بالعلم
قادر بالقدرة
أحوال
عالمية
قادرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions on attributes are completely opposite and irreconcilable.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the deniers of modes and Abu Ali/Hashim actually affirm something additional to the essence, making the disagreement partly about terminology.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, why might the dispute between Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila deniers of modes be considered verbal?
A) Because both sides deny attributes completely.
B) Because both sides affirm an additional attribute but differ on naming it.
C) Because the Mu'tazila reject all attributes.
D) Because Al-Razi says the dispute is meaningless.

Key Takeaway
The initial dispute between Ash'aris and some Mu'tazila over divine attributes may be partly semantic, as both sides accept something additional to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing,' do you truly believe His knowledge is a real attribute distinct from His essence, or do you reduce it to a mere label? How does this belief affect your trust that He knows your innermost thoughts and deeds? Commit to affirming this reality in your next prayer.
?
The Mu'tazila and Ash'aris debated over naming—but your heart knows the truth. Are you more concerned with correct labels or with the reality of Allah's attributes in your worship? What attribute of Allah do you need to actively rely on today?
?
Al-Razi shows that the dispute may be partly verbal. In your own faith, have you hidden behind verbal agreements while your heart disagrees with the reality? Which of Allah's attributes do you struggle to believe is truly with Him, and how can you move from lip-service to heartfelt conviction this week?

Al-Razi's Critique of Abu Hashim's Modes Theory
Al-Razi presents and refutes Abu Hashim's modes theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's theory of modes (ahwal) and Al-Razi's argument against it, based on the principle that what is inconceivable in itself cannot be affirmed for others.

Core Idea
If a mode is not knowable in itself, it cannot be used to know anything else – this invalidates Abu Hashim's theory.

Having noted the potentially verbal dispute, Al-Razi now turns to Abu Hashim's distinctive view that attributes are 'modes' (ahwal) which are not known directly but through which the essence is known. Al-Razi delivers a sharp critique, arguing that such a notion is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on Abu Hashim's claim that the mode is not known but the essence is known through it.
2
Understand Al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi argues: if something is not conceivable in itself, we cannot affirm it for anything else. This is a logical principle.
Think about relations: are they conceivable by themselves?
3
Compare with Abu Ali's position
Abu Ali al-Jubba'i conceded that modes are known. Al-Razi notes this eliminates any substantive dispute with the Ash'ari deniers of modes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اتفق أصحابنا على أنه تعالى عالم بالعلم قادر بالقدرة حي بالحياة خلافا للفلاسفة والمعتزلة. وأهم المهمات في هذه المسألة الكشف عن محل النزاع فنقول: أما نفاة الأحوال فقد زعموا أن العلم نفس العالمية والقدرة نفس القادرية وهما صفتان زائدتان على الذات، واعترف أبو علي وأبو هاشم هذا الزائد إلا أنهم قالوا لا يسمى هذه الأمور علما وقدرة بل عالمية وقادرية فيكون الخلاف في الحقيقة لفظيا، بل ذهب أبو هاشم إلى أنها أحوال والحال لا تعلم ولكن تعلم الذات عليها. وعندنا أن هذه الأمور معلومة في نفسها وقول أبي هاشم باطل قطعا لأن ما لا يتصور في نفسه استحال التصديق بثبوته لغيره، وأما أبو علي الجبائي فإنه سلم فيها أنها معلومة فعلى هذا لا يبقى بينه وبين نفاة الأحوال منا خلاف معنوي البتة، وأما مثبتو الحال منا فقد زعموا أن عالمية الله تعالى صفة معللة لمعنى قائم به وهو العلم وهو لا يتحقق الخلاف بينهم وبين المعتزلة في المعنى، وأما نحن فلا نقول ذلك، لأن الدلالة ما دلت إلا على إثبات أمر زائد على الذات فأما على الأمر الثالث فلا دليل عليه البتة لا في الشاهد ولا في الغائب (1). (1) أقول: أكثر هذا الكلام نقل المذاهب. وقوله: في إبطال قول أبي هاشم أن ما لا يتصور في نفسه استحال التصديق بثبوته في غيره فيه نظر، لأنه إن كان المراد أن ما لا يتصور بانفراده استحال التصديق بثبوته فذلك غير مسلم لأن النسب لا تتصور بانفرادها وقد يصدق بثبوتها لغيرها، وإن كان المراد أن ما لا يتصور أصلا فهو حق وقوله: الخلاف بين أبي علي وأبي هاشم وبين أصحابنا لفظي فيه نظر، لأن الزائد عندهم ليس موجودا ولا معدوما وهو محلول العلم وهو معلول العلم الذي ليس زائدا على الذات، وعند أصحابنا أن العلم زائد وهو موجود. والباقي ظاهر.
Issue: Our companions agree that He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, living by life, contrary to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila. The most important things in this issue are to uncover the point of dispute. We say: As for the deniers of modes, they claimed that knowledge is identical to being-knowing and power is identical to being-powerful, and both are attributes additional to the essence. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim acknowledged this additional, but they said that these things are not called knowledge and power, rather being-knowing and being-powerful, so the dispute is in reality verbal. Rather Abu Hashim held that they are modes, and the mode is not known, but the essence is known through it. In our view, these things are known in themselves, and the statement of Abu Hashim is absolutely invalid, because that which is not conceivable in itself is impossible to affirm its existence for another. As for Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, he conceded that they are known, so on this no substantive dispute remains between him and the deniers of modes from us. As for the affirmers of modes from us, they claimed that God's being-knowing is an attribute caused by a meaning subsisting in Him, which is knowledge, and no dispute in meaning occurs between them and the Mu'tazila. And we do not say that, because the evidence only leads to affirming something additional to the essence; as for the third thing, there is no evidence for it at all, neither in the witnessed nor the absent. (1) I say: Most of this speech is transmission of schools. His statement: 'in invalidating the statement of Abu Hashim that what is not conceivable in itself is impossible to affirm its existence for another' - there is consideration in it. Because if he means that what is not conceivable by itself is impossible to affirm its existence, that is not accepted, because relations are not conceivable by themselves yet they may be affirmed to exist for others. And if he means that what is not conceivable at all, that is correct. His statement: 'the dispute between Abu Ali and Abu Hashim and our companions is verbal' - there is consideration in it. Because the additional in their view is neither existent nor non-existent, and it is a solved (?) science, and it is an effect of science which is not additional to the essence. According to our companions, knowledge is additional and existent. The rest is clear.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage states the Ash'ari position that God is knowing by a real knowledge, powerful by a real power, etc., contra Mu'tazila and philosophers. It then analyzes the dispute: (1) Deniers of modes (among Mu'tazila) say knowledge is the same as being-knowing, both additional. (2) Abu Ali and Abu Hashim concede the additional nature but rename them as being-knowing / being-powerful, making it a verbal dispute. (3) Abu Hashim says these are modes, which are not known directly but the essence is known through them. Al-Razi says modes are known in themselves, rejecting Abu Hashim. (4) Abu Ali al-Jubba'i concedes they are known, so no substantive dispute with Ash'ari deniers of modes. (5) Among Ash'aris, affirmers of modes say being-knowing is caused by knowledge—this agrees with Mu'tazila in meaning, but Al-Razi rejects that, saying evidence only proves one extra attribute (knowledge) not a third thing. The marginal note critiques Al-Razi's reasoning on conceivability and verbal nature of the dispute.

Key Terms
أحوال
الحال لا تعلم
ما لا يتصور في نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if something is not directly known, it can still serve as a means of knowledge.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that if something is not conceivable in itself, we cannot even talk about it, let alone use it to know something else.

Checkpoint
What is Al-Razi's main argument against Abu Hashim's view that modes are not known?
A) Modes are actually known by intuition.
B) If a mode is not conceivable in itself, it cannot be affirmed for another.
C) Abu Hashim contradicts himself.
D) The Mu'tazila themselves reject modes.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's modes theory fails because if modes are not known in themselves, they cannot serve as a basis for knowledge of anything else.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes like His knowledge or power, do you ever find yourself treating them as abstract concepts you cannot truly understand? This passage warns against relying on unknowable intermediaries. Will you honestly examine your heart for any area where you have accepted a comfortable but logically weak understanding of Allah?
?
Al-Razi argues that if something is not conceivable in itself, it cannot be used to know anything else. Is there a belief about Allah that you hold without being able to conceive of it clearly? Are you willing to let go of that belief if it proves incoherent, even if it feels familiar?
?
The difference between Abu Hashim and Abu Ali was ultimately insubstantial because both accepted that modes are known. Do you ever find yourself arguing over minor theological distinctions while missing the core truth of knowing your Creator? Will you commit today to prioritizing clear and coherent knowledge of Allah over intellectual pride or point-scoring?

Ash'ari Affirmers of Modes and Al-Razi's Own Position
Al-Razi rejects the Ash'ari affirmers of modes and the marginal note critiques his reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of the Ash'ari affirmers of modes, Al-Razi's rejection of it, and the marginal critique of his reasoning.

Core Idea
Al-Razi insists the evidence only proves one additional attribute, not a third thing causing it – this is his core objection to the affirmers of modes.

Al-Razi now examines the view of some Ash'aris who affirm modes (ahwal) and hold that God's being-knowing is caused by a meaning (knowledge). He finds this agrees with Mu'tazila in meaning, but he rejects it because the evidence only shows one extra attribute beyond the essence, not a chain of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the main passage and the marginal note
The main passage presents Al-Razi's rejection of the Ash'ari affirmers of modes. The marginal note critiques some of Al-Razi's own arguments.
Notice the commentator challenges Al-Razi's claim about conceivability and the verbal nature of the dispute.
2
Identify the 'third thing'
Al-Razi says evidence only proves one addition to the essence (knowledge), not a third thing (like a cause). Understand what he means.
3
Analyze the marginal critique
The commentator points out issues with Al-Razi's principle about conceivability and with calling the dispute verbal. Consider if the critique is valid.

Philosophers on Divine Knowledge and the Proof for Additional Attributes
Al-Razi presents the philosophers' view on knowledge and gives a proof that attributes are additional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of knowledge as an equal form and how al-Razi argues that divine attributes are additional to the essence, along with the marginal critique of that argument.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a form; attributes are proved additional by the need for separate evidence.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the doctrine of the philosophers (falāsifa) concerning divine knowledge, explaining that for them knowledge is the presence of a form equal to the known in the intellect. He then argues that this view, along with his own independent proof, establishes that God's attributes are additional to His essence, not identical to it. The marginal notes clarify the debate between the philosophers, the Ash'ari affirmers of modes (ahwāl), and the deniers.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' definition
Read the opening sentences where al-Razi states the philosophers' view: knowledge is an equal form. Notice that this makes knowledge something additional to the essence but a necessary concomitant.
Ibn Sina explicitly affirmed this in the Book of Indications.
2
Understand the terminological shift
The philosophers concede that knowledge subsists in the essence but call it an 'exiting attribute' and 'precedence by essence'. See how al-Razi interprets this as agreement on the meaning of an additional attribute.
3
Examine al-Razi's proof
Read the proof: after knowing God exists, we need another evidence to prove He is knowing. The second known differs from the first, so the attribute is additional. Ask yourself if this is conclusive.
The marginal note (3) will critique this inference.
4
Consider the marginal critique
Read the marginal notes (2) and (3). Note how al-Razi (or the commentator) points out that the need for separate evidence only shows a difference in aspects, not necessarily distinct realities.
This nuance is crucial for the broader kalam debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فمن مذهبهم أن العلم عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية للمعلوم في العالم، فإذا كانت المعلومات مختلفة في الماهيات فيكون علم الله تعالى بالمعلومات أمورا زائدة على ذاته وهي من لوازم ذاته، وقد صرح ابن سينا بذلك في الخط السابع من كتاب الإشارات، وعلى هذا فقد سلموا أن علم الله تعالى معنى قائم بذاته إلا أنهم يعبرون عن هذا المعنى بعبارة أخرى فيقولون علم الله تعالى صفة خارجة عن ذات الله تعالى متقومة بتلك الذات، فكأنهم عبروا عن المعنى بالصفة الخارجة وعن القيام بالذات بالتقدم بالذات، فظهر أنهم يساعدون في هذه المسألة عن المعنى، بل يبقى الخلاف بينهم وبين مثبتي الحال منا فإنهم لا يقولون إلا بالذات، وتلك الصور اللازمة للذات ومثبتو الحال منا قالوا بأمور ثلاثة الذات والعالمية والعلم، فظهر أن الذي يقوله نفاة الحال منا متفق عليه بين كل من أقر بكون الله تعالى عالما قادرا (2). لنا أنا بعد العلم بكونه تعالى موجودا يفتقر إلى دليل آخر يدل على كونه عالما قادرا والمعلوم ثانيا غير المعلوم أولا فعليه تعالى زائد على ذاته (3). احتج الخصم بأمور. (2) أقول: ابن سينا صرح بكون العلم صورة زائدة على الذات ولم يذكر قوله في القدرة، ثم ذكر أخيرا أن قولها يوافق قول من أقر بكونه تعالى عالما قادرا، والفلاسفة يقولون إن علم الله تعالى فعل يوجد به هو صادر عنه والعلم والقدرة والإرادة عندهم واحد بالحقيقة مختلف بالاعتبار، ونحن لا نقول بذلك وهم يقولون العلم ليس بمحمول على الذات إنما هو مبدأ العالمية المحمولة على الذات، فالعالمية هي أيضا لا يقولون بها. (3) أقول: افتقار العلم بالوجود إلى دليل آخر يدل على العلم لا يدل على تغاير الوجود والعلم، الصانع مغاير للدليل الدال على أنه واحد، ومع ذلك لا يلزم كون الصانع الموجود فان الدليل الدال على وجود غير ذلك الواحد وأيضا إذا دل الدليل على وجوده وآخر على كون وجوده عين ذاته لم يدل ذلك على أن وجوده غير كون وجوده غير ذاته يدل ذلك على تغاير الاعتبارين لا على تغاير الحقيقتين.
As for the philosophers, their doctrine is that knowledge is the obtaining of a form equal to the known in the intellect. So if the known things are different in essences, then God's knowledge of the known things is something additional to His essence, and it is among the necessities of His essence. Ibn Sina has stated this in the seventh line of the Book of Indications. On this, they have conceded that God's knowledge is a meaning subsisting in His essence, except that they express this meaning with another expression, saying 'God's knowledge is an attribute exiting from God's essence, subsisting through that essence.' It is as if they expressed the meaning by the exiting attribute, and the subsistence by the essence by the precedence by essence. So it appears that they support in this issue the meaning; rather the dispute remains between them and the affirmers of modes among us, because they (philosophers) only affirm the essence and those forms necessary for the essence, while the affirmers of modes from us said three things: the essence, being-knowing, and knowledge. So it appears that what the deniers of modes among us say is agreed upon by everyone who affirms that God is knowing and powerful (2). Our proof: After knowing that He exists, we need another evidence to prove that He is knowing and powerful. The second known is different from the first known, so [the attribute] is additional to His essence (3). The opponent argued with some things. (2) I say: Ibn Sina explicitly stated that knowledge is a form additional to the essence, and he did not mention his statement about power. Then he mentioned at last that their statement agrees with the statement of one who affirmed that He is knowing and powerful. The philosophers say that God's knowledge is an act by which He exists, and He is the source of it, and knowledge, power, and will are one in reality but different in consideration. We do not say that. They say that knowledge is not predicated of the essence; rather it is the principle of being-knowing predicated of the essence. So being-knowing also, they do not affirm it. (3) I say: The need for another evidence to prove knowledge after knowing existence does not prove the distinctness of existence and knowledge. The Maker is different from the evidence that points to His oneness, yet it does not necessitate that the Maker is the existing being (?) And if evidence points to His existence and another to that His existence is identical to His essence, that does not prove that His existence is other than His existence being identical to His essence; it proves a difference in aspects, not a difference in realities.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' view and gives a proof for attributes being additional. First, the philosophers: knowledge is a form equal to the known; since knowns differ, God's knowledge is additional to the essence but necessary to it. Ibn Sina said this. They concede knowledge subsists in essence but call it an 'exiting attribute'. Al-Razi notes this supports the meaning (that knowledge is a real attribute), leaving a dispute with Ash'ari mode-affirmers on whether there are three things (essence, being-knowing, knowledge) or two (essence and forms). Then Al-Razi gives his own proof: after proving God exists, we need a separate proof for His being knowing and powerful; the second known (the attribute) differs from the first (existence), so they are additional. The marginal notes critique: the proof does not necessarily prove distinct realities, just distinct aspects; the philosophers reduce knowledge to an act, differing from Ash'aris. The note also argues that Ibn Sina's position aligns with those who affirm knowing and powerful.

Key Terms
صورة مساوية
لوازم الذات
صفة خارجة
تقدم بالذات
مثبتو الحال
نفاة الحال
افتقار إلى دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we need a separate proof for an attribute after proving existence, then the attribute must be a distinct entity from the essence.
Correction: The marginal note argues that the need for separate evidence only shows a difference in aspects (اعتبارات), not necessarily distinct realities. Separate proofs do not automatically entail ontological distinctness.

Checkpoint
According to the proof given by al-Razi, why are attributes additional to the essence?
A) Because the philosophers say so.
B) Because after knowing God's existence, we need another evidence to prove He is knowing, so what is proven is different from existence.
C) Because the attributes are not predicated of the essence.
D) Because they are modes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi presents the philosophers' view that divine knowledge is a form additional to the essence, and then argues that the need for a separate evidence for attributes proves their addition, though the marginal notes caution that this proof may only show a difference in aspects.

?

But consider your own knowledge of God: is it merely an intellectual concept, or does it truly transform how you live? If your knowledge of God were stripped down to just a mental form, would your heart still be moved to obedience?
?
Al-Razi's proof uses the need for separate evidence to argue that attributes are additional. But the marginal note warns that separate evidence may only show a difference in aspects, not realities. Reflect: Do you ever confuse the evidence for a thing with the thing itself? Is your faith built on real certainty or on arguments that could be reinterpreted?

In your personal worship, do you ever relate to God through His attributes (e.g., His mercy, His power) as if they are distinct from Him? How does your understanding of this issue affect the way you call upon Him by His names?

The Philosophers' Doctrine of Divine Knowledge as Superadded Forms
Razi reports the philosophers' doctrine and compares to Ash'arite views on states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' view that God's knowledge is a form superadded to His essence, and how this compares to the Ash'arite positions on states.

Core Idea
Philosophers affirm divine knowledge as an attribute, but call it an external form.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi reports and analyzes the position of the philosophers (falāsifa) on the nature of divine knowledge. He shows that despite their different terminology — calling knowledge a 'form' or 'external attribute' — they ultimately agree with the Ash'arite affirmers of state that knowledge is not identical to the essence. Understanding this comparison clarifies the real points of agreement and disagreement in the debate over divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow of Razi's argument.
Notice how Razi starts with the philosophers' definition and then draws comparisons.
2
Identify the philosophers' definition
Note that the philosophers define knowledge as the occurrence of a form equal to the known in the world (or in the knower).
This definition implies multiplicity of forms due to diverse known things.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's statement
Recognize that Ibn Sina explicitly asserts that knowledge is something additional to the essence, though a necessary concomitant.
This shows the philosophers are not denying an attribute.
4
Compare terminology between philosophers and Ash'arites
See how the philosophers express the same reality as 'external attribute' while the Ash'arites use 'attribute subsisting in the essence.'
Razi says they agree on the meaning but differ in expression.
5
Distinguish the three positions
Realize the contrast: philosophers (essence + forms), Ash'arite affirmers of state (essence + knowing + knowledge), and Ash'arite negators of state (essence only? but Razi says they agree with all who affirm God is knowing).
Razi concludes that the negators of state are actually in agreement with the philosophers and others on the basic point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فمن مذهبهم أن العلم عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية للمعلوم في العالم ، فإذا كانت المعلومات مختلفة في الماهيات فيكون علم الله تعالى بالمعلومات أمور زائدة على ذاته وهى من لوازم ذاته ، وقد صرح ابن سينا بذلك في الخط السابع من كتاب الإشارات ، وعلى هذا فقد سلموا أن علم الله تعالى معنى قائم بذاته إلا أنهم يعبرون عن هذا المعنى بعبارة أخرى فيقولون علم الله تعالى صفة خارجة عن ذات الله تعالى متقومة بتلك الذات ، فكأنهم عبروا عن المعنى بالصفة الخارجة وعن القيام بالذات بالتقدم بالذات ، فظهر أنهم يساعدون في هذه المسألة عن المعنى ، بل يبقى الخلاف بينهم وبين مثبتى الحال منا فإنهم لا يقولون إلا بالذات ، وتلك الصور اللازمة للذات ومثبتو الحال منا قالوا بأمور ثلاثة الذات والعالمية والعلم ، فظهر أن الذي يقوله نفاة الحال منا متفق عليه بين كل من أقر بكون الله تعالى عالما قادرا
As for the philosophers, their doctrine is that knowledge is the occurrence of a form equal to the known in the world, so if the known things are different in essences, then God's knowledge of known things is something additional to His essence, and it is among the necessary concomitants of His essence. Ibn Sina explicitly stated this in the seventh line of the Book of Indications. On this basis, they have conceded that God's knowledge is a meaning subsisting in His essence, except that they express this meaning with another expression, saying that God's knowledge is an attribute external to God's essence, constituted by that essence. So it is as if they expressed the meaning by the external attribute, and the subsistence by the essence by priority by essence. Thus it becomes clear that they agree on the meaning in this issue; rather, the disagreement remains between them and the affirmers of state (muthbitū l-ḥāl) among us, for they (the philosophers) affirm nothing except the essence, and these forms are necessary for the essence, while the affirmers of state among us affirmed three things: the essence, knowing (al-ʿālimiyya), and knowledge. So it becomes clear that what the negators of state (nufāt al-ḥāl) among us say is agreed upon by everyone who acknowledges that God is knowing and powerful.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a report of the philosophers' position on divine knowledge. Razi states that the philosophers define knowledge as the presence of a form of the known in the knower. Since the known things are diverse in essences, God's knowledge of them must be a plurality of forms superadded to His essence, though these forms are necessary concomitants of the essence. He cites Ibn Sina's Ishārāt. Razi then notes that the philosophers implicitly affirm that knowledge is an attribute subsisting in the essence, but they express it differently: they call it an 'external' attribute 'constituted' by the essence. Thus they agree on the reality of the attribute, differing only in terminology. The real disagreement is between them and the Ash'arite 'affirmers of state' (muthbitū l-ḥāl), who posit three distinct entities: essence, the attribute of knowing (ʿālimiyya), and knowledge. The philosophers only affirm the essence and its necessary forms. Razi concludes that the view of the 'negators of state' among the Ash'arites (i.e., those who deny that attributes are states) is actually agreed upon by all who affirm that God is knowing and powerful, meaning that the core point—that knowledge is something over and above the essence—is universally conceded.

عالمية
حال
نفاة الحال
مثبتو الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers deny any attribute in God, equating knowledge with essence.
Correction: Razi shows they affirm knowledge as a form superadded to essence, thus conceding an attribute, though expressed as 'external'.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what do the philosophers affirm about God's knowledge?
A) It is identical to the essence.
B) It is a form superadded to the essence.
C) It is a non-existent state.
D) It is entirely denied.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the philosophers, despite their different terminology, affirm that God's knowledge is something over and above His essence, thus aligning with the minimal Ash'arite position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that even the philosophers, often accused of denying attributes, still affirm that God's knowledge is real and necessary. Reflect: Have you ever treated God's attributes as mere labels, empty of real meaning? Where did you learn that? Today, resolve to study each divine attribute with the conviction that it describes a genuine perfection in your Lord.

Razi shows that disagreements can be about words rather than substance. Think of a recent argument you had about religion: was it over terminology or over truth? Ask Allah to help you discern real differences from mere expressions, and to purify your heart from pride that clings to empty words.
?
If you truly believe that God's knowledge is a real attribute that encompasses everything, does your daily life reflect that awareness? Do you act as if every thought, word, and deed is known? This lesson confronts you with the reality of God's knowledge. Will you let it change how you live today?

Proof that Divine Knowledge is Superadded from Need for a Separate Proof
Razi presents a proof that God's knowledge is superadded to His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that because proving God's attributes requires a separate proof from proving His existence, those attributes must be additional to His essence.

Core Idea
Separate proof means separate reality – attributes are not identical to essence.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a concise but powerful proof that God's attributes of knowledge and power are superadded to His essence. He argues from the fact that proving God's existence does not automatically prove His attributes; a distinct proof is needed. This distinction in our knowledge reflects a real distinction in reality. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice the concise style of Razi's argument.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage contains two premises: (1) after knowing God's existence, we need another proof for His attributes, and (2) what is known second is different from what is known first.
3
Understand the conclusion
From the premises, Razi concludes that the attribute (e.g., knowledge) is additional to the essence. Reflect on why this follows logically.
The argument assumes that if the attribute were identical to essence, knowing existence would suffice to know the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أنا بعد العلم بكونه تعالى موجودا يفتقر إلى دليل آخر يدل على كونه عالما قادرا والمعلوم ثانيا غير المعلوم أولا فعليه تعالى زائد على ذاته
We [argue] that after knowing that He, exalted, exists, we need another proof to show that He is knowing and powerful. And the known second is other than the known first; therefore [the attribute] is additional to His essence.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a proof (lana) for the position that God's knowledge and power are additional to His essence. The reasoning is: 1) We first come to know God's existence through one proof. 2) We then need a separate proof to establish that He is knowing and powerful. 3) Since the second known fact (that He is knowing) is different from the first known fact (that He exists), the attribute of knowledge must be something over and above the essence. The argument assumes that if knowledge were identical to essence, then knowing existence would entail knowing the attribute, which is not the case.

Key Terms
علم
دليل
ذات
عالما
قادرا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the order of proof only reflects our limited understanding, not a real distinction in God.
Correction: Razi argues that if the attribute were identical to essence, proving existence would automatically prove the attribute, which is not the case. Thus, the need for a separate proof indicates a real distinction.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Razi's proof that knowledge is additional to essence?
A) God's essence is simple.
B) Proving existence does not automatically prove attributes.
C) Knowledge is a contingent accident.
D) The philosophers say so.

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof uses the order of knowledge to argue for the ontological distinction between God's essence and His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This proof shows that knowing God exists is not enough—you must also know His attributes. Have you settled for a vague 'creator' without plumbing the depths of His specific attributes? What step will you take this week to seek deeper knowledge of one of His names?

The First Objection: Superadded Knowledge Makes the Essence Both Agent and Recipient
The opponent's first argument against superadded knowledge, claiming it entails an impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the view that divine knowledge is superadded to the essence, and why the opponent considers it impossible.

Core Idea
The opponent argues that superadded knowledge would force the impossible: God being both cause and receiver of the same attribute.

The debate over whether God's attributes are identical to His essence or superadded is a foundational issue in Islamic theology. The first objection against the superadded view contends that if knowledge were additional, it would depend on the essence for its existence, making the essence both its cause and its subject — a logical impossibility. This lesson examines that objection in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise logical structure.
2
Identify the core argument
Trace the opponent's reasoning: superadded knowledge → dependence → contingency → essence as cause and subject → impossibility.
Pay attention to the terms 'agent' (fā'il) and 'recipient' (qābil).
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection is important for the broader debate on divine attributes. What view is it attacking?
The answer to this objection is given earlier in the text, but we focus here on understanding the objection itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم بأمور . أحدها : أن علمه لو كان زائدا على ذاته لكان مفتقراً إلى ذاته فيكون ممكنا لذاته واجبا لعلة وتلك العلة ليست إلا تلك الذات والموصوف به ليس إلا الذات فتكون الذات فاعلة وقابلة معا وهو محال . والجواب عن الأول قد تقدم .
The opponent argued with [the following] things. First: If His knowledge were superadded to His essence, it would be dependent on His essence, thus it would be contingent in itself and necessary through a cause, and that cause is none other than that essence, and that which is described [by knowledge] is none other than the essence, so the essence would be both agent and recipient, which is impossible. The answer to the first has already preceded.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage states the first objection from the opponent against the view that divine knowledge is superadded. The opponent argues: if knowledge is additional to the essence, then knowledge would be contingent per se and necessary through another (the essence). That would make the essence both the cause of knowledge and the subject that receives it, which is impossible because a thing cannot be both agent and patient. The text notes that the answer to this objection has been given earlier (presumably in a previous section of the book).

Key Terms

مفتقر
فاعل
قابل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the objection is about having multiple eternal entities, but the objection specifically focuses on the impossibility of one essence being both cause and effect of the same attribute.
Correction: The objection does not claim multiplicity per se, but rather that a single essence cannot be both the cause of an attribute and the subject that receives it, as this would entail a contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what impossibility would arise if knowledge were superadded?
A) Knowledge would be eternal.
B) The essence would be both agent and recipient.
C) God would be multiple.
D) Knowledge would be finite.

Key Takeaway
The first objection claims that superadded knowledge leads to the absurdity of the essence being both agent and recipient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How might this subtle assumption affect your trust in His absolute power and wisdom?
?
If you encountered a theological puzzle that seemed logically contradictory, would you dismiss it outright or humbly accept that God's reality transcends your reasoning? Can you recall a moment when you had to submit your intellect to a divine truth that felt impossible to fully grasp?
?
The author notes that the answer to this objection has already been given. When you hear a strong argument against a belief you hold, do you immediately seek out the counterargument, or do you let doubt linger? What does that reveal about the strength of your conviction?
