Second Objection: Necessity of God's Being Knowing
Razi refutes the necessity-based independence claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that God's attribute of being knowing (ʿālimiyya) is necessary and therefore independent of any cause.

Core Idea
A necessary attribute can still depend on its cause; necessity does not guarantee self-sufficiency.

In this passage, Razi addresses a second objection: the opponent argues that since God's being knowing is necessary, it must be self-sufficient and need no cause. Razi counters by distinguishing how the Ash'arites conceive of the attribute and by exposing a logical flaw in the opponent's reasoning. This lesson unpacks his sophisticated refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opponent's claim: God's being knowing (ʿālimiyya) is necessary, and the necessary is independent of a cause. Notice the implicit premise that necessity entails self-sufficiency.
2
Examine Razi's first response
Razi points out that the objection only applies to those who first affirm ʿālimiyya and then explain it by an additional attribute (knowledge). The Ash'arites do not do this—they identify ʿālimiyya with the attribute of knowledge.
This denies the opponent's assumption that ʿālimiyya is a separate necessary state.
3
Examine Razi's second response
Even assuming the opponent's framework, Razi forces a choice: Is ʿālimiyya necessary per se or through another? If per se, that is the very point in dispute. If through another (knowledge), its necessity does not make it independent of that cause, as seen in the physical world.
This refutes the idea that necessity implies independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : أن عالمية الله تعالى واجبة والواجب يستغنى بوجوه عن الدالة . والجواب : أنه إنما يتوجه على من أثبت عالميته ثم يعللها بمعنى ونحن لا نقول به ، وأيضا فبتقدر الفول نقول الواجب متى لا يعلل إذا كان واجبا بذاته أو بغيره ، والأول مسلم لكن لم قلت أن عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لذاته بل هذا أول المسألة ، والثانى باطل لأن وجوب العالمية بالعلم لا يوجب استغناؤه عنه كما في الشاهد .
Second: That God's being knowing (ʿālimiyya) is necessary, and the necessary is independent by its necessity from a cause. The answer: It only applies to one who affirms His being knowing and then explains it by a meaning (attribute), and we do not say that. Also, if we assume the statement, we say: The necessary is not caused when it is necessary by itself or by another. The first is conceded, but why do you say that God's being knowing is necessary per se? This is exactly the point of the issue. The second is false because the necessity of being-knowing through knowledge does not entail its independence from it, as seen in the witnessed realm.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and Razi's answer. The opponent argues that God's attribute of being knowing (ʿālimiyya) is necessary, and what is necessary is self-sufficient and needs no cause. Razi replies in two ways: 1) This objection only applies to those who first affirm ʿālimiyya and then explain it by an additional attribute (like knowledge); but the Ash'arites do not do that—they hold that ʿālimiyya is exactly the attribute of knowledge, not a separate necessary state. 2) Even if we accept the opponent's framework, we ask: is ʿālimiyya necessary per se or through another? If per se, that is precisely the point of dispute—why assume it is necessary per se? If through another (i.e., through knowledge), then its necessity does not make it independent of that cause, just as in the physical world a necessary effect still depends on its cause.

Key Terms
عالمية
واجب
استغناء
علة
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that ʿālimiyya is a necessary attribute without cause, so it must be independent.
Correction: Razi shows that either ʿālimiyya is the very attribute of knowledge (so not separate) or if necessary through knowledge, it still depends on that cause.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's main critique of the second objection?
A) He denies that God is knowing.
B) He argues that the opponent's assumption that worldliness is necessary per se is the very question.
C) He says the objection is irrelevant.
D) He concedes the point.

Key Takeaway
Razi's refutation shows that claiming an attribute is necessary does not make it independent of its cause; the cause (knowledge) remains essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that your worship or good deeds, because they are 'obligatory' (wājib), should be effortless and require no intentional cause from you? In what specific area of your life has this assumption caused you to neglect the inner work of sincerity?
?
If God's attribute of knowledge is not independent but is His very essence, how does that reshape your understanding of His intimacy with your every thought? Do you truly believe that He knows you more intimately than you know yourself?
?
The opponent thought necessity means independence. Reflect: In your own life, is there something you consider 'necessary'—like prayer, honesty, or justice—yet you treat it as if it should come naturally without effort? What would change if you recognized that even necessary things require a cause (your will and action)?

Co-Eternity Does Not Imply Similarity
Objection and answer on co-eternity and similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sharing the attribute of pre-eternity does not necessitate similarity between the divine essence and its attributes.

Core Idea
Sharing a description is not sharing an essence.

The third objection claims that if knowledge is eternal like the essence, they must be similar. Imam Razi refutes this by showing that sharing a property (pre-eternity) does not entail equal essence. The example of opposites illustrates the point: two opposites share the property of being opposite yet are not similar. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim followed by Razi's counter.
The objection has two parts: co-eternity implies similarity, and then a further point about which is essence or attribute.
2
Analyze the opponent's reasoning
Identify why the opponent thinks co-eternity leads to similarity. They assume any shared property makes two things alike in essence.
This assumption is the weak point Razi attacks.
3
Understand Razi's answer
Razi distinguishes between sharing a description (positive or negative) and sharing an essence. Pre-eternity is a description, not the essence.
The example of opposites (e.g., black and white) sharing the description of being opposite without being similar is key.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for Islamic theology: it defends the distinction between Allah's essence and attributes while affirming their co-eternity.
Without this answer, one might fall into polytheism or anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : لو كان له علم قديم لكان مشاركا للذات في القدم وذلك يقتضى تماثلها ، وأن لا يكون أحدهما بكونه ذاتا والآخر صفة أولى من العكس لأنها تكون مغايرة للذات فيلزم القول بقدماء مغايرة . الجواب : أن الاشتراك فى القدم اشتراك فى الوصف سلي أو ثبوت وذلك لا يوجب التماثل أصلا كما أن الضدين لا يلزم من اشتراكهما فى التضاد مماثلتهما .
Third: If He had an eternal knowledge, it would share in pre-eternity with the essence, and that necessitates their similarity, and that it is not more appropriate that one is essence and the other attribute rather than the reverse, because they are distinct from the essence, so it would necessitate the belief in distinct eternal entities. The answer: Participation in pre-eternity is participation in a description, whether negative or positive, and that does not necessitate similarity at all, just as two opposites do not necessitate similarity from their sharing in being opposite.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its answer. The opponent argues: if knowledge is eternal like the essence, then both share pre-eternity, which would imply similarity (tamāthul). Then neither would be more fitting as essence or attribute, leading to multiple eternal entities. Razi's answer: Sharing a description (pre-eternity) does not entail essential similarity. For example, two opposites (like black and white) share the description of being opposites, but they are not similar in essence. Likewise, the essence and knowledge can share the property of pre-eternity without being similar.

Key Terms
قديم
مشارك
تماثل
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a property like pre-eternity, they must be essentially similar.
Correction: Sharing a description (e.g., pre-eternity) does not entail essential similarity; properties can be common without essence being common.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute the claim that co-eternity implies similarity?
A) He denies that knowledge is eternal.
B) He gives the example of opposites sharing a description.
C) He says the essence is not eternal.
D) He accepts the objection.

Key Takeaway
Sharing a description like pre-eternity does not necessitate essential similarity; the divine essence and attributes remain distinct despite co-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as 'Eternal', do you ever catch yourself picturing His eternity as something like an endless stretch of time, thereby making it similar to your own limited concept of time? How can you remind yourself that sharing the name 'Eternal' does not mean sharing the reality?
?
Think of a person you disagree with about a core belief. You both claim to love Allah, but you differ. Do you assume that because you share the description 'believer', you must be essentially similar in your faith? How does Razi's distinction help you honor that differences in understanding do not erase shared love for the same Lord?
?

Answering the Fourth Objection: Divine Knowledge and Temporality
Objection and answer about God's knowledge being temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's response to the objection that God's knowledge, being similar to human knowledge, must be temporal.

Core Idea
Sharing a relation does not mean sharing temporality.

The fourth objection attempts to liken God's knowledge to human knowledge, arguing that since our knowledge is temporal, His must be too. Razi's sharp answer dissects the meaning of 'difference' between two knowledges, showing that the objection relies on an ambiguous term. This lesson walks through his precise response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the opponent's claim: God's knowledge relates to the same objects as our knowledge, so it must resemble it, and therefore be temporal like ours.
Note the key term 'taghāyur' (difference) which Razi will analyze.
2
Find Razi's three-part answer
Razi outlines three possible meanings of 'difference'. Identify each one: 1) numerical distinction, 2) separability in time/place, 3) some other meaning.
He shows that only the first is true, but it doesn't imply temporality.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Razi refuses to use the term 'difference' for God's attributes even though they are distinct. How does this guard against anthropomorphism?
This aligns with the principle of tanzīh (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : أن علم الله تعالى المتعلق بمعلومنا يجب أن يكون مثل أعلمنا فيلزم من حدوث علمنا حدوث علمه . الجواب : أنكم إن عنيتم بالتغاير كون كل واحد منهما مخالفا للآخر فهو كذلك، لكنا لا نطلق هذا اللفظ لعدم الإذن وإن عنيتم جواز المفارقة في الزمان والمكان والثبوت والعدم فلم قلتم به ، وإن عنيتم معنى ثالثا فبينوه .
Fourth: That God's knowledge related to our known object must be like our knowledge, so from the temporality of our knowledge follows the temporality of His knowledge. The answer: If you mean by 'difference' that each one is distinct from the other, then it is so, but we do not use this term due to lack of permission. If you mean the possibility of separation in time, place, existence, or non-existence, then why do you say that? If you mean a third meaning, then explain it.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and Razi's answer. The opponent argues that since God's knowledge relates to the same objects as our knowledge, it must be similar, and since our knowledge is temporal, His must be temporal too. Razi's answer distinguishes three possible meanings of 'difference' (taghāyur): 1) numerical distinction (each is not the other)—this is true, but it does not imply temporality. 2) temporal or spatial separation—this is not necessarily true for God's attributes. 3) some other meaning—the opponent must clarify. Razi says they do not use the term 'difference' for God's attributes because of lack of scriptural permission, even if they are conceptually distinct.

Key Terms
معلوم
حدوث
تغاير
مفارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's knowledge and human knowledge both relate to the same object, they must be similar in nature, so God's knowledge must be temporal.
Correction: Razi shows that sharing a relation does not entail sharing temporality; the term 'difference' is ambiguous and does not automatically imply separation in time.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's main point in answering the fourth objection?
A) He admits God's knowledge is temporal.
B) He argues that sharing a relation does not entail sharing temporality.
C) He denies that God's knowledge relates to our objects.
D) He says difference implies separation.

Key Takeaway
Razi rebuts the claim that God's knowledge must be temporal by clarifying that 'difference' (taghāyur) does not necessarily mean separation in time or place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that because God knows your sins, His knowledge must be like a regrettable memory? How does Razi's distinction between numerical difference and temporal separation confront that assumption in your heart?
?
When you try to imagine God's knowledge, do you unconsciously picture it as a larger version of your own? What in this passage forces you to stop and repent of that limitation?
?
If God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging, yet He knows every detail of your changing life, does that make you feel distant or comforted? Which feeling dominates, and why?

The Fifth Objection: Infinite Knowables Imply Infinite Knowledges
Razi presents the fifth objection and his two-part answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that infinite knowables would require infinite knowledges in God and how Razi refutes it by distinguishing between shared relation and similarity.

Core Idea
Two things sharing a relation does not make them identical; God's knowledge is not like ours.

Razi continues addressing objections against the doctrine of divine attributes. Here, the opponent argues that since God knows infinitely many things, His knowledge would have to be infinite in number, which is absurd. Razi's response is subtle: he points out that the mere fact that our knowledge and God's knowledge both relate to the same object does not imply they are similar, nor does the temporality of our knowledge imply temporality of His.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage for the fifth objection. Notice the opponent's premise: knowledge of each known is distinct, and God's knowables are infinite, so His knowledges must be infinite.
2
Identify Razi's first answer
Razi says that our knowledge and God's knowledge share a relation to the same known, but sharing some concomitants does not imply similarity. Consider this distinction carefully.
The text contains a unclear word 'الميتين'; likely meaning 'the two' (e.g., the two knowledges).
3
Understand the second answer
Even if we grant similarity, the temporality of our knowledge does not entail temporality of God's knowledge. Razi gives an analogy: existence is common to God and creatures, but God's existence is eternal while ours is temporal.
This answer rejects the inference from temporal to eternal.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Compare the analogy of existence: just as commonality in existence does not force temporality, commonality in knowledge relation does not force multiplicity or temporality.
This shows the opponent's argument is not valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها : أن العلم بكل المعلوم غير العلم بغيره على ما تقدم ومعلومات الله تعالى غير متناهية فيلزم أن يكون له علوم غير متناهية . الجواب عن الخامس : أن علمه المتعلق بمعلومنا مع علمنا يشتركان فى التعلق بذلك المعلوم ، ولا يلزم من اشتراك الميتين فى بعض اللوازم عائلتهما ، وائن سلمناه لكن لا يلزم من حدوث علمنا حدوث علمه كما لا يلزم من كون وجوده تعالى مساويا لوجودنا فى كونه وجودا حدوث وجوده . وعن السادس : أن ما ألزمتم علينا في العلم يلزمكم في نفس العالمية وهذه المعارضة لازمة على جميع الشبه .
Fifth: That knowledge of each known is different from knowledge of another as established earlier, and God's known things are infinite, so it would necessitate that He has infinite knowledges. The answer to the fifth: That His knowledge related to our known and our knowledge share in relation to that known, and from the sharing of the two [??] in some concomitants, their similarity is not necessary. And even if we concede it, the temporality of our knowledge does not necessitate the temporality of His knowledge, just as the fact that His existence is equal to our existence in being existence does not necessitate the temporality of His existence. And about the sixth: That what you required of us regarding knowledge applies to you regarding the very attribute of knowing (ʿālimiyya), and this counter-argument applies to all the doubts.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and answer, plus an additional answer (the sixth). The opponent argues that since knowledge of each known is distinct, and God's knowables are infinite, He would have infinite knowledges, which is absurd. Razi's answer: 1) The fact that two knowledges share a relation to the same known does not entail their similarity. 2) Even if we grant similarity, the temporality of our knowledge does not imply the temporality of His, just as existence in common does not imply temporality. Then Razi adds a sixth point: the same problem (infinity of attributes) applies to the opponent's own concept of 'the attribute of knowing' (ʿālimiyya), if it is considered a real attribute. This is a counter-argument that undermines all the opponent's doubts.

Key Terms
معلوم
علم
غير متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God knows many things, His knowledge must be multiple or composite.
Correction: God's knowledge is one simple attribute that encompasses all knowables without multiplication or division, as argued by Razi's response.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Razi's second answer to the fifth objection?
A) He denies that God knows many things.
B) He says that temporality of our knowledge does not necessitate temporality of God's knowledge.
C) He admits that God has infinite knowledges.
D) He claims that knowledge is not an attribute.

Key Takeaway
Two knowledges sharing a relation does not imply their similarity, and temporality of one does not transfer to the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever limit God's knowledge in your mind, thinking He doesn't see every detail of your life? Razi shows that God's knowledge is not like ours—it is one, eternal, and infinite. How does this truth confront your tendency to hide sins or neglect accountability? What will you do today to live with the awareness that Allah knows all, yet His knowledge is perfect and single?
?
Razi uses the analogy of existence: both God and creatures exist, but one is eternal and the other temporal. Apply this to your own life: your existence is fleeting, but God's is permanent. How does this humble you? In what specific area are you living as if your existence is the measure of reality, forgetting the eternal perspective? Write a concrete commitment to realign your priorities today.
?
The objection claims that infinite objects require infinite knowledges in God, which seems logical to human reasoning. Razi's answer shows that divine attributes are not bound by human categories. Are you willing to submit your intellect to the reality that God is beyond comparison, even when your mind protests? What is one area of aqeedah where you struggle to accept a divine attribute simply because it seems paradoxical? Pray for a heart that says 'سَمِعْنَا وَأَطَعْنَا'.

The Sixth Answer: A Counter-Argument
Razi delivers a counter-argument that the opponent's reasoning boomerangs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi turns the opponent's argument against them by pointing out that the same reasoning applies to their own concept of the attribute of being knowing (ʿālimiyya).

Core Idea
If your argument works, it also destroys your own position.

After answering the fifth objection, Razi adds a powerful counter-argument: the very problem the opponent raises about knowledge applies equally to their own concept of the attribute of 'being knowing' (ʿālimiyya). If infinite knowables require infinite knowledges, then by the same reasoning, the attribute of knowing would also have to be infinite. This shows the opponent's criticism is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth answer passage
Read the Arabic text for the sixth answer. Notice it is very short but packs a strong logical punch.
The text says: 'And about the sixth: that what you required of us regarding knowledge applies to you regarding the very attribute of knowing.'
2
Understand the counter-argument
Razi argues that if infinite objects of knowledge require infinite knowledges (as the opponent claims), then the same requirement would hold for the attribute of 'being knowing' (ʿālimiyya) if it is considered a real attribute. The opponent cannot escape this consequence.
This is a tu quoque (you also) argument.
3
Reflect on the scope
Razi says this counter-argument applies to all the previous doubts, meaning it is a general refutation of the opponent's methodology.
It undermines the entire line of objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Fifth: That knowledge of each known is different from knowledge of another as established earlier, and God's known things are infinite, so it would necessitate that He has infinite knowledges. The answer to the fifth: That His knowledge related to our known and our knowledge share in relation to that known, and from the sharing of the two [??] in some concomitants, their similarity is not necessary. And even if we concede it, the temporality of our knowledge does not necessitate the temporality of His knowledge, just as the fact that His existence is equal to our existence in being existence does not necessitate the temporality of His existence. And about the sixth: That what you required of us regarding knowledge applies to you regarding the very attribute of knowing (ʿālimiyya), and this counter-argument applies to all the doubts.

The Five Objections Against Knowledge as an Additional Attribute
Imam Razi enumerates five objections against the attribute of knowledge being additional to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five logical objections raised by opponents against the doctrine that God's knowledge is an attribute additional to His essence.

Core Idea
The five objections argue that additional attributes lead to logical contradictions and multiplicity.

Imam Razi begins by presenting the arguments of those who deny that knowledge is a distinct attribute. These objections aim to show that positing an additional attribute leads to absurdities such as composition, contingency, multiplicity, and similarity to creation. Understanding these objections is crucial before examining their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the five objections
Read each objection carefully, noting the logical structure and the assumptions behind them.
2
Identify the key concepts
Notice how each objection appeals to concepts like contingency, necessity, similarity, and infiniteness.
The third and fifth objections involve multiple eternal entities and infinite regress.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why each objection would be considered problematic for the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : أن علمه لو كان زائدا على ذاته لكان مفتقراً إلى ذاته فيكون ممكنا لذاته واجبا لعلة وتلك العلة ليست إلا تلك الذات والموصوف به ليس إلا الذات فتكون الذات فاعلة وقابلة معا وهو محال . وثانيها : أن عالمية الله تعالى واجبة والواجب يستغنى بوجوه عن الدالة . وثالثها : لو كان له علم قديم ل كان مشاركا للذات في القدم وذلك يقتضى تماثلها ، وأن لا يكون أحدهما بكونه ذاتا والآخر صفة أولى من العكس لأنها تكون مغايرة للذات فيلزم القول بقدماء مغايرة . ورابعها : أن علم الله تعالى المتعلق بمعلومنا يجب أن يكون مثلا أعلمنا فيلزم من حدوث علمنا حدوث علمه . وخامسها : أن العلم بكل المعلوم غير العلم بغيره على ما تقدم ومعلومات الله تعالى غير متناهية فيلزم أن يكون له علوم غير متناهية . والجواب عن الأول قد تقدم . وعن الثاني : أنه إنما يتوجه على من أثبت عالميته ثم يعللها بمعنى ونحن لا نقول به ، وأيضا فبتقدر القول نقول الواجب متى لا يعلل إذا كان واجبا بذاته أو بغيره ، والأول مسلم لكن لم قلت أن عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لذاته بل هذا أول المسألة ، والثانى باطل لأن وجوب العالمية بالعلم لا يوجب استغناؤه عنه كما في الشاهد. وعن الثالث : أن الاشتراك فى القدم اشتراك فى الوصف سلبى أو ثبوت وذلك لا يوجب التماثل أصلا كما أن الضدين لا يلزم من اشتراكهما فى التضاد مماثلتهما . وعن الرابع : أنكم إن عنيتم بالتغاير كون كل واحد منهما مخالفا للآخر فهو كذلك، لكنا لا نطلق هذا اللفظ لعدم الإذن وإن عنيتم جواز المفارقة في الزمان والمكان والثبوت والعدم فلم قلتم به ، وإن عنيتم معنى ثالثا فبينوه . وعن الخامس : أن علمه المتعلق بمعلومنا مع علمنا يشتركان فى التعلق بذلك المعلوم ، ولا يلزم من اشتراك الشيئين فى بعض اللوازم تشاكلهما ، وإن سلمناه لكن لا يلزم من حدوث علمنا حدوث علمه كما لا يلزم من كون وجوده تعالى مساويا لوجودنا فى كونه وجودا حدوث وجوده . وعن السادس : أن ما ألزمتم علينا في العلم يلزمكم في نفس العالمية وهذه المعارضة لازمة على جميع الشبه. وبالله التوفيق .
First: That if His knowledge were additional to His essence, it would be in need of His essence, thus it would be contingent in itself and necessary by a cause, and that cause is none other than that essence, and the described is none other than the essence, so the essence would be both agent and recipient, which is impossible. Second: That the omniscience of God the Exalted is necessary, and the necessary is self-sufficient in essence from the proof. Third: If He had an eternal knowledge, it would share in eternity with the essence, and that would entail their similarity, and that it would not be more appropriate for one to be essence and the other attribute than the reverse, because they would be distinct from the essence, thus requiring multiple eternal entities. Fourth: That God's knowledge related to our known must be like our knowledge, so from the temporality of our knowledge would follow the temporality of His knowledge. Fifth: That knowledge of each known is different from knowledge of another, as previously established, and God's known things are infinite, so He would have infinite knowledges. The answer to the first has preceded. As for the second: It is directed only against one who affirms His omniscience then explains it by a meaning, and we do not say that. Also, assuming the statement, we say: When does the necessary not require a cause? If it is necessary in itself or by another. The first is granted, but why do you say that God's omniscience is necessary in itself? This is the very point of the question. The second is false because the necessity of omniscience through knowledge does not entail its independence from it, as in the witnessed. As for the third: Sharing in eternity is a sharing in a negative or positive attribute, and that does not entail similarity at all, just as from two opposites sharing in opposition, their similarity does not follow. As for the fourth: If you mean by difference that each is different from the other, then it is so, but we do not use this term due to lack of permission; if you mean the permissibility of separation in time, place, existence, and non-existence, then why do you assert it? If you mean a third meaning, clarify it. As for the fifth: His knowledge related to our known and our knowledge share in relation to that known, and from the sharing of two things in some concomitants, their similarity does not follow; and even if we grant it, from the temporality of our knowledge does not follow the temporality of His knowledge, just as from the fact that His existence is equal to our existence in being existence, the temporality of His existence does not follow. As for the sixth: What you have required of us regarding knowledge applies to you regarding omniscience itself, and this counter-argument is necessary against all the doubts. And success is from God.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents five objections against the view that God's knowledge is an attribute additional to His essence, followed by their refutations. The objections are based on logical consequences, divine simplicity, and comparison with human knowledge. The answers are nuanced: the first objection is dismissed as already answered; the second is refuted by distinguishing between necessary-in-itself and necessary-by-another; the third by denying that sharing in a non-essential attribute entails similarity; the fourth by clarifying the ambiguity of 'difference'; the fifth by rejecting parallelism with human knowledge; and the sixth by turning the argument against the opponents themselves. Each answer respects the unique nature of divine attributes.

Key Terms
علم
ذات
عارض
قديم
مثل
متغاير
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sharing an eternal attribute implies similarity of essences.
Correction: Sharing a negative or positive attribute does not necessitate the essences being similar, just as two opposites share the attribute of 'being opposite.'

Checkpoint
What is the main thrust of the third objection against knowledge being additional to the essence?
A) It would make God composite.
B) It would entail multiple eternal entities.
C) It would make knowledge contingent.
D) It would contradict God's omniscience.

Key Takeaway
The five objections challenge the coherence of an additional attribute by pointing to logical consequences that Ash'aris must address.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge were like yours, it would be limited and temporal. How does this lesson's fourth objection confront your tendency to imagine Allah's attributes in human terms? What specific thought or action can you take today to affirm that His knowledge is utterly unlike yours?
?
The first objection shows that if knowledge were additional, Allah would be both agent and recipient—an impossibility. In what areas of your life do you unconsciously attribute human limitations to Allah? How can you transform your worship to reflect His absolute perfection?
?
The third objection warns against implying multiple eternal entities. Have you ever fallen into thinking of Allah's attributes as semi-independent 'parts'? Repent by affirming that His attributes are not separable from His essence, and let this truth deepen your reverence in every prayer.

Refutations of the Objections Against Additional Knowledge
Imam Razi refutes each objection and presents a counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes each of the five objections against the additional attribute of knowledge, including a counter-argument.

Core Idea
Imam Razi answers each objection by clarifying concepts and turning the argument against the opponents.

Having presented the objections, Imam Razi now provides careful refutations. He notes that the first objection has already been answered, and then systematically deconstructs the remaining four. His answers often involve distinctions between different meanings of terms, such as 'difference' and 'similarity', and he demonstrates that the objections do not hold if properly understood.

Guided Path
1
Read the first two answers
Note how Razi dismisses the second objection by questioning the assumption that omniscience is necessary in itself.
2
Analyze the answer to the third objection
Focus on the point that sharing in a negative attribute does not imply similarity.
3
Understand the answer to the fourth objection
Razi distinguishes between different meanings of 'difference' to undermine the objection.
4
Examine the answer to the fifth and the counter-argument
Razi rejects the parallel with human knowledge and then turns the tables on the opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : أن علمه لو كان زائدا على ذاته لكان مفتقراً إلى ذاته فيكون ممكنا لذاته واجبا لعلة وتلك العلة ليست إلا تلك الذات والموصوف به ليس إلا الذات فتكون الذات فاعلة وقابلة معا وهو محال . وثانيها : أن عالمية الله تعالى واجبة والواجب يستغنى بوجوه عن الدالة . وثالثها : لو كان له علم قديم ل كان مشاركا للذات في القدم وذلك يقتضى تماثلها ، وأن لا يكون أحدهما بكونه ذاتا والآخر صفة أولى من العكس لأنها تكون مغايرة للذات فيلزم القول بقدماء مغايرة . ورابعها : أن علم الله تعالى المتعلق بمعلومنا يجب أن يكون مثلا أعلمنا فيلزم من حدوث علمنا حدوث علمه . وخامسها : أن العلم بكل المعلوم غير العلم بغيره على ما تقدم ومعلومات الله تعالى غير متناهية فيلزم أن يكون له علوم غير متناهية . والجواب عن الأول قد تقدم . وعن الثاني : أنه إنما يتوجه على من أثبت عالميته ثم يعللها بمعنى ونحن لا نقول به ، وأيضا فبتقدر القول نقول الواجب متى لا يعلل إذا كان واجبا بذاته أو بغيره ، والأول مسلم لكن لم قلت أن عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لذاته بل هذا أول المسألة ، والثانى باطل لأن وجوب العالمية بالعلم لا يوجب استغناؤه عنه كما في الشاهد. وعن الثالث : أن الاشتراك فى القدم اشتراك فى الوصف سلبى أو ثبوت وذلك لا يوجب التماثل أصلا كما أن الضدين لا يلزم من اشتراكهما فى التضاد مماثلتهما . وعن الرابع : أنكم إن عنيتم بالتغاير كون كل واحد منهما مخالفا للآخر فهو كذلك، لكنا لا نطلق هذا اللفظ لعدم الإذن وإن عنيتم جواز المفارقة في الزمان والمكان والثبوت والعدم فلم قلتم به ، وإن عنيتم معنى ثالثا فبينوه . وعن الخامس : أن علمه المتعلق بمعلومنا مع علمنا يشتركان فى التعلق بذلك المعلوم ، ولا يلزم من اشتراك الشيئين فى بعض اللوازم تشاكلهما ، وإن سلمناه لكن لا يلزم من حدوث علمنا حدوث علمه كما لا يلزم من كون وجوده تعالى مساويا لوجودنا فى كونه وجودا حدوث وجوده . وعن السادس : أن ما ألزمتم علينا في العلم يلزمكم في نفس العالمية وهذه المعارضة لازمة على جميع الشبه. وبالله التوفيق .
First: That if His knowledge were additional to His essence, it would be in need of His essence, thus it would be contingent in itself and necessary by a cause, and that cause is none other than that essence, and the described is none other than the essence, so the essence would be both agent and recipient, which is impossible. Second: That the omniscience of God the Exalted is necessary, and the necessary is self-sufficient in essence from the proof. Third: If He had an eternal knowledge, it would share in eternity with the essence, and that would entail their similarity, and that it would not be more appropriate for one to be essence and the other attribute than the reverse, because they would be distinct from the essence, thus requiring multiple eternal entities. Fourth: That God's knowledge related to our known must be like our knowledge, so from the temporality of our knowledge would follow the temporality of His knowledge. Fifth: That knowledge of each known is different from knowledge of another, as previously established, and God's known things are infinite, so He would have infinite knowledges. The answer to the first has preceded. As for the second: It is directed only against one who affirms His omniscience then explains it by a meaning, and we do not say that. Also, assuming the statement, we say: When does the necessary not require a cause? If it is necessary in itself or by another. The first is granted, but why do you say that God's omniscience is necessary in itself? This is the very point of the question. The second is false because the necessity of omniscience through knowledge does not entail its independence from it, as in the witnessed. As for the third: Sharing in eternity is a sharing in a negative or positive attribute, and that does not entail similarity at all, just as from two opposites sharing in opposition, their similarity does not follow. As for the fourth: If you mean by difference that each is different from the other, then it is so, but we do not use this term due to lack of permission; if you mean the permissibility of separation in time, place, existence, and non-existence, then why do you assert it? If you mean a third meaning, clarify it. As for the fifth: His knowledge related to our known and our knowledge share in relation to that known, and from the sharing of two things in some concomitants, their similarity does not follow; and even if we grant it, from the temporality of our knowledge does not follow the temporality of His knowledge, just as from the fact that His existence is equal to our existence in being existence, the temporality of His existence does not follow. As for the sixth: What you have required of us regarding knowledge applies to you regarding omniscience itself, and this counter-argument is necessary against all the doubts. And success is from God.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents five objections against the view that God's knowledge is an attribute additional to His essence, followed by their refutations. The objections are based on logical consequences, divine simplicity, and comparison with human knowledge. The answers are nuanced: the first objection is dismissed as already answered; the second is refuted by distinguishing between necessary-in-itself and necessary-by-another; the third by denying that sharing in a non-essential attribute entails similarity; the fourth by clarifying the ambiguity of 'difference'; the fifth by rejecting parallelism with human knowledge; and the sixth by turning the argument against the opponents themselves. Each answer respects the unique nature of divine attributes.

Key Terms
معارضة
شاهد
سلبى
ثبوت
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In his answer to the third objection, how does Imam Razi argue that sharing in eternity does not imply similarity?
A) Eternity is a positive attribute that all beings share.
B) Sharing in a negative attribute does not entail similarity, as with opposites.
C) Knowledge and essence are identical in all respects.
D) The opponent misunderstands the term 'eternity'.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's refutations show that the objections rely on misunderstandings of key concepts and can be answered without compromising the doctrine of additional attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Razi uses a counter-argument in the sixth response, turning the opponent's argument back on them. How often do you apply this method when you encounter doubts about your faith? Are you willing to critically examine your own assumptions about God as deeply as Razi did?

Refuting the Analogy: God's Will Is Not Essential
Razi refutes the analogy between essential knowledge and essential will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between God's knowledge and His will fails, and why essential will does not necessitate universality.

Core Idea
An essential attribute need not be universal; will differs from knowledge.

Imam Razi engages with a key Ash'ari argument that denies God's will is essential. The opponents draw an analogy with knowledge: just as essential knowledge encompasses all, essential will would encompass all, leading to contradictions. Razi dismantles this analogy, exposing its flawed reasoning. Let's examine his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the argument. Note the positions of Abu Ali and Abu Hashim and the refutation.
2
Identify the opponents' analogy
The opponents argue that if will were essential, it would be universal like knowledge. Find this comparison in the text.
The analogy is: as essential knowledge = knowledge of all, so essential will = will of all.
3
Understand the refutation
Razi rejects the analogy. He points out that the premise (knowledge being essential) is not granted, and even if it were, will could still be particular. Trace his two counterpoints.
The editor adds that the analogy is merely a tamthīl (example), not a binding proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : البارى تعالى ليس مريدا لذاته وهو قول أبى على وأبى هاشم والخلاف فيه مع التجار. لنا ما تقدم في مسألة العلم. واحتج أبو على وأبو هاشم على أنه تعالى ليس مريدا لذاته بأنه لو كان كذلك لكان مريدا لجميع المرادات، كما أنه لما كان عالما لذاته كان عالما بكل المعلومات، لكن ذلك محال، لأن زيدا إذا أراد موت رجل وعمرو أراد حياته فلو كان الله تعالى مريدا لكل المرادات للزم أن يكون مريدا لموته وحياته معا وهو محال. ولقائل أن يقول: لم قلت لو كان مريدا لذاته لكان مريدا لكل المرادات، والقياس على العلم لا يسمن ولا يغني من جوع. وقولهم: لما كانت المريدية صفة ذاتية لم يكن تعلقها ببعض المرادات أولى من تعلقها بالباقي فقد عرفت ضعفه. (1) أقول : ما تقدم في مسألة العلم وهو أن كون العلم بذاته مغايرا للعلم بإرادته يقتضى تغايرهما وقياس الإرادة على العلم لا يفيد التعين لكونه تمثيلا ولا إلزام لأن المقيس عليه ممنوع وهو كون العلم له بذاته . وقوله : وقد عرفت ضعفه إشارة إلى أن الإرادة على تقدير كونها ذاتية لم لا يجوز أن تتعلق ببعض المرادات دون البعض.
Issue: The Preeternal Exalted is not willing by His essence; this is the view of Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, and the disagreement is with al-Tajjar. We have what preceded in the issue of knowledge. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim argued that He is not willing by His essence, because if He were, He would will all objects of will, just as when He was knowing by essence, He was knowing all known things. But that is impossible, because if Zaid wills the death of a man and Amr wills his life, then if God willed all objects of will, it would entail that He wills his death and life together, which is impossible. Someone may say: Why do you say that if He were willing by essence He would will all objects of will? The analogy with knowledge does not avail nor satisfy hunger. And their statement: Since the quality of will is an essential attribute, its relation to some objects of will would not be more appropriate than to the rest – you have already known its weakness. (1) I say: What preceded in the issue of knowledge is that the fact that knowledge is by essence is different from knowledge of His will, which entails their difference; and analogizing will to knowledge does not yield determinacy because it is a mere analogy and does not oblige, since the basis of analogy is denied, namely that knowledge is by His essence. And his statement 'you have already known its weakness' indicates that will, assuming it is essential, why should it not attach to some objects of will and not others?
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of whether God is willing by His essence. It begins by stating the position of Abu Ali and Abu Hashim that God is not willing by essence, with disagreement from 'al-Tajjar'. The author says that the proof from the knowledge issue (previously discussed) supports his view. The opponents' argument is then presented: if God were willing by essence, He would will all things, just as His essential knowledge encompasses all; but that is impossible because it would lead to willing contradictory things. The author then provides a refutation: the analogy with knowledge is flawed, as one can question the premise that essential will must be universal. The editor's note adds that the previous argument about knowledge being by essence is not accepted by all, and that even if will were essential, it could still be particular. The lesson thus exposes the weakness of the opponents' reasoning.

ذات
صفة ذاتية
مرادات
قياس
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An essential attribute must apply to all instances of its category (like knowledge).
Correction: The text shows that essentiality does not entail universality; will could be essential yet particular.

Checkpoint
What is the core of the opponents' analogy that the author refutes?
A) Will is like knowledge in being essential and universal.
B) God's will must be eternal like knowledge.
C) Will cannot be additional to essence.
D) Essential attributes are all necessary.

Key Takeaway
Essential will does not require universality; the analogy with essential knowledge is flawed and does not prove that God is not willing by essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that God's will must be predictable or uniform like His knowledge? How does this assumption affect your trust in His wisdom when you face situations that seem contradictory? Will you actively remind yourself that His will can be specific and wise even when you don't understand?
?
When you see conflicting events in your life—loss and gain, joy and sorrow—do you doubt that God wills both? How can you anchor your faith in the truth that His will is not bound by human logic, as this lesson shows? Will you commit to meditating on this when trials arise?
?
This lesson refutes the need for will to be universal; God's will is particular. Are you living as if you believe that? Or do you secretly think God must will everything in a general way, leaving you to handle specifics? Will you trust Him with the details starting today?

Against a Created Will in God
Imam Razi states the issue and gives the proof against a created will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God cannot will by a created will because it would lead to an infinite regress of wills.

Core Idea
If God's will were created, it would need another will, causing an endless chain.

Imam Razi begins by stating the position that God does not will by a created will, against the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya. He then presents the key proof: every originated thing requires a will, so if will itself were originated, it would require another will, leading to an infinite regress. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the key terms 'إرادة حادثة' (created will) and 'تسلسل' (infinite regress).
2
Understand the core argument
Identify the premise that origination requires will, and see how applying it to the will itself creates a chain.
This is a classic kalam argument against created attributes.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why infinite regress is impossible for God's attributes, and how this supports the doctrine of uncreated will.
The argument assumes that a will caused by another will would be contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا يجوز أن يكون الله تعالى مريدا بإرادة حادثة خلافا للمعتزلة والكرامية. فهو تعالى مريد بإرادة محدثة لا في محل. وأما عند الكرامية فهو مريد بإرادة يخلقها في ذاته لنا أن إحداث الشيء لا يصح إلا بالإرادة على ما تقدم، فلو كانت الإرادة حادثة لافتقرت إلى إرادة أخرى ولزم التسلسل. (٢) أقول : لهم أن يقولوا عليه أنكم أبيتم الإرادة لترجح أحد وقتى الإيجاد على سائر أوقاته وجوزتم أن للقادر أن يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر من غير مرجح، فلم لا يجوز أن يصدر عن القادر إرادة بلا مرجح ثم تصير تلك الإرادة مرجحة لما عداها فلا يلزم التسلسل.
Issue: It is not permissible for God the Exalted to be willing by a created will, contrary to the Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya. He the Exalted is willing by an originated will not in a locus. As for the Karramiyya, He is willing by a will He creates in His essence. We have that the origination of a thing is only valid by will, as preceded; so if the will were originated, it would need another will and an infinite regress would follow. (2) I say: They may say against it: You have refused will to prefer one time of origination over other times, and you have permitted that the capable can prefer one of his capable acts over another without a preponderator; so why is it not permissible that from the capable an will issues without a preponderator, and then that will becomes a preponderator for what follows, so that regress does not follow?
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This lesson presents the issue of whether God wills by a created will. The author states the position that it is impermissible, against the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya. He explains that God's will is an originated will not in a locus (for his own school), while the Karramiyya say God creates a will in His essence. The proof is that origination requires a will, so if the will itself were originated, it would require another will, leading to infinite regress. The editor then adds a possible counterobjection: the opponents could question why the first will cannot arise without a preponderator, just as the author already allows God to prefer one act over another without a preponderator. If that is allowed, then the original will could arise without a preponderator, and then serve as preponderator for further acts, avoiding regress. The lesson thus presents both sides of the argument.

Key Terms
إرادة حادثة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will is a created attribute like human will.
Correction: If God's will were created, it would require another will to create it, leading to an infinite chain, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that God's will cannot be created?
A) Because a created will would be in a locus.
B) Because a created will would require another will to create it, leading to infinite regress.
C) Because God does not need a will.
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny it.

Key Takeaway
God's will is an uncreated attribute; if it were created, it would require an infinite regress of wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will were created, it would be dependent on something else. What does it mean for you personally to rely on a will that is absolutely independent and uncreated—do you truly trust that God's will for your life is not subject to change or deficiency?
?
In your daily decisions, do you act as if God's will is just another factor among many, or do you recognize it as the sole and sufficient cause of everything? How would your anxiety level change if you internalized that nothing happens without His uncreated will?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible for God. Are there areas in your spiritual life where you seek 'infinite regress'—endless reasons why you aren't closer to God—instead of accepting that He is the Final Cause and turning to Him directly?

Objection: Could a Will Arise Without a Preponderator?
Imam Razi records an objection from the opponents against his proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a potential objection to the infinite regress argument: that a will might arise directly from God without a preponderator, avoiding regress.

Core Idea
If God can prefer one act without a cause, He could create a will without a prior will.

Imam Razi presents an objection from his opponents: if God can simply choose one of two acts without a preponderator (as the author seems to allow), then why couldn't He create a will directly without needing a prior will? This would stop the regress. This lesson examines that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting how the opponents use Razi's own principle against him.
The objection hinges on the concept of 'preponderator' (مرجح).
2
Understand the logic of the objection
The opponents argue that if God can prefer one time or act without a reason, then He can create a will without a reason.
This challenges the assumption that every contingent needs a cause.
3
Consider the implications
Think about how Razi might respond. The objection is left unanswered here, so the lesson is to understand the point.
A full response would come elsewhere in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا يجوز أن يكون الله تعالى مريدا بإرادة حادثة خلافا للمعتزلة والكرامية. فهو تعالى مريد بإرادة محدثة لا في محل. وأما عند الكرامية فهو مريد بإرادة يخلقها في ذاته لنا أن إحداث الشيء لا يصح إلا بالإرادة على ما تقدم، فلو كانت الإرادة حادثة لافتقرت إلى إرادة أخرى ولزم التسلسل. (٢) أقول : لهم أن يقولوا عليه أنكم أبيتم الإرادة لترجح أحد وقتى الإيجاد على سائر أوقاته وجوزتم أن للقادر أن يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر من غير مرجح، فلم لا يجوز أن يصدر عن القادر إرادة بلا مرجح ثم تصير تلك الإرادة مرجحة لما عداها فلا يلزم التسلسل.
Issue: It is not permissible for God the Exalted to be willing by a created will, contrary to the Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya. He the Exalted is willing by an originated will not in a locus. As for the Karramiyya, He is willing by a will He creates in His essence. We have that the origination of a thing is only valid by will, as preceded; so if the will were originated, it would need another will and an infinite regress would follow. (2) I say: They may say against it: You have refused will to prefer one time of origination over other times, and you have permitted that the capable can prefer one of his capable acts over another without a preponderator; so why is it not permissible that from the capable an will issues without a preponderator, and then that will becomes a preponderator for what follows, so that regress does not follow?
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This lesson presents the issue of whether God wills by a created will. The author states the position that it is impermissible, against the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya. He explains that God's will is an originated will not in a locus (for his own school), while the Karramiyya say God creates a will in His essence. The proof is that origination requires a will, so if the will itself were originated, it would require another will, leading to infinite regress. The editor then adds a possible counterobjection: the opponents could question why the first will cannot arise without a preponderator, just as the author already allows God to prefer one act over another without a preponderator. If that is allowed, then the original will could arise without a preponderator, and then serve as preponderator for further acts, avoiding regress. The lesson thus presents both sides of the argument.

مرجح
قادر
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the infinite regress argument is unanswerable.
Correction: The opponents object that if God can act without a preponderator, He can create a will without a prior will, stopping the regress.

Checkpoint
What principle do the opponents use to object to Razi's argument?
A) That a created will is impossible.
B) That God can prefer one act over another without a preponderator.
C) That infinite regress is impossible.
D) That will must be uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The objection suggests that if God can act without a cause, He could create a will directly, circumventing the infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This objection suggests that if God can act without a reason, then His will might be arbitrary. Does this disturb you? How do you reconcile the idea of a wise, purposeful God with the possibility that some of His actions have no discernible cause?
?
Imagine you are defending the belief that God's will is eternal. How would you answer this objection in a way that does not rely on logic alone but also on trust in God's perfection? What personal conviction would you lean on?

intermediate

God is not willing by His essence
Razi presents and refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that God is not willing by His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God is not willing by His essence and Razi's objection to their analogy with knowledge.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites argued that if God willed by essence, He would will all objects, including opposites.

In this lesson, we examine a key debate between the Ash'ari school and the Mu'tazilites regarding the attribute of will. The passage presents the Mu'tazilite position (Abu Ali and Abu Hashim) that God's will is not essential but superadded. The author recounts their proof by analogy with knowledge and then raises an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: statement of position, proof, consequence, objection.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim and proof
The Mu'tazilites claim God is not willing by His essence. Their proof: if He were, He would will all objects of will, just as He knows all known things by essence.
3
Understand the problematic consequence
They argue that willing all objects would include willing opposites (e.g., life and death of a man), which is impossible. This is the core of their objection.
4
Analyze the author's objection
Razi questions the analogy with knowledge, stating it is not valid. He also dismisses the secondary argument about essential attributes applying equally.
This shows that the Mu'tazilite argument is not decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : البارى تعالى ليس مريدا لذاته وهو قول أبى على وأبى هاشم والخلاف فيه مع التجار[؟] لنا ما تقدم في مسألة العلم ، واحتج أبو على وأبو هاشم على أنه تعالى ليس مريدا لذاته بأنه لو كان كذلك لكان مريدا لجميع المرادات ، كما أنه لما كان عالما لذاته كان عالما بكل المعلومات ، لكن ذلك محال ، لأن زيدا إذا أراد موت رجل وعمرو أراد حياته فلو كان الله تعالى مريدا لجميع المرادات للزم أن يكون مريدا لموته وحياته معا وهو محال ، ولقائل أن يقول : لم قلت لو كان مريدا لذاته لكان مريدا لكل المرادات، والقياس على العلم لا يسمن ولا يغني من جوع . وقولهم : لما كانت المريدية صفة ذاتية لم يكن تعلقها ببعض المرادات أولى من تعلقها بالباقي فقد عرفت ضعفه
Question: The Exalted Creator is not willing by His essence, and this is the statement of Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, and the dispute with regard to it is with al-Tajjar[?]. For us is what preceded in the question of knowledge. And Abu Ali and Abu Hashim argued that He, exalted, is not willing by His essence, because if He were so, He would be willing all objects of will, just as since He was knowing by His essence, He was knowing all known things. But that is impossible, because if Zaid wills the death of a man and Amr wills his life, then if God, exalted, were willing all objects of will, it would necessitate that He be willing his death and his life together, which is impossible. And it may be said: Why did you say that if He were willing by His essence He would be willing all objects of will? And the analogy with knowledge is of no avail. And their statement: since willness is an essential attribute, its relation to some objects of will would not be more appropriate than its relation to the rest—you have already known its weakness.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a question (mas'ala) addressing whether God is willing (mureed) by His essence or by a distinct attribute. The author first states the position of the Mu'tazilites Abu Ali and Abu Hashim that God is not willing by His essence, meaning will is a superadded attribute. The author then presents his own proof based on a previous discussion on knowledge. Next, he recounts the Mu'tazilite argument: if God were willing by essence, He would have to will all objects of will, analogous to knowledge. This leads to a contradiction because it would require willing opposites (e.g., life and death) simultaneously. The author then introduces an objection to this analogy: one can argue that the analogy with knowledge is weak. Finally, he mentions another Mu'tazilite claim that if willness were an essential attribute, it would apply equally to all objects, but the author says this has already been shown to be weak.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that if an attribute is essential, it must apply to all objects equally.
Correction: The passage argues that this assumption is weak; essential attributes do not necessarily entail universal application.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of Abu Ali and Abu Hashim against God being willing by His essence?
A) It would entail God willing opposites simultaneously.
B) It would make will an attribute of essence.
C) It would require infinite regress.
D) It contradicts revelation.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument against essential will is based on an analogy with knowledge, but Razi questions the validity of that analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will, do you subconsciously model it after human will—changing, conditional, limited? What does this lesson force you to reconsider about how you imagine God's will?
?
Have you ever made a faulty analogy in your own understanding of God—applying something true of one attribute to another without warrant? How can Razi's critique of the Mu'tazilite analogy help you avoid such errors in your own theology?
?
This passage shows that will and knowledge are not identical in their scope. Is there an area of your life where you assume that because God knows something, He must will it in the same way? How does that assumption affect your view of divine decree and your own responsibility?

God's Will is Not Created
Razi proves that God's will cannot be created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that God's will must be eternal and cannot be a created attribute.

Core Idea
Every created thing requires a will; if will were created, infinite regress.

This lesson tackles a critical dispute in Islamic theology: is God's will eternal or created? The Mu'tazila and Karramiyya held that God's will is created, but Razi presents a concise argument showing why this leads to an impossible infinite regress. Understanding this argument clarifies the nature of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing views
Read the passage and note the positions of the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya regarding God's will.
The Mu'tazila say will is created; the Karramiyya say God creates a will in His essence.
2
Understand the logical argument
Follow Razi's reasoning: every origination needs a will, so if will itself were originated, it would need another will, leading to an infinite chain.
This is a classic argument against infinite regress (tasalsul).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Why is infinite regress impossible? Think about the impossibility of an endless chain of causes.
The argument shows that God's will must be eternal, not created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا يجوز أن يكون الله تعالى مريدا بإرادة حادثة خلافا للمعتزلة والكرامية فهو تعالى مريد بإرادة محدثة لا في محل وأما عند الكرامية فهو مريد بإرادة يخلقها في ذاته لنا أن الإحداث الذي لا يصح إلا بالإرادة على ما تقدم ، فلو كانت الإرادة حادثة لافتقرت إلى إرادة أخرى ولزم التسلسل .
Question: It is not permissible that God, exalted, be willing by a created will, contrary to the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya. So He, exalted, is willing by an eternal will not in a locus. As for according to the Karramiyya, He is willing by a will that He creates in His essence. For us: that origination which is not valid except through will, as has preceded. So if the will were created, it would need another will, and an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a question (mas'ala) asserting that God's will cannot be created (haditha), opposing the Mu'tazila who claim will is created and the Karramiyya who claim God creates a will in His essence. The author clarifies that God is willing by an eternal will not in a locus (i.e., not inhering in a subject). The proof is simple: every originated thing (ihdath) requires a will. If the will itself were originated, it would require another will, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, God's will must be eternal.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation can occur without a pre-existing will.
Correction: Every originated thing requires a will; thus will itself cannot be originated.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that God's will cannot be created?
A) Because that would make God subject to change.
B) Because creation requires a will, leading to infinite regress.
C) Because the Quran says so.
D) Because will is an attribute of perfection.

Key Takeaway
God's will must be eternal because if it were created, it would require another will, leading to an impossible infinite chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is eternal and not created, then every event in your life, both good and bad, is under His direct control. Are you living as if you truly believe that, or do you harbor resentment against circumstances as if they happen by chance?
?
The proof against created will shows that nothing can originate without a will. Reflect on your own actions: Do you acknowledge that even your own choices are only possible because God wills them? How would your daily decisions change if you consciously remembered that?
?
This lesson shows that infinite regress is impossible. Your life is not an infinite chain of causes; it ultimately traces back to God's eternal will. Have you traced the deepest desires of your heart back to their source? What would it mean for you to align your will with His eternal will?

Three Points of Dispute and Consensus Proof
Razi outlines the three points of dispute and gives a consensus proof for eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three points of disagreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila regarding God's speech, and the first proof for its eternity based on consensus.

Core Idea
Consensus among those who affirm God's eternal speech proves its eternity.

Razi begins by identifying the three areas of contention: the essence of speech, its ascription to God, and its eternity. He then presents a proof from consensus, arguing that all who affirm God's description by this speech consider it eternal, so to claim otherwise would break consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning until the end of the first proof. Note the three points of dispute.
The first two points are assumed already covered; focus on the third.
2
Identify the parties
The passage contrasts two groups: those who affirm God's description by this speech and those who deny. Recognize that the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya belong to the latter group regarding this specific speech.
3
Analyze the consensus proof
Understand the logical structure: (1) those who affirm say it is eternal, (2) Mu'tazila and Karramiyya do not affirm this speech, (3) saying it is temporal would be a third opinion, breaking consensus, which is false.
This proof relies on the definition of 'this speech' as the eternal attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : كلام الله تعالى قديم خلافا للمعتزلة والكرامية

واعلم أن الجمهور منا يعتقدون أن المعتزلة يوافقوننا في كونه تعالى متكلما ويخالفوننا في قدم الكلام ، فأما نحن قد بينا أن الذي يقول به المعتزلة فنحن نقول به من حيث المعنى والذي نقول به فهم لا يقولون به البتة، فإذا حاولنا مكالمة المعتزلة وجب علينا أن نحقق ماهية الكلام ثم نقيم الدلالة على أن الله تعالى موصوف بها ثم نقيم الدلالة على قدمها فإنهم يخالفوننا في المواضع الثلاثة فنقول .

أما المقامان الأولان وهما المقامان الصعبان مع المعتزلة فقد تقدم القول فيهما

وأما الثالث فالدليل عليه من وجهين .

الأول : أن الفريقان قائلان قائل اعترف بكون الله تعالى موصوفا بهذا الكلام وقائل أنكر ذلك ، وكل من اعترف به قال إنه قديم، لأن المعتزلة والكرامية لم يعترفوا بكون الله تعالى موصوفا بهذا الكلام وإنما قالوا بحدوث الكلام الذي يكون حرفا وصوتا ، وإذا ثبت ذلك فلو قلنا بحدوث هذا الكلام كان ذلك قولا ثالثا وهو خرق الإجماع وهو باطل .

الثاني : وهو أن هذا الكلام لو كان محدثا لكان إما أن يحدث في ذات الله تعالى فيكون محلا للحوادث وهو محال أو لا يحدث فيه وهو محال ، لأن كون الله تعالى متكلما قد دللنا على أنه من صفاته وصفة الشيء يستحيل أن لا تكون حاصلة فيه وإلا لجاز أن يكون الجسم متحركا بحركة قائمة بالغير وذلك محال واحتجوا بأمور . أولها : أن الأمر بلا مأمور عبث وهو غير جائز على الله تعالى .

وثانيها : أنه تعالى في الأزل لو كان متكلما بقوله إنا أرسلنا نوحا وهو إخبار عن الماضي لكان كاذبا

وثالثها : أن الأمة مجمعة على أن كلام الله ناسخ ومنسوخ وسور وآيات وذلك من صفات المحدثات .

والجواب عن الأول : أن عبد الله بن سعيد ذهب إلى أن كلام الله تعالى وإن كان قديما لكنه ما كان في الأزل أمرا ولا نهيا ولا خبرا ثم صار فيما لا يزال كذلك وهذا في غاية البعد، لأنا لما وجدنا في النفس طلبا واقتضاء وبينا الفرق بينه وبين الإرادة أمكننا بعد ذلك أن نشير إلى ماهية معقولة وندعي ثبوتها لله تعالى فأما الكلام الذي يغاير هذه الحروف والأصوات ويغاير ماهية الأمر والنهي والخبر فغير معلوم التصور فإن القول بثبوته لله تعالى في الأزل محض الجهالة

أما جمهور الأصحاب : فقد زعموا أن كلام الله تعالى كان أمرا ونهيا في الأزل ، ثم منهم من يقول المعدوم مأمور على تقدير الوجود وهذا في غاية البعد ، لأن الجماد إذا لم يجز أن يكون مأمورا فالمعدوم أولى ، والذي هو في محض العدم كيف يعقل أن يكون مأمورا ومنهم من قال إنه في الأزل كان أمرا من غير مأمور، ثم لما استمر وبقي صار المكلفون بعد دخولهم في الوجود مأمورين بذلك الأمر وضربوا له مثالا وهو أن الإنسان إذا أخبره النبي الصادق بأن الله تعالى سيرزقه ولدا ولكنه مات قبل ولادته فإنه ربما قال لبعض الناس إذا أدركت ولدي بالغا فقل له أباك يأمرك بتحصيل العلم فهنا قد وجد الأمر والمأمور معدوم ، حتى أنه لو بقي ذلك الأمر إلى أوان بلوغ ذلك الصبي لصار مأمورا بذلك الأمر .
Question: The speech of God, exalted, is eternal, contrary to the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya.

Know that the majority of us believe that the Mu'tazila agree with us that He, exalted, is speaking but disagree with us on the eternity of speech. As for us, we have shown that what the Mu'tazila affirm, we affirm it with respect to meaning, and what we affirm, they do not affirm at all. So if we attempt to debate the Mu'tazila, we must verify the essence of speech, then establish the proof that God, exalted, is described by it, then establish the proof of its eternity, for they disagree with us in the three points. We say:

As for the first two points, which are the two difficult points with the Mu'tazila, the discussion has already preceded on them.

As for the third, the proof for it is from two aspects:

First: that the parties are two: one who affirms that God, exalted, is described by this speech, and one who denies that. And everyone who affirmed it said it is eternal, because the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya did not affirm that God, exalted, is described by this speech, but only said that the speech which is composed of letters and sounds is temporal. And if that is established, then if we said this speech is temporal, that would be a third opinion, which breaks consensus, and that is false.

Second: that this speech, if it were temporal, then either it would come to be in the essence of God, exalted, so He would be a locus for temporal events, which is impossible, or it would not come to be in Him, which is impossible, because that God, exalted, is speaking we have already proved that it is one of His attributes, and it is impossible for a thing's attribute not to be present in it, otherwise it would be permissible for a body to be moving by a movement subsisting in another, and that is impossible. And they argue with things: First: that a command without a commanded person is absurd, and that is not permissible for God, exalted. Second: that He, exalted, in pre-eternity, if He were speaking by His saying 'Indeed, We sent Noah,' which is an informing about the past, He would be lying. Third: that the community is agreed that the speech of God is abrogating and abrogated, and surahs and verses, and that is among the characteristics of temporal things.

The answer to the first: that Abdullah bin Saeed went to the view that the speech of God, exalted, though eternal, was not in pre-eternity a command nor a prohibition nor a report, then it became so in what will be, and this is extremely far-fetched, because when we find in the soul a demand and requirement and we have shown the difference between it and will, we can then point to an intelligible essence and claim its existence for God, exalted. As for the speech that differs from these letters and sounds and differs from the essence of command, prohibition, and report, its conception is unknown, and the statement asserting its existence for God, exalted, in pre-eternity is pure ignorance.

As for the majority of the companions: they held that the speech of God, exalted, was command and prohibition in pre-eternity. Then among them are those who say that the non-existent is commanded on the assumption of existence, and this is extremely far-fetched, because if an inanimate object cannot be commanded, then the non-existent is even more so. How can it be understood that what is in pure non-existence be commanded? And among them are those who say that it was in pre-eternity a command without a commanded person, then when it continued and remained, the obligated persons after their coming into existence become commanded by that command. And they gave an example for this: when a truthful prophet informs a man that God, exalted, will give him a son, but he dies before his birth, he might say to some people: 'If you catch my son as an adult, tell him: Your father commands you to acquire knowledge.' So here the command exists while the commanded person is non-existent, until if that command remains until the time of that boy's reaching adulthood, he would become commanded by that command.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive question (mas'ala) on the eternity of God's speech (kalam). The author begins by noting the agreement on God being speaking (mutakallim) but disagreement on temporality. He identifies three points of dispute: the essence of speech, the ascription to God, and its eternity. The first two were covered earlier. For the third, he gives two proofs. The first proof is by consensus: all who affirm that God is described by this speech say it is eternal; to say it is temporal would break consensus. The second proof: if speech were temporal, it would either inhere in God (making Him a locus of events) or not inhere (impossible for an attribute). He then presents three Mu'tazilite objections: (1) command without a commanded person is absurd, (2) if eternal speech included past-tense reports, it would be false, (3) the Quran's abrogation and verses indicate temporality. The author answers the first objection by discussing Abdullah bin Saeed's view (speech was not command/prohibition/report in pre-eternity) but finds it unsatisfactory. Then he presents the Ash'ari view: speech is eternal and includes commands and prohibitions even with non-existent commanded persons, using the example of a man commanding his future son through a proxy.

Key Terms
كلام
قديم
محدث
إجماع
موصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila deny that God speaks at all.
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm that God speaks, but they consider His speech to be created and temporal.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first proof, why would saying God's speech is temporal be invalid?
A) Because the Mu'tazila agree it is eternal.
B) Because it would break the consensus of those who affirm that God is described by this speech.
C) Because the Quran is miraculous.
D) Because God's speech cannot be temporal.

Key Takeaway
The consensus among those who affirm that God is described by eternal speech supports its eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly perceive it as the eternal, uncreated speech of God, or do you unconsciously treat it as a temporal human book? How would your heart and actions change if you fully internalized its eternity?
?
The consensus of the scholars who affirmed God's speech as eternal broke the third opinion. In your own life, when the overwhelming majority of learned scholars agree on a matter, do you still seek out the fringe opinion because it is easier or more comfortable? Which is greater in you: the desire for truth or the desire for ease?
?
Razi emphasizes that the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya do not even affirm God's eternal speech, only temporal sounds. Have you ever reduced God's speech to a mere product of time, such as by treating the Quran as culturally conditioned? If so, what repentance is demanded of you today?
