The Three Interpretations of Takwin
Razi critiques the Hanafite claim by demanding a definition of takwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi demands a clear definition of takwin and how three possible interpretations each lead to logical problems.

Core Idea
Takwin must be defined before its eternity can be assessed.

Razi begins by challenging the Hanafite claim that takwin is an eternal attribute. He insists that we first clarify what takwin actually means. Without a clear definition, the debate is meaningless. He then presents three possible interpretations and shows that each one fails to support the claim of eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Hanafite claim
Read the opening line: some Hanafi jurists claimed takwin is an eternal attribute and the brought-into-existence is originated.
Note that the opponents assert both eternity of the attribute and temporality of its effect.
2
Identify the three possible meanings
Razi lists three possibilities: (1) the effectiveness of power itself, (2) an effective attribute identical to power, (3) something else. Follow his reasoning for each.
The first leads to origination of takwin, the second reduces to power, the third requires explanation.
3
Understand the logic
Ask yourself: why does the first interpretation make takwin originated? Because relations exist only when relata exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : زعم بعض فقهاء الحنفية أن التكوين صفة أزلية لله تعالى وأن المكون محدث
فنقول لهم : القول بأن التكوين قديم ومحدث يستدعى تصور ماهية التكوين ، فإن كان المراد منه نفس مؤثرية القدرة في المقدور فهى صفة نسبية والنسب لا يوجد إلا عند وجود المنتسبين فيلزم من حدوث المكون حدوث التكوين ، وإن عنيتم صفة مؤثرة في وجود الأثر فهى عين القدرة وإن عنيتم به أمرا اعتمادنا فبينوه .
قالوا : القدرة صفة مؤثرة في صحة وجود المقدور والتكوين مؤثر فى نفس وجود المقدور .
قلنا القدرة لا تأثير لها فى كون المقدور فى نفسه جائز الوجود . لأن ذلك له لذاته وما بالذات لا يكون بالغير فلم يبق إلا أن يكون تأثيرها في وجود المقدور تأثيرها على سبيل الصحة لا على سبيل الوجوب ، فلو أثبتنا صفة أخرى لله تعالى مؤثرة في وجود المقدور لمكان تأثيرها في المقدور ، وإن كان على سبيل الصحة كان عين القدرة فيلزم اجتماع المثلين ويلزم اجتماع صفتين مستقلتين بالتأثير على المقدور الواحد وهو محال ، وإن كان على سبيل الوجوب لزم استحالة أن لا يوجد ذلك المقدور من الله تعالى فيكون الله تعالى موجبا بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار وهو باطل بالاتفاق ، وأيضاً فالقدرة تنافى هذه الصفة لأن الموجب بالذات لا يكون قادرا مختارا (1)
أقول : إنما أخذ التكوين من قوله تعالى {إنما أمرنا إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون} فجعل قوله كن مقدما على التكوين وهو المسمى بالأمر ، والكلمة والتكوين والاختراع والإيجاد والخلق ألفاظ تشترك في معنى وتتباين بمعانى والمشترك فيه كون الشىء موجودا من العدم مالم يكن موجودا وهى أخص من القدرة ، لأن القدرة متساوية النسبة إلى جميع المقدورات وهي قائمة لما يدخل منها فى الوجود وليست صفة سلبية تتعقل مع المنتسبين بل هى صفة تقتضى بعد حصول الأثر تلك النسبة . وأما ادعاء أنهم قالوا القدرة مؤثرة فى صحة وجود المقدور فليس بصحيح ، إنما الصحيح أن القدرة متعلقة بصحة وجود المقدور والتكوين متعلق بوجود المقدور ومؤثر فيه ونسبته إلى الفعل الحادث كنسبة الإرادة إلى المراد والعلم والقدرة لا يقتضيان كون المقدور والمعلوم موجودين بهما والتكوين يقتضيه وقالوا بأزليته لقولهم بامتناع قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى
وقوله : وإن كانت تلك الصفة مؤثرة على سبيل الوجوب كان الله تعالى موجبا ليس بجيد لأن ذلك الوجوب يكون لاحقا لا سابقا يعنى إذا أراد الله تعالى خلق شيء من مقدوراته كان حصول ذلك الشيء واجبا فيه ، لا بمعنى أنه كان واجبا أن يخلقه
قوله: إن عنيتم به صفة مؤثرة في وجود الأثر فهو عين القدرة وجوابه أن القدرة لو كانت مؤثرة لكان جميع المقدورات أثرا لها فيكون موجودا ولا يلزم من إثبات التكوين جمع المثلين ، لأن متعلق القدرة غير متعلق التكوين فهذا ما يمكن أن يقال من جانبهم ، والحق أن القدرة والإرادة مجموعين هما اللذان يتعلقان بوجود الأثر ولا حاجة معهما إلى إثبات صفة أخرى .
Issue: Some Hanafi jurists claimed that takwin (bringing into existence) is an eternal attribute of God, and that the brought-into-existence (al-mukawwan) is originated. We say to them: The statement that takwin is eternal and originated requires conceptualizing the essence of takwin. If what is meant is the very effectivity of power (qudra) with respect to the object of power, then it is a relative attribute, and relations do not exist except when the two relata exist, so the origination of the originated entails the origination of takwin. If you intend an attribute effective in the existence of the effect, then it is identical to power. If you intend something else, then explain it. They said: Power is an attribute effective in the possibility of the existence of the object of power, while takwin is effective in the very existence of the object of power. We said: Power has no effect on the object of power being possible in itself, because that belongs to it by itself, and what is by itself cannot be by another. So it remains that its effect on the existence of the object of power is by way of possibility, not by way of necessity. If we posit another attribute of God effective in the existence of the object of power, then if its effect is by way of possibility, it would be identical to power, leading to the conjunction of two similars and the conjunction of two independent attributes effective on one object of power, which is impossible. If its effect is by way of necessity, then it becomes impossible that that object of power not exist from God, so God would be necessary by essence, not a free agent, which is invalid by consensus. Also, power contradicts this attribute because the necessary by essence cannot be powerful and free. (1) I say: Takwin is taken from His statement {Only Our saying, when We will a thing, is to say to it 'Be,' and it is} so He made the saying 'Be' prior to takwin, and it is called the command (amr). The words takwin, ikhtira' (invention), ijad (bringing into existence), and khalq (creation) share a meaning and differ in meanings, and the common element is the thing's becoming existent from non-existence after it was not existent, and they are more specific than power, because power is equally related to all objects of power and is established for what enters into existence among them. It is not a negative attribute that is understood with the two relata, but it is an attribute that necessitates that relation after the effect's occurrence. As for the claim that they said power is effective in the possibility of the existence of the object of power, this is not correct. The correct view is that power is related to the possibility of the existence of the object of power, while takwin is related to the existence of the object of power and is effective in it, and its relation to the originated act is like the relation of will (irada) to the object of will. Knowledge and power do not necessitate that the object of power and object of knowledge exist through them, while takwin necessitates it. They said it is eternal because of their statement that originated things cannot subsist in His essence. His statement: 'If that attribute were effective by way of necessity, then God would be necessary' is not good, because that necessity is consequent, not antecedent—meaning when God wills to create something of His objects of power, the occurrence of that thing becomes necessary in it, not that it was necessary for Him to create it. His statement: 'If you intend an attribute effective in the existence of the effect, it is identical to power'—the response to that is that if power were effective, then all objects of power would be its effects and thus existent, and positing takwin does not entail conjunction of two similars, because the object of power is different from the object of takwin. This is what can be said from their side. The truth is that power and will together are what relate to the existence of the effect, and there is no need to posit another attribute.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a detailed debate about the attribute of takwin (bringing into existence) as claimed by some Hanafi jurists. The author begins by stating the claim and then demands clarification of what takwin means. Three possibilities are explored: (1) if it means the effectiveness of power itself, then it is relative and arises only when the effect exists, making it originated; (2) if it means an effective attribute for producing the effect, then it is identical to power; (3) if it means something else, they must explain. The opponents argue that power only makes the object possible, while takwin makes it actual. The author refutes this by noting that power's effect is only on possibility, not actuality. Adding another attribute for actuality would either duplicate power (if merely making possible) or impose necessity on God (if making actual by necessity), both unacceptable. The marginal note further clarifies that takwin is more specific than power and is related to existence, while power is related to possibility. The note also defends against charges of compulsion by distinguishing antecedent and consequent necessity. Ultimately, the author concludes that power and will together are sufficient, and no extra attribute is needed.

Key Terms

صفة أزلية
مكون
قدرة
صفة نسبية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Takwin can be considered eternal without specifying what it is.
Correction: Razi shows that without a clear definition, the claim is meaningless; every possible meaning has problems.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what happens if takwin is interpreted as the effectiveness of power with respect to the object of power?
A) It becomes identical to power.
B) It becomes a relative attribute and thus originated.
C) It becomes eternal.
D) It becomes impossible.

Key Takeaway
Takwin must be defined before its eternity can be assessed; all three possible definitions lead to problems.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have any beliefs about God that you have never stopped to define clearly? What would it look like for you to follow Razi's method and demand a conceptualization before affirming such a belief?
?
When you say 'God creates,' what exactly do you understand by 'create'? Is it an act of power, a command, or something else? If you cannot define it, how can you claim to know what you mean?
?
This passage shows that even respected scholars can make claims without conceptual precision. Are there theological positions you hold that might crumble under the same kind of logical scrutiny? Will you examine one such position this week?

Takwin, Power, and the Necessity of Divine Agency
Razi refutes the Hanafite distinction between power and takwin and defends free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanafite argument distinguishing power and takwin, Razi's refutation, and the final solution that power and will together suffice.

Core Idea
Positing takwin as a distinct attribute either duplicates power or imposes necessity on God.

The debate deepens as the Hanafites respond: power only makes action possible, while takwin makes it actual. Razi refutes this by showing that an attribute for actuality either duplicates power (if it only makes possible) or forces God to act necessarily (if it makes actual by necessity). The passage concludes with a careful distinction between antecedent and consequent necessity and affirms that power and will are sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hanafite distinction
Read the opponents' claim: power is for possibility, takwin is for actual existence. Notice how they separate the two roles.
2
Follow Razi's refutation
Razi argues that power has no effect on possibility (that is intrinsic), so its effect on existence is only by possibility. Adding another attribute either duplicates power (if by possibility) or makes God a necessary agent (if by necessity).
The key is that if takwin were effective by necessity, God would be 'muijib bi al-dhat' (necessary by essence), which contradicts free choice.
3
Analyze the marginal note
The note clarifies that takwin is more specific than power and is related to actual existence. It also defends against the charge of necessitarianism by distinguishing antecedent (preceding) necessity from consequent (resulting) necessity.
The note concludes that power and will together are sufficient for bringing about existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : زعم بعض فقهاء الحنفية أن التكوين صفة أزلية لله تعالى وأن المكون محدث
فنقول لهم : القول بأن التكوين قديم ومحدث يستدعى تصور ماهية التكوين ، فإن كان المراد منه نفس مؤثرية القدرة في المقدور فهى صفة نسبية والنسب لا يوجد إلا عند وجود المنتسبين فيلزم من حدوث المكون حدوث التكوين ، وإن عنيتم صفة مؤثرة في وجود الأثر فهى عين القدرة وإن عنيتم به أمرا اعتمادنا فبينوه .
قالوا : القدرة صفة مؤثرة في صحة وجود المقدور والتكوين مؤثر فى نفس وجود المقدور .
قلنا القدرة لا تأثير لها فى كون المقدور فى نفسه جائز الوجود . لأن ذلك له لذاته وما بالذات لا يكون بالغير فلم يبق إلا أن يكون تأثيرها في وجود المقدور تأثيرها على سبيل الصحة لا على سبيل الوجوب ، فلو أثبتنا صفة أخرى لله تعالى مؤثرة في وجود المقدور لمكان تأثيرها في المقدور ، وإن كان على سبيل الصحة كان عين القدرة فيلزم اجتماع المثلين ويلزم اجتماع صفتين مستقلتين بالتأثير على المقدور الواحد وهو محال ، وإن كان على سبيل الوجوب لزم استحالة أن لا يوجد ذلك المقدور من الله تعالى فيكون الله تعالى موجبا بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار وهو باطل بالاتفاق ، وأيضاً فالقدرة تنافى هذه الصفة لأن الموجب بالذات لا يكون قادرا مختارا (1)
أقول : إنما أخذ التكوين من قوله تعالى {إنما أمرنا إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون} فجعل قوله كن مقدما على التكوين وهو المسمى بالأمر ، والكلمة والتكوين والاختراع والإيجاد والخلق ألفاظ تشترك في معنى وتتباين بمعانى والمشترك فيه كون الشىء موجودا من العدم مالم يكن موجودا وهى أخص من القدرة ، لأن القدرة متساوية النسبة إلى جميع المقدورات وهي قائمة لما يدخل منها فى الوجود وليست صفة سلبية تتعقل مع المنتسبين بل هى صفة تقتضى بعد حصول الأثر تلك النسبة . وأما ادعاء أنهم قالوا القدرة مؤثرة فى صحة وجود المقدور فليس بصحيح ، إنما الصحيح أن القدرة متعلقة بصحة وجود المقدور والتكوين متعلق بوجود المقدور ومؤثر فيه ونسبته إلى الفعل الحادث كنسبة الإرادة إلى المراد والعلم والقدرة لا يقتضيان كون المقدور والمعلوم موجودين بهما والتكوين يقتضيه وقالوا بأزليته لقولهم بامتناع قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى
وقوله : وإن كانت تلك الصفة مؤثرة على سبيل الوجوب كان الله تعالى موجبا ليس بجيد لأن ذلك الوجوب يكون لاحقا لا سابقا يعنى إذا أراد الله تعالى خلق شيء من مقدوراته كان حصول ذلك الشيء واجبا فيه ، لا بمعنى أنه كان واجبا أن يخلقه
قوله: إن عنيتم به صفة مؤثرة في وجود الأثر فهو عين القدرة وجوابه أن القدرة لو كانت مؤثرة لكان جميع المقدورات أثرا لها فيكون موجودا ولا يلزم من إثبات التكوين جمع المثلين ، لأن متعلق القدرة غير متعلق التكوين فهذا ما يمكن أن يقال من جانبهم ، والحق أن القدرة والإرادة مجموعين هما اللذان يتعلقان بوجود الأثر ولا حاجة معهما إلى إثبات صفة أخرى .
Issue: Some Hanafi jurists claimed that takwin (bringing into existence) is an eternal attribute of God, and that the brought-into-existence (al-mukawwan) is originated. We say to them: The statement that takwin is eternal and originated requires conceptualizing the essence of takwin. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that everything happens by God's will, but do you ever catch yourself thinking of God's decrees as cold, mechanical necessities rather than personal acts of love and wisdom? Commit today to thanking God for even difficult events as coming from His deliberate, personal choice for your good.
?
If God's power and will together are sufficient for creation, why do you sometimes act as if your own abilities are autonomous from His will? ?

Or do you sometimes expect God to act automatically without your participation? Which error do you lean toward, and what concrete step will you take today to trust in power and will together, without adding or subtracting?

Survey of Additional Divine Attributes
Razi enumerates extra attributes proposed by various theologians and concludes with suspension of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diversity of opinions among theologians regarding extra divine attributes and the author's conclusion that suspension of judgment is the fair stance.

Core Idea
When evidence is lacking for both sides, the honest position is to suspend judgment.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi surveys a range of additional divine attributes proposed by various schools and figures beyond the commonly accepted seven or eight. He presents their views without bias and then delivers a fair judgment: there is no conclusive evidence for or against these attributes, so the rational attitude is to refrain from affirming or denying them. This lesson explores that survey and the principle of suspension.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then read the translation. Notice the variety of opinions and the author's impartial listing.
The passage lists many theologians; don't worry about memorizing all names now.
2
Identify the different theologians and their attributes
Go through the passage and note each theologian or group and the specific attribute(s) they affirm beyond the usual seven or eight.
3
Reflect on the author's conclusion
After the list, al-Razi states that there is no evidence for or against these attributes, so suspension (tawaqquf) is obligatory. Consider why this is a fair position and how it differs from outright rejection.
Suspension is not denial; it is acknowledging lack of conclusive evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الظاهريون من المتكلمين زعموا أنه لا صفة لله تعالى وراء السبع أو الثاني وأثبت أبو الحسن الأشعرى اليد صفة وراء القدرة والوجه صفة وراء الوجود وأثبت الاستواء صفة أخرى ، وأثبت أبو إسحاق الاسفرائينى صفة توجب الاستغناء عن المكان ، وأثبت القاضي صفات ثلاثة أخرى وهى إدراك الشم والذوق واللمس ، وأثبت عبد الله بن سعيد القدم صفة وراء البقاء، وأثبت مثبتو الحال العالمية أمرا وراء العلم وكذا القول في سائر الصفات ، وأثبت أبو سهل الصعلوكى لله تعالى بحسب كل معلوم وبحسب كل مقدور قدرة وأثبت عبد الله بن سعيد الرحمة والكرم والرضا والسخط صفات وراء الإرادة . والانصاف أنه لا دلالة على ثبوت هذه الصفات ولا على نفيها فيجب التوقف .
واحتج من حصر الصفات في السبع أو الثماني بأنا كلفنا بكمال المعرفة وكمال المعرفة إنما يحصل بمعرفة جميع الصفات ومعرفة جميع الصفات لا يتأتى إلا بطريق ولا طريق إلا الاستدلال بالأفعال والتنزيه عن النقائص ، وهذان الطريقان لا يدلان إلا على هذه الصفات .
والجواب : لم قلت إنا أمرنا بكمال المعرفة، ولم لا يجوز أن يقال إنا ما أمرنا بأن نعرف من صفات
Issue: The Zahiri theologians claimed that there is no attribute of God beyond the seven or eight. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari affirmed the hand (yad) as an attribute beyond power, and the face (wajh) as an attribute beyond existence, and affirmed istiwa' as another attribute. Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini affirmed an attribute that necessitates independence from place. Al-Qadi affirmed three other attributes: perception of smell, taste, and touch. Abd Allah ibn Sa'id affirmed pre-eternity (qidam) as an attribute beyond everlastingness (baqa). The affirmers of the state (hal) affirmed 'worldliness' as something beyond knowledge, and likewise for other attributes. Abu Sahl al-Su'luki affirmed for God, according to every object of knowledge and every object of power, a power. Abd Allah ibn Sa'id affirmed mercy, generosity, approval, and displeasure as attributes beyond will. The fair judgment is that there is no evidence for the affirmation of these attributes nor for their denial, so suspension is obligatory. One who confines attributes to seven or eight argued: We are commanded to have comprehensive knowledge, and comprehensive knowledge is only obtained by knowing all the attributes, and knowing all attributes is only possible by a way, and there is no way except inference from acts and transcendence above imperfections, and these two ways indicate only these attributes. The answer: Why did you say we are commanded to have comprehensive knowledge? Why may it not be said that we are not commanded to know among the attributes...
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a survey and critical discussion of extra divine attributes beyond the commonly accepted seven or eight. The author begins by stating the Zahiri position that there are no attributes beyond seven or eight. He then lists numerous additional attributes proposed by various theologians: al-Ash'ari's hand, face, and istiwa'; al-Isfarayini's attribute of independence from place; al-Qadi's three perceptions; Ibn Sa'id's pre-eternity; affirmers of state's 'worldliness'; al-Su'luki's multiple powers; and Ibn Sa'id's mercy, generosity, approval, and displeasure. The author delivers a fair judgment: there is no evidence for or against these, so we must suspend judgment. He then presents the proof of those who limit attributes to seven or eight: we are commanded to attain comprehensive knowledge of God, and that is only possible by knowing all attributes, which in turn can only be known through inference from God's acts and transcendence, which only yield those limited attributes. The author's rebuttal begins with a question: why assume we are commanded to have comprehensive knowledge? It may be that we are not commanded to know all attributes. The passage cuts off before the full response, but the argument is clear that the premise of commanding comprehensive knowledge is challenged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Suspension of judgment means the author denies these attributes.
Correction: The author explicitly says there is no evidence for or against, so suspension is not denial but agnosticism.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion about the additional attributes proposed by various theologians?
A) They are all proven by revelation.
B) They are all false and should be rejected.
C) There is no evidence for or against them, so we must suspend judgment.
D) Only the seven attributes are valid.

Key Takeaway
When rational and revelatory evidence is inconclusive, the prudent course is to suspend judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When are you most tempted to assert a belief without clear evidence? Think of a specific area in your faith or daily life. How would applying the principle of suspension change your inner posture?
?
Is there a theological question—perhaps about God's attributes or actions—that you have felt pressured to have a definitive answer for? Can you honestly admit to yourself that the evidence is inconclusive and step into that uncertainty with humility?
?
Imagine you are in a discussion where someone insists on a position about God that the evidence neither confirms nor denies. How will you resist both the urge to agree and the urge to refute, and instead practice tawaqquf? What will that cost you?

The Argument for Limiting Attributes and Its Critique
Razi presents the proof for limiting attributes and begins his rebuttal by questioning the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that limits divine attributes to seven or eight and the beginning of Imam al-Razi's critique that challenges the premise of being commanded to have comprehensive knowledge.

Core Idea
The proof for limiting attributes rests on an unproven premise that we are commanded to know all attributes.

In this continuation, al-Razi presents the argument of those who restrict God's attributes to seven or eight. Their reasoning is that we are obligated to have comprehensive knowledge of God, which can only be achieved by knowing all attributes, and the only ways to know them—inference from acts and transcendence—yield only those limited attributes. Al-Razi then begins to dismantle this argument by questioning the premise: why assume we are commanded to have comprehensive knowledge? Perhaps we are not. This lesson examines that argument and the initial response.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof presented by proponents of limiting attributes
Read the passage and translation carefully. Identify the logical steps: command to comprehensive knowledge → need to know all attributes → only two ways to know → these ways yield only 7/8 attributes.
2
Examine al-Razi's rebuttal
Al-Razi directly challenges the first premise: 'Why did you say we are commanded to have comprehensive knowledge?' Consider why this is a powerful critique. If the premise is false, the entire argument collapses.
The passage cuts off, but the direction is clear: the obligation to know all attributes is not established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الظاهريون من المتكلمين زعموا أنه لا صفة لله تعالى وراء السبع أو الثاني وأثبت أبو الحسن الأشعرى اليد صفة وراء القدرة والوجه صفة وراء الوجود وأثبت الاستواء صفة أخرى ، وأثبت أبو إسحاق الاسفرائينى صفة توجب الاستغناء عن المكان ، وأثبت القاضي صفات ثلاثة أخرى وهى إدراك الشم والذوق واللمس ، وأثبت عبد الله بن سعيد القدم صفة وراء البقاء، وأثبت مثبتو الحال العالمية أمرا وراء العلم وكذا القول في سائر الصفات ، وأثبت أبو سهل الصعلوكى لله تعالى بحسب كل معلوم وبحسب كل مقدور قدرة وأثبت عبد الله بن سعيد الرحمة والكرم والرضا والسخط صفات وراء الإرادة . والانصاف أنه لا دلالة على ثبوت هذه الصفات ولا على نفيها فيجب التوقف .
واحتج من حصر الصفات في السبع أو الثماني بأنا كلفنا بكمال المعرفة وكمال المعرفة إنما يحصل بمعرفة جميع الصفات ومعرفة جميع الصفات لا يتأتى إلا بطريق ولا طريق إلا الاستدلال بالأفعال والتنزيه عن النقائص ، وهذان الطريقان لا يدلان إلا على هذه الصفات .
والجواب : لم قلت إنا أمرنا بكمال المعرفة، ولم لا يجوز أن يقال إنا ما أمرنا بأن نعرف من صفات
Issue: The Zahiri theologians claimed that there is no attribute of God beyond the seven or eight. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari affirmed the hand (yad) as an attribute beyond power, and the face (wajh) as an attribute beyond existence, and affirmed istiwa' as another attribute. Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini affirmed an attribute that necessitates independence from place. Al-Qadi affirmed three other attributes: perception of smell, taste, and touch. Abd Allah ibn Sa'id affirmed pre-eternity (qidam) as an attribute beyond everlastingness (baqa). The affirmers of the state (hal) affirmed 'worldliness' as something beyond knowledge, and likewise for other attributes. Abu Sahl al-Su'luki affirmed for God, according to every object of knowledge and every object of power, a power. Abd Allah ibn Sa'id affirmed mercy, generosity, approval, and displeasure as attributes beyond will. The fair judgment is that there is no evidence for the affirmation of these attributes nor for their denial, so suspension is obligatory. One who confines attributes to seven or eight argued: We are commanded to have comprehensive knowledge, and comprehensive knowledge is only obtained by knowing all the attributes, and knowing all attributes is only possible by a way, and there is no way except inference from acts and transcendence above imperfections, and these two ways indicate only these attributes. The answer: Why did you say we are commanded to have comprehensive knowledge? Why may it not be said that we are not commanded to know among the attributes...
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a survey and critical discussion of extra divine attributes beyond the commonly accepted seven or eight. The author begins by stating the Zahiri position that there are no attributes beyond seven or eight. He then lists numerous additional attributes proposed by various theologians: al-Ash'ari's hand, face, and istiwa'; al-Isfarayini's attribute of independence from place; al-Qadi's three perceptions; Ibn Sa'id's pre-eternity; affirmers of state's 'worldliness'; al-Su'luki's multiple powers; and Ibn Sa'id's mercy, generosity, approval, and displeasure. The author delivers a fair judgment: there is no evidence for or against these, so we must suspend judgment. He then presents the proof of those who limit attributes to seven or eight: we are commanded to attain comprehensive knowledge of God, and that is only possible by knowing all attributes, which in turn can only be known through inference from God's acts and transcendence, which only yield those limited attributes. The author's rebuttal begins with a question: why assume we are commanded to have comprehensive knowledge? It may be that we are not commanded to know all attributes. The passage cuts off before the full response, but the argument is clear that the premise of commanding comprehensive knowledge is challenged.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi endorses the proof for limiting attributes.
Correction: He presents it only to critique it, starting with challenging the premise.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the proof that limits attributes to seven or eight?
A) There are only seven attributes.
B) Inference from acts is insufficient.
C) We are commanded to have comprehensive knowledge.
D) The attributes are unknowable.

Key Takeaway
A critical approach to theological arguments involves examining their foundational premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever settle for a comfortable, minimal understanding, assuming that is enough for your salvation? How would your worship change if you were convinced that God commanded you to know every possible attribute?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly logical argument can rest on an unproven premise. In your own faith journey, where have you accepted a belief without questioning its foundation? Will you now examine it?
?
If the scholars of the past debated whether we are even required to know all of God's attributes, how often have you assumed you fully understand God? What would it mean to truly embrace that your knowledge of Him is always incomplete and that you must constantly seek more?

Takwin: Bringing into Existence and Its Relation to Divine Power
Defines takwin and distinguishes it from divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic basis for takwin and how it is distinguished from divine power (qudra) as a more specific attribute related to bringing things into existence.

Core Idea
Takwin is the power to bring into existence; power is the possibility of existence.

This lesson introduces the concept of takwin (bringing into existence) as derived from the Quranic verse "Be, and it is." The author explains that while various terms like command, word, and creation share a common meaning of bringing something from non-existence into existence, takwin is more specific than power. Power relates to all possible things equally, but takwin is uniquely tied to actual existence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping later debates on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on takwin's origin
Read from 'أقول: إنما أخذ التكوين' to 'والتكوين يقتضيه.' Notice how the author grounds takwin in the Quran.
2
Identify the common meaning of terms
The author lists several terms (command, word, takwin, etc.) and says they share a meaning of bringing into existence. Identify that commonality.
This shows the conceptual overlap.
3
Distinguish takwin from power
Power is equally related to all possible things, while takwin is specifically related to the existence of a thing. Also, power relates to validity, takwin to actual existence.
This distinction is key to the argument.
4
Reflect on the last sentence
Knowledge and power do not necessitate existence, but takwin does. Why? Because takwin is the attribute that directly brings about existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: إنما أخذ التكوين من قوله تعالى: "إنما أمرنا إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون". فجعل قوله "كن" مقدما على الكون؟، وهو المسمى بالأمر والكلمة والتكوين والاختراع والإيجاد والخلق، ألفاظ تشترك في معنى وتتباين بمعان، والمشترك فيه كون الشيء موجودًا من العدم ما لم يكن موجودًا، وهي أخص من القدرة، لأن القدرة متساوية النسبة إلى جميع المقدورات، وهي قائمة لما يدخل منها في الوجود، وليست صفة سلبية تعقل مع النسبتين؟، بل هي صفة تقتضي بعد حصول الأثر تلك النسبة. وأما ادعاء أنهم قالوا القدرة مؤثرة في صحة وجود المقدور فليس بصحيح، إنما الصحيح أن القدرة متعلقة بصحة وجود المقدور، والتكوين متعلق بوجود المقدور ومؤثر فيه، ونسبته إلى الفعل الحادث كنسبة الإرادة إلى المراد. والعلم والقدرة لا يقتضيان كون المقدور والمعلوم موجودين بهما، والتكوين يقتضيه. وقالوا بأزليته لقولهم بامتناع قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى.

وقوله: "وإن كانت تلك الصفة مؤثرة على سبيل الوجوب كان الله تعالى موجبا ليس بذات" لأن ذلك الوجوب يكون لاحقا لا سابقا، يعني إذا أراد الله تعالى خلق شيء من مقدوراته كان حصول ذلك الشيء واجبا فيه، لا بمعنى أنه كان واجبا أن يخلقه.

قوله: "إن عنيتم به صفة مؤثرة في وجود الأثر فهو عين القدرة" وجوابه أن القدرة لو كانت مؤثرة لكان جميع المقدورات أثرا لها فيكون موجودا، ولا يلزم من إثبات التكوين جمع المثلين، لأن متعلق القدرة غير متعلق التكوين، فهذا ما يمكن أن يقال من جانبهم. والحق أن القدرة والإرادة مجموعين هما اللذان يتعلقان بوجود الأثر، ولا حاجة معهما إلى إثبات صفة أخرى.
I say: Indeed, takwin (bringing into existence) is taken from His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, Our command, when We intend a thing, is only to say to it, "Be," and it is.' So He made His word 'Be' preceding the existence [?], and it is called 'command', 'word', 'takwin', 'invention', 'bringing into being', and 'creation'—terms that share in a meaning and differ in meanings; the commonality is the coming into existence of a thing from non-existence when it was not existing, and it is more specific than power, because power is equally related to all predestined things, and it pertains to what among them enters into existence, and it is not a negative attribute conceived with two relationships [?], but rather it is an attribute that necessitates after the occurrence of the effect that relationship. As for the claim that they said power is effective in the validity of the existence of the predestined, it is not correct; rather the correct thing is that power is related to the validity of existence of the predestined, while takwin is related to the existence of the predestined and is effective in it, and its relation to the originated act is like the relation of will to the desired. And knowledge and power do not necessitate that the predestined and the known exist by them, but takwin necessitates it. And they affirmed its eternality due to their assertion of the impossibility of accidents subsisting in His essence, the Exalted.

And his statement: 'And if that attribute were effective in the mode of necessity, God would be a necessitator, not a being' because that necessity would be subsequent, not prior, meaning if God wills to create something from His predestined things, the occurrence of that thing becomes necessary in it, not in the sense that He was necessarily to create it.

His statement: 'If you mean by it an attribute effective in the existence of the effect, then it is the very power' — and its answer is that if power were effective, then all predestined things would be its effect, and they would exist; and the establishment of takwin does not necessitate the combination of two similars, because the object of power is other than the object of takwin. So this is what can be said from their side. But the truth is that power and will together are the two that relate to the existence of the effect, and there is no need with them to establish another attribute.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (marked by 'I say') that discusses the concept of takwin (bringing into existence) as a possible attribute of God. It begins by grounding takwin in the Quranic verse 'Be, and it is,' noting that various terms like command, word, takwin, invention, etc., share a common meaning of bringing something from non-existence into existence, but are more specific than mere power. Power is equally related to all possible things, while takwin is specifically related to the actual existence of a thing. The author then refutes the claim that power is effective in the validity of existence, asserting that power relates to validity, while takwin relates to and is effective in actual existence.

The passage then addresses a possible objection: if this attribute were a necessitator, God would be a necessary agent by essence, not a free chooser. The response clarifies that the necessity here is subsequent (when God wills something, its existence becomes necessary), not prior (that God must create it).

Another objection: if takwin is an attribute effective in existence, then it is the same as power. The answer argues that if power were effective, all predestined things would exist, which is not the case. Takwin has a distinct object of relation from power, so no duplication occurs. The conclusion is that power and will together suffice to bring about existence, without needing a separate attribute of takwin.

Key Terms
التكوين
القدرة
الأمر
الكلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think takwin and power are identical attributes.
Correction: The passage shows they have different objects: power relates to validity of existence, takwin to actual existence.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between takwin and power according to the passage?
A) They are identical.
B) Power relates to actual existence, takwin to validity.
C) Power relates to validity, takwin to actual existence.
D) Takwin is a type of power.

Key Takeaway
Takwin is the attribute that brings things into existence, distinct from power which only relates to the possibility of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you find yourself relying on the mere possibility of change rather than actively trusting that Allah's takwin—His effective bringing into existence—can transform your situation? What would it look like to live today as if takwin is already at work in that area?
?
The passage teaches that power relates to validity but takwin actualizes. When you look at your own weaknesses and struggles, do you tend to only acknowledge that Allah has power over them, or do you truly believe that His takwin can bring about a concrete, real change? What is holding you back from expecting that?
?
If knowledge and power do not necessitate existence, but takwin does, how does that affect your prayer life? Do you pray with the expectation that Allah's takwin will bring your request into being, or do you treat it as a mere possibility? What needs to shift in your heart to pray with certainty that His 'Be' is enough?

Answering Objections to Takwin as a Separate Attribute
Addresses objections to takwin as a separate attribute and affirms sufficiency of power and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against takwin as a separate attribute and how the author resolves them, concluding that power and will together suffice.

Core Idea
Takwin is not a separate attribute; power and will together bring about existence.

In this lesson, we examine the objections raised against the concept of takwin as a distinct attribute. The author addresses two main objections: one concerning necessity (that takwin would make God a necessitator) and another concerning duplication (that if takwin is effective, it is just power). The author refutes these and concludes that power and will together are sufficient, without needing an additional attribute of takwin.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections portion
Read from 'وقالوا بأزليته' to the end of the passage. Identify the two objections mentioned.
2
Understand the first objection and response
The first objection is about necessity. The response: necessity is subsequent to will, not prior.
This preserves God's free will.
3
Understand the second objection and response
The second objection is about duplication. The response: power and takwin have different objects of relation.
Thus no duplication.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that power and will together are sufficient; no separate takwin attribute needed.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Debate on Limiting Divine Attributes
Lists extra attributes, then presents and refutes the argument for limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether Allah's attributes are limited to seven or eight, the arguments for limitation, and the critique offered by Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
The number of divine attributes is not fixed by reason alone.

This passage from al-Muhassal presents a classic debate in kalam: Are Allah's attributes limited to seven or eight, or can they be many more? After surveying various scholarly positions, Imam al-Razi presents and then dismantles the argument for limiting attributes, ultimately advocating for suspension of judgment (tawaqquf). The passage is a model of critical theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Survey the list of extra attributes
Read the first part carefully. Each scholar (al-Ash'ari, al-Isfarayini, etc.) added specific attributes. See how they differ from each other.
Al-Ash'ari added hand and face as attributes, while others added perception or states.
2
Understand the verdict of suspension
After listing, al-Razi states that fairness requires tawaqquf—neither affirming nor denying these extra attributes without proof.
This is a key methodological point.
3
Examine the argument for limitation
The opponent argues we must have complete knowledge of attributes, but the only way to know is through inference from actions and transcendence, which only yield seven/eight.
Identify the premises and conclusion.
4
Trace the three objections
Al-Razi questions: (1) Are we commanded to know all attributes? (2) Even if we are, do we need a proof? (3) Even if we need proof, does it only yield these attributes? Each objection dismantles the argument.
Notice how he uses the Ash'ari doctrine of taklif ma la yutaq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الظاهريون من المتكلمين زعموا أنه لا صفة لله تعالى وراء السبع أو الثماني. وأثبت أبو الحسن الأشعري اليد صفة وراء القدرة، والوجه صفة وراء الوجود، وأثبت الاستواء صفة أخرى. وأثبت أبو إسحاق الإسفرائيني صفة توجب الاستغناء عن المكان. وأثبت القاضي صفات ثلاثة أخرى وهي إدراك الشم والذوق واللمس. وأثبت عبد الله بن سعيد القدم صفة وراء البقاء. وأثبت مثبتو الحال العالمية أمرًا وراء العلم، وكذا القول في سائر الصفات. وأثبت أبو سهل الصعلوكي لله تعالى بحسب كل معلوم وبحسب كل مقدور قدرة، وأثبت عبد الله بن سعيد الرحمة والكرم والرضا والسخط صفات وراء الإرادة. والإنصاف أنه لا دلالة على ثبوت هذه الصفات ولا على نفيها، فيجب التوقف.

واحتج من حصر الصفات في السبع أو الثماني بأنا كلفنا بكمال المعرفة، وكمال المعرفة إنما يحصل بمعرفة جميع الصفات، ومعرفة جميع الصفات لا يتأتى إلا بطريق، ولا طريق إلا الاستدلال بالأفعال والتنزيه عن النقائص، وهذان الطريقان لا يدلان إلا على هذه الصفات.

والجواب: لم قلت إننا أمرنا بكمال المعرفة؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال إنا ما أمرنا بأن نعرف من صفات الله تعالى إلا القدرة التي يتوقف على العلم بها تصديق محمد عليه السلام؟ سلمناه، لكن لا نسلم أنه لا بد من الدليل، سيما وعندنا التكاليف بأسرها تكليف ما لا يطاق. سلمناه، لكن لم قلت إن الاستدلال بالأفعال وتنزيه الله عن النقائص لا يدل إلا على هذه الصفات.
Issue: The Zahirites among the theologians claimed that there is no attribute for God beyond seven or eight. And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari affirmed the hand as an attribute beyond power, and the face as an attribute beyond existence, and affirmed al-Istiwa' as another attribute. And Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini affirmed an attribute that necessitates independence from place. And the judge affirmed three other attributes: perception of smell, taste, and touch. And Abdullah ibn Sa'id affirmed eternity as an attribute beyond everlastingness. And the affirmers of states affirmed 'worldliness' as something beyond knowledge, and likewise for the rest of attributes. And Abu Sahl al-Su'luki affirmed for God, according to every known and every predestined, a power, and Abdullah ibn Sa'id affirmed mercy, generosity, pleasure, and anger as attributes beyond will. And fairness dictates: there is no proof for the existence of these attributes nor for their negation, therefore suspension of judgment is necessary.

And the one who restricts attributes to seven or eight argued: we are obligated with complete knowledge, and complete knowledge is only attained by knowing all attributes, and knowing all attributes is only possible through a method, and there is no method except inference from actions and transcendence above deficiencies, and these two methods only indicate these attributes.

And the answer: Why do you say that we are commanded with complete knowledge? And why is it not permissible to say that we are only commanded to know from the attributes of God that power upon whose knowledge depends the verification of Muhammad, peace be upon him? We grant it, but we do not grant that there must be a proof, especially since with us all obligations are an obligation of what is not possible. We grant it, but why do you say that inference from actions and transcendence above deficiencies only indicates these attributes?
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a theological debate about whether divine attributes are limited to seven or eight. It begins by listing various scholars who affirmed additional attributes beyond the seven or eight, including al-Ash'ari's affirming Hand and Face, al-Isfarayini's attribute of independence, and others. The passage then states the fair position: there is no proof for or against these, so suspension is required.

The second part presents the argument of those who restrict attributes to seven/eight: we are commanded to have perfect knowledge of God, and perfect knowledge requires knowing all attributes; but the only ways to know attributes are through inference from God's actions and from His transcendence of deficiencies; and these two methods only indicate seven or eight attributes. Therefore, only those attributes are established.

The refutation proceeds in three steps: First, it questions the premise that we are commanded with perfect knowledge—perhaps we are only commanded to know the attributes necessary for verifying prophethood. Second, it grants that we are commanded but denies that we must have a proof from inference, especially since the Ash'ari school holds that all obligations are obligations of what is beyond capacity. Third, it grants the need for proof but denies that inference from actions and transcendence only yields seven/eight attributes—perhaps they could indicate more. Thus the limiting argument is refuted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that al-Ash'ari and others added attributes arbitrarily based on personal opinion.
Correction: The passage shows they added attributes based on scriptural evidence (e.g., hand, face) or reasoning (e.g., independence from place). The debate is about the method of establishing attributes, not arbitrary addition.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to the argument that we must know all attributes through inference from actions and transcendence?
A) Inference from actions is invalid.
B) We are not commanded to know all attributes, only those necessary for faith.
C) The two methods indicate more than seven attributes.
D) The attributes are unknowable.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi argues that there is no decisive proof for limiting divine attributes to a fixed number, and that the claim of necessary complete knowledge through inference is unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you feel a personal pressure to know everything about God's attributes to be a 'complete' believer? What if the Prophet only required you to know what is necessary to trust and obey Him? Are you willing to let go of that burden tonight?
?
The refutation questions whether reason alone can prove that we are obligated to have complete knowledge. If your own reasoning has led you to demand exhaustive answers about God, could that be a form of misplaced certainty? What would it look like to suspend judgment on matters where the Quran and Sunnah are silent?
?
Imam al-Razi says there is no proof for or against many proposed attributes. Is your heart comfortable with not knowing? Or does uncertainty about God's nature trouble you? If you struggle, would you be willing to say, 'I don't know, but I trust Allah's wisdom' and leave it there?

The Two Positions on Knowing God's Essence
Introduces the debate and the majority's proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views on whether God's essence is knowable and the argument from the majority that existence is identical to essence.

Core Idea
Knowing God's existence means knowing His essence—unless a thing can be both known and unknown.

This passage opens a major theological debate: can humans know the reality of God's essence? Two camps are presented: the negators (Dirar, al-Ghazali, and philosophers) and the affirmers (majority of theologians). The affirmers offer a direct proof: since we know God's existence and existence is identical to essence, we must know His essence. Let's examine this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the two positions carefully
Identify the two groups: those who deny knowledge of God's essence (Dirar, al-Ghazali, philosophers) and those who affirm it (majority of Ash'ari and Mu'tazila).
Notice that the negators include both earlier and later figures.
2
Analyze the majority's proof
The proof is a simple syllogism: (1) We know God's existence. (2) Existence is identical to essence. (3) Therefore we know His essence. Consider why the consequence of denial is contradictory.
The key premise is the identity of existence and essence.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: What would it mean if existence and essence were not identical? How does the argument rely on this identity?
This proof is foundational for the affirmative position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: ذهب ضرار من المتكلمين والغزالي من المتأخرين إلى أنا لا نعرف حقيقة ذات الله تعالى، وهو قول الحكماء. وذهب جمهور المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة إلى أنها معلومة.

حجة المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة: أنا نعرف وجوده ووجوده عين ذاته، فلا بد وأن نعلم ذاته، وإلا لكان الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد معلوما مجهولا.

حجة الفريق الثاني من وجهين:
الأول: أما المعلوم عندنا منه سبحانه، فإما السلب كقولنا ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض، ولا شك أن الماهية مغايرة لسلب ما عداها عنها، وإما الإضافات كقولنا قادر عالم، فلا شك أن الماهية مغايرة لهذه الإضافات، لأن المعلوم عندنا من قدرة الله تعالى أنه أمر مستلزم للتأثير في الفعل على سبيل الصحة، فماهية القدرة مجهولة والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا اللازم وهو التأثير المخصوص. وكذلك المعلوم عندنا من علم الله تعالى ليس إلا أنه أمر يلزمه الإحكام والإتقان في الفعل، فماهية ذلك العلم غير هذا الأثر، والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا الأثر. فظهر أن ماهية صفات الله تعالى غير معلومة لنا، وبتقدير أن تكون معلومة، لكن العلم بالصفة لا يستلزم العلم بماهية الموصوف على التفصيل. ولما دل الاستقراء على سبيل الإنصاف أنا لا نعلم من الله تعالى إلا المسلوب والإضافات، وثبت أن العلم بهما لا يستلزم العلم بالماهية، ثبت أنا لا نعلم حقيقة الله تعالى.

الثاني: أنا قد بينا في أول هذا الكتاب أنه لا يمكننا أن نتصور شيئا إلا الذي ندركه بحواسنا أو نجده من نفوسنا أو نتصوره من عقولنا أو ما يتركب عن أحد هذه الثلاثة، فالماهية الإلهية خارجة عن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، فهي غير معلومة لنا.

(٢) أقول: القول بأن المعلوم منه تعالى إما السلب والإضافات ليس بمسلم عند المتكلمين، لأنهم يقولون وجود الله تعالى معلوم وليس هو صفة سلبية ولا إضافية. والحكماء يقولون في الجواب عنه: إن الموجود المعلوم هو المشترك الذي يحمل عليه تعالى وعلى غيره لا بالسواء بل بالتشكيك، والموضوع بهذا المحمول هو حقيقته تعالى الواجب وجودها لذاته التي لا يعبر عنها إلا بوصف سلب أو إضافي. فيقال مثلاً الوجود القائم بذاته الذي لا يعارض الماهية، ولو نعتا هذا هو الأمر المشترك المقارب المقارن الأسلوب، أو تلك الحقيقة فغير معلومة لغيره تعالى.
Issue: Dirar among the theologians and al-Ghazali among the later ones held that we do not know the reality of the essence of God, the Exalted, and it is the saying of the philosophers. And the majority of theologians from us (Ash'aris) and from the Mu'tazila held that it is known.

Proof of the theologians from us and the Mu'tazila: We know His existence, and His existence is identical to His essence, so necessarily we know His essence; otherwise one thing would be, in one respect, both known and unknown.

Proof of the second group in two ways:
First: What is known from Him, the Exalted, is either negations (as our saying 'not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident'), and there is no doubt that the quiddity is different from the negation of what is other than it; or relations (as our saying 'powerful, knowing'), and there is no doubt that the quiddity is different from these relations, because what is known from the power of God is that it is something necessitating effect in the act in the mode of validity, so the quiddity of power is unknown, and what is known is only this necessary consequent, which is the specific effect. Likewise, what is known from the knowledge of God is only that it is something that necessitates perfection and precision in the act, so the quiddity of that knowledge is other than this effect, and what is known is only this effect. So it appears that the quiddity of the attributes of God is unknown to us, and even if it were known, knowledge of the attribute does not necessitate knowledge of the quiddity of the described in detail. And when induction reveals, in fairness, that we only know from God negations and relations, and it is established that knowledge of them does not necessitate knowledge of the quiddity, it is established that we do not know the reality of God, the Exalted.

Second: We have shown at the beginning of this book that we cannot conceive anything except what we perceive by our senses, or find in our selves, or conceive by our intellects, or what is composed of one of these three. The divine quiddity is outside these three categories, so it is unknown to us.

(2) I say: The statement that what is known from Him, the Exalted, is either negations or relations is not granted by the theologians, because they say that the existence of God, the Exalted, is known, and it is neither a negative nor a relational attribute. And the philosophers say in response: The known existent is the common predicate that applies to Him, the Exalted, and to other than Him not univocally but analogically, and the subject of this predicate is His reality, the Exalted, whose existence is necessary by itself, which cannot be expressed except by a negative or relational description. For example, 'the existence subsisting by itself that does not oppose quiddity'—if it is described thus, this is the common, approximate, analogous expression; or that reality is unknown to other than Him, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a debate on whether the essence (dhat) of God can be known. It first states two positions: Dirar and al-Ghazali (later supported by philosophers) hold that we cannot know the reality of God's essence, while the majority of theologians (Ash'ari and Mu'tazila) hold that it is known.

The proof of the majority is straightforward: we know God's existence, and that existence is identical to His essence, so we must know His essence; otherwise the same thing would be both known and unknown in the same respect.

The second group (negators) offers two proofs. The first proof argues that from God, we only know negations (like 'not a body') and relations (like 'powerful'). The quiddity (mahiyya) of these attributes is unknown; we only know their effects. Since knowledge of negations and relations does not entail knowledge of the quiddity, we do not know the divine essence. The second proof draws on an epistemological principle: we can only conceive things through senses, introspection, or reason; the divine essence falls outside these categories, so it is unknowable.

The passage ends with a comment (marked 'I say') that replies to the negators' first proof: theologians do not accept that we only know negations and relations, because they hold that God's existence itself is known and is neither negative nor relational. The philosophers counter that the known existence is a common term applied analogically, and the subject of that term is God's reality, which can only be described negatively or relationally, implying it is ultimately unknown.

Overall, the passage lays out the arguments and counterarguments without definitively settling the issue, leaving the reader to consider both sides.

الوجود

A) Because the Qur'an describes it.

D) Because negation of knowledge leads to atheism.

Key Takeaway
The majority argues that since we know God's existence and existence is the same as essence, we must know His essence on pain of contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowing God's existence is identical to knowing His essence, then every time you affirm His existence you claim to know His very nature. Do you live as if you truly know Him? What would change if you took this knowledge seriously in your worship and daily choices?
?
The majority argues that we cannot avoid knowing God's essence without absurdity. Yet many Muslims act as though God is an abstract concept. In what area of your life do you treat God as unknown, and what one step can you take today to let that knowledge transform your actions?
?
You know that God exists, but do you know Him intimately? Reflect on the gap between intellectual acknowledgment and heart-felt certainty. What is the biggest obstacle preventing your knowledge of God from penetrating your soul, and what will you do about it before the day ends?

The First Negator Argument: Negations and Relations
First proof of the negators using negations and relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that we cannot know God's essence, which argues that we only know negations and relations, not the quiddity itself.

Core Idea
We only know God through what He is not and through His effects, not His essence.

The negators (those who deny knowledge of God's essence) offer two proofs. The first proof argues that everything we know about God is either a negation (e.g., 'not a body') or a relation (e.g., 'powerful'). The quiddity (mahiyya) of these attributes is unknown; we only know their effects. Therefore, we do not know the divine essence. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof in full
Follow the argument carefully: it divides what we know into negations and relations, then argues that the quiddity of each is unknown.
The argument uses 'quiddity' to refer to the real essence of something.
2
Understand the distinction between attribute and effect
For power and knowledge, the text says we only know their effects (e.g., power's effect is producing acts, knowledge's effect is precision). The quiddity of the attribute itself remains unknown.
This is crucial for the conclusion.
3
Consider the inductive claim
The argument concludes from induction that we only know negations and relations from God, which do not reveal the essence.
Induction here means surveying all possible knowledge.

Source Text

مسألة: ذهب ضرار من المتكلمين والغزالي من المتأخرين إلى أنا لا نعرف حقيقة ذات الله تعالى، وهو قول الحكماء. وذهب جمهور المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة إلى أنها معلومة.

حجة المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة: أنا نعرف وجوده ووجوده عين ذاته، فلا بد وأن نعلم ذاته، وإلا لكان الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد معلوما مجهولا.

حجة الفريق الثاني من وجهين:
الأول: أما المعلوم عندنا منه سبحانه، فإما السلب كقولنا ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض، ولا شك أن الماهية مغايرة لسلب ما عداها عنها، وإما الإضافات كقولنا قادر عالم، فلا شك أن الماهية مغايرة لهذه الإضافات، لأن المعلوم عندنا من قدرة الله تعالى أنه أمر مستلزم للتأثير في الفعل على سبيل الصحة، فماهية القدرة مجهولة والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا اللازم وهو التأثير المخصوص. وكذلك المعلوم عندنا من علم الله تعالى ليس إلا أنه أمر يلزمه الإحكام والإتقان في الفعل، فماهية ذلك العلم غير هذا الأثر، والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا الأثر. فظهر أن ماهية صفات الله تعالى غير معلومة لنا، وبتقدير أن تكون معلومة، لكن العلم بالصفة لا يستلزم العلم بماهية الموصوف على التفصيل. ولما دل الاستقراء على سبيل الإنصاف أنا لا نعلم من الله تعالى إلا المسلوب والإضافات، وثبت أن العلم بهما لا يستلزم العلم بالماهية، ثبت أنا لا نعلم حقيقة الله تعالى.

الثاني: أنا قد بينا في أول هذا الكتاب أنه لا يمكننا أن نتصور شيئا إلا الذي ندركه بحواسنا أو نجده من نفوسنا أو نتصوره من عقولنا أو ما يتركب عن أحد هذه الثلاثة، فالماهية الإلهية خارجة عن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، فهي غير معلومة لنا.

(٢) أقول: القول بأن المعلوم منه تعالى إما السلب والإضافات ليس بمسلم عند المتكلمين، لأنهم يقولون وجود الله تعالى معلوم وليس هو صفة سلبية ولا إضافية. والحكماء يقولون في الجواب عنه: إن الموجود المعلوم هو المشترك الذي يحمل عليه تعالى وعلى غيره لا بالسواء بل بالتشكيك، والموضوع بهذا المحمول هو حقيقته تعالى الواجب وجودها لذاته التي لا يعبر عنها إلا بوصف سلب أو إضافي. فيقال مثلاً الوجود القائم بذاته الذي لا يعارض الماهية، ولو نعتا هذا هو الأمر المشترك المقارب المقارن الأسلوب، أو تلك الحقيقة فغير معلومة لغيره تعالى.
Issue: Dirar among the theologians and al-Ghazali among the later ones held that we do not know the reality of the essence of God, the Exalted, and it is the saying of the philosophers. And the majority of theologians from us (Ash'aris) and from the Mu'tazila held that it is known.

Proof of the theologians from us and the Mu'tazila: We know His existence, and His existence is identical to His essence, so necessarily we know His essence; otherwise one thing would be, in one respect, both known and unknown.

Proof of the second group in two ways:
First: What is known from Him, the Exalted, is either negations (as our saying 'not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident'), and there is no doubt that the quiddity is different from the negation of what is other than it; or relations (as our saying 'powerful, knowing'), and there is no doubt that the quiddity is different from these relations, because what is known from the power of God is that it is something necessitating effect in the act in the mode of validity, so the quiddity of power is unknown, and what is known is only this necessary consequent, which is the specific effect. Likewise, what is known from the knowledge of God is only that it is something that necessitates perfection and precision in the act, so the quiddity of that knowledge is other than this effect, and what is known is only this effect. So it appears that the quiddity of the attributes of God is unknown to us, and even if it were known, knowledge of the attribute does not necessitate knowledge of the quiddity of the described in detail. And when induction reveals, in fairness, that we only know from God negations and relations, and it is established that knowledge of them does not necessitate knowledge of the quiddity, it is established that we do not know the reality of God, the Exalted.

Second: We have shown at the beginning of this book that we cannot conceive anything except what we perceive by our senses, or find in our selves, or conceive by our intellects, or what is composed of one of these three. The divine quiddity is outside these three categories, so it is unknown to us.

(2) I say: The statement that what is known from Him, the Exalted, is either negations or relations is not granted by the theologians, because they say that the existence of God, the Exalted, is known, and it is neither a negative nor a relational attribute. And the philosophers say in response: The known existent is the common predicate that applies to Him, the Exalted, and to other than Him not univocally but analogically, and the subject of this predicate is His reality, the Exalted, whose existence is necessary by itself, which cannot be expressed except by a negative or relational description. For example, 'the existence subsisting by itself that does not oppose quiddity'—if it is described thus, this is the common, approximate, analogous expression; or that reality is unknown to other than Him, the Exalted.

Commentary
This passage presents a debate on whether the essence (dhat) of God can be known. It first states two positions: Dirar and al-Ghazali (later supported by philosophers) hold that we cannot know the reality of God's essence, while the majority of theologians (Ash'ari and Mu'tazila) hold that it is known.

The proof of the majority is straightforward: we know God's existence, and that existence is identical to His essence, so we must know His essence; otherwise the same thing would be both known and unknown in the same respect.

The second group (negators) offers two proofs. The first proof argues that from God, we only know negations (like 'not a body') and relations (like 'powerful'). The quiddity (mahiyya) of these attributes is unknown; we only know their effects. Since knowledge of negations and relations does not entail knowledge of the quiddity, we do not know the divine essence. The second proof draws on an epistemological principle: we can only conceive things through senses, introspection, or reason; the divine essence falls outside these categories, so it is unknowable.

The passage ends with a comment (marked 'I say') that replies to the negators' first proof: theologians do not accept that we only know negations and relations, because they hold that God's existence itself is known and is neither negative nor relational. The philosophers counter that the known existence is a common term applied analogically, and the subject of that term is God's reality, which can only be described negatively or relationally, implying it is ultimately unknown.

Overall, the passage lays out the arguments and counterarguments without definitively settling the issue, leaving the reader to consider both sides.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that knowing God's attributes (e.g., power) gives us direct knowledge of His essence.
Correction: The argument shows that we only know the effects of the attributes, not the quiddity of the attributes themselves, and even if we did, that would not give us the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does knowing God's power not give us knowledge of its quiddity?
A) Because power is unknowable by definition.
B) Because we only know its effect (producing acts), not its real nature.
C) Because the theologians disagree.
D) Because power is a negation.

Key Takeaway
The first negator proof argues that all our knowledge of God is either negative or relational, neither of which reveals the quiddity, so the essence remains unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is all-knowing,' do you realize that you are only describing a relation to His acts? How does this affect your struggle to trust His plan when you don't see the perfection?
?
If you truly cannot know God's essence, then what is the object of your love and devotion? Is it enough that your descriptions of Him are based only on what He is not and how He acts?
?
The lesson says we only know negations and relations. Have you ever stopped to ask yourself whether your prayer is directed at a being you admit you cannot know? How does that thought change your sense of awe?

The Second Negator Argument and the Author's Response
Second negator proof and author's comment with reply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against knowing God's essence based on epistemological limits, and the author's reply distinguishing existence as a common analogical term.

Core Idea
The divine essence exceeds all human modes of conception, but existence is known analogically.

The negators present a second proof: humans can only conceive things through senses, introspection, or reason; the divine essence falls outside all three categories. Therefore, it is unknowable. The author then adds a comment: the theologians object that we know God's existence, which is neither negation nor relation. The philosophers respond by saying existence is a common analogical term, and the subject of that term is God's reality, which can only be described negatively or relationally. This leaves the essence ultimately unknown.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Notice the epistemological premise: all conception arises from senses, introspection, or reason. The divine essence is outside these, so it is inconceivable.
This is a philosophical argument about the limits of human cognition.
2
Read the author's comment
The author (likely al-Razi) presents an objection from the theologians: we know God's existence, which is not a negation or relation. Then the philosophers' reply: existence is a common analogical term, and the reality behind it is only describable negatively or relationally.
The key term here is 'تشكيك' (tashkik, analogical predication).
3
Reflect on the debate's outcome
The passage does not declare a winner; it leaves the issue open. The philosophers effectively argue that even the knowledge of existence does not give the essence.
This is a sophisticated theological-epistemological discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: ذهب ضرار من المتكلمين والغزالي من المتأخرين إلى أنا لا نعرف حقيقة ذات الله تعالى، وهو قول الحكماء. وذهب جمهور المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة إلى أنها معلومة.

حجة المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة: أنا نعرف وجوده ووجوده عين ذاته، فلا بد وأن نعلم ذاته، وإلا لكان الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد معلوما مجهولا.

حجة الفريق الثاني من وجهين:
الأول: أما المعلوم عندنا منه سبحانه، فإما السلب كقولنا ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض، ولا شك أن الماهية مغايرة لسلب ما عداها عنها، وإما الإضافات كقولنا قادر عالم، فلا شك أن الماهية مغايرة لهذه الإضافات، لأن المعلوم عندنا من قدرة الله تعالى أنه أمر مستلزم للتأثير في الفعل على سبيل الصحة، فماهية القدرة مجهولة والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا اللازم وهو التأثير المخصوص. وكذلك المعلوم عندنا من علم الله تعالى ليس إلا أنه أمر يلزمه الإحكام والإتقان في الفعل، فماهية ذلك العلم غير هذا الأثر، والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا الأثر. فظهر أن ماهية صفات الله تعالى غير معلومة لنا، وبتقدير أن تكون معلومة، لكن العلم بالصفة لا يستلزم العلم بماهية الموصوف على التفصيل. ولما دل الاستقراء على سبيل الإنصاف أنا لا نعلم من الله تعالى إلا المسلوب والإضافات، وثبت أن العلم بهما لا يستلزم العلم بالماهية، ثبت أنا لا نعلم حقيقة الله تعالى.

الثاني: أنا قد بينا في أول هذا الكتاب أنه لا يمكننا أن نتصور شيئا إلا الذي ندركه بحواسنا أو نجده من نفوسنا أو نتصوره من عقولنا أو ما يتركب عن أحد هذه الثلاثة، فالماهية الإلهية خارجة عن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، فهي غير معلومة لنا.

(٢) أقول: القول بأن المعلوم منه تعالى إما السلب والإضافات ليس بمسلم عند المتكلمين، لأنهم يقولون وجود الله تعالى معلوم وليس هو صفة سلبية ولا إضافية. والحكماء يقولون في الجواب عنه: إن الموجود المعلوم هو المشترك الذي يحمل عليه تعالى وعلى غيره لا بالسواء بل بالتشكيك، والموضوع بهذا المحمول هو حقيقته تعالى الواجب وجودها لذاته التي لا يعبر عنها إلا بوصف سلب أو إضافي. فيقال مثلاً الوجود القائم بذاته الذي لا يعارض الماهية، ولو نعتا هذا هو الأمر المشترك المقارب المقارن الأسلوب، أو تلك الحقيقة فغير معلومة لغيره تعالى.
Issue: Dirar among the theologians and al-Ghazali among the later ones held that we do not know the reality of the essence of God, the Exalted, and it is the saying of the philosophers. And the majority of theologians from us (Ash'aris) and from the Mu'tazila held that it is known.

Proof of the theologians from us and the Mu'tazila: We know His existence, and His existence is identical to His essence, so necessarily we know His essence; otherwise one thing would be, in one respect, both known and unknown.

Proof of the second group in two ways:
First: What is known from Him, the Exalted, is either negations (as our saying 'not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident'), and there is no doubt that the quiddity is different from the negation of what is other than it; or relations (as our saying 'powerful, knowing'), and there is no doubt that the quiddity is different from these relations, because what is known from the power of God is that it is something necessitating effect in the act in the mode of validity, so the quiddity of power is unknown, and what is known is only this necessary consequent, which is the specific effect. Likewise, what is known from the knowledge of God is only that it is something that necessitates perfection and precision in the act, so the quiddity of that knowledge is other than this effect, and what is known is only this effect. So it appears that the quiddity of the attributes of God is unknown to us, and even if it were known, knowledge of the attribute does not necessitate knowledge of the quiddity of the described in detail. And when induction reveals, in fairness, that we only know from God negations and relations, and it is established that knowledge of them does not necessitate knowledge of the quiddity, it is established that we do not know the reality of God, the Exalted.

Second: We have shown at the beginning of this book that we cannot conceive anything except what we perceive by our senses, or find in our selves, or conceive by our intellects, or what is composed of one of these three. The divine quiddity is outside these three categories, so it is unknown to us.

(2) I say: The statement that what is known from Him, the Exalted, is either negations or relations is not granted by the theologians, because they say that the existence of God, the Exalted, is known, and it is neither a negative nor a relational attribute. And the philosophers say in response: The known existent is the common predicate that applies to Him, the Exalted, and to other than Him not univocally but analogically, and the subject of this predicate is His reality, the Exalted, whose existence is necessary by itself, which cannot be expressed except by a negative or relational description. For example, 'the existence subsisting by itself that does not oppose quiddity'—if it is described thus, this is the common, approximate, analogous expression; or that reality is unknown to other than Him, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a debate on whether the essence (dhat) of God can be known. It first states two positions: Dirar and al-Ghazali (later supported by philosophers) hold that we cannot know the reality of God's essence, while the majority of theologians (Ash'ari and Mu'tazila) hold that it is known.

The proof of the majority is straightforward: we know God's existence, and that existence is identical to His essence, so we must know His essence; otherwise the same thing would be both known and unknown in the same respect.

The second group (negators) offers two proofs. The first proof argues that from God, we only know negations (like 'not a body') and relations (like 'powerful'). The quiddity (mahiyya) of these attributes is unknown; we only know their effects. Since knowledge of negations and relations does not entail knowledge of the quiddity, we do not know the divine essence. The second proof draws on an epistemological principle: we can only conceive things through senses, introspection, or reason; the divine essence falls outside these categories, so it is unknowable.

The passage ends with a comment (marked 'I say') that replies to the negators' first proof: theologians do not accept that we only know negations and relations, because they hold that God's existence itself is known and is neither negative nor relational. The philosophers counter that the known existence is a common term applied analogically, and the subject of that term is God's reality, which can only be described negatively or relationally, implying it is ultimately unknown.

Overall, the passage lays out the arguments and counterarguments without definitively settling the issue, leaving the reader to consider both sides.

التشكيك
الوجود
السلب
الإضافات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that knowing God's existence directly implies knowing His essence in detail.
Correction: The philosophers argue that existence is an analogical term, and the subject of that term is only describable through negations and relations, not in itself.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' response, why does knowing God's existence not give knowledge of His essence?
A) Because existence is a negative attribute.
B) Because existence is a common analogical term applied to God and creatures, and the subject of that term can only be described negatively or relationally.
C) Because existence is unknowable.
D) Because the theologians deny existence.

Key Takeaway
The second proof and the author's comment reinforce the negators' position: even if we know existence, that knowledge is analogical and does not reveal the divine essence in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that your most refined intellectual concepts cannot reach God's essence. In your moments of prayer and worship, do you secretly imagine that you 'understand' God? How would surrendering to His incomprehensibility change the posture of your heart?
?
If God's existence is known only analogically, not as you know the existence of other things, does that distance feel frightening or freeing? What would it mean for you to trust in His revealed names even when they do not match your mental categories?
?
When you seek closeness to God, do you rely on your own reasoning or on the guidance He gave? The author here shows the limits of unaided reason. Where in your life are you still trying to reach God through your own intellect rather than through submission to His revelation?

The Opponent's Challenge on Proof for Attributes
A dialectical exchange on the need for proof to know God's attributes through actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's dialectical move in challenging both the necessity and the scope of rational proof for divine attributes.

Core Idea
Concession is not agreement: the opponent questions both the need for proof and its scope.

In this passage, the opponent engages in a dialectical exchange, first conceding that knowledge of God's power is required for affirming prophethood. However, he then raises two objections: first, that proof may not be necessary, especially given the controversial doctrine of 'obligation of what is beyond capacity'; second, even if proof is needed, it may not yield only these specific attributes. This sets the stage for the author's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the concessive tone and the two questions.
The opponent uses 'sallamnā' to concede, then 'lā nusallim' to object.
2
Identify the three dialectical moves
The passage has three parts: a concession, a first objection (against the necessity of proof), and a second objection (against the scope of inference). Find each.
3
Reflect on the opponent's strategy
Ask yourself: Why does the opponent concede one point but attack others? What does this reveal about the broader debate on divine attributes?
This connects to the learning objective about dialectical argumentation.

الله تعالى إلا القدرة التى يتوقف على العلم بها تصديق محمد عليه السلام ، سلمناه لكن لا نسلم أنه لابد من الدليل سيما وعندنا التكاليف بأسرها تكليف ما لا يطاق ، سلمناه لكن لم قلت إن الاستدلال بالأفعال وتنزيه الله عن النقائص لا يدل إلا على هذه الصفات (١)
