We conceded that, but why did you say that inference from actions and the transcendence of God from imperfections indicates only these attributes?
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange within a larger argument about divine attributes. The opponent (possibly a Mu'tazilite or other) appears to have conceded that we need to know God's power to affirm prophethood, but then raises two objections: (1) that proof (dalīl) is not always necessary, especially given the doctrine that God may oblige what is beyond capacity (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), a reference to a debated theological position; and (2) even if proof is needed, why does inference from God's actions and from negating imperfections lead only to these specific attributes (presumably the attributes that can be inferred from creation and transcendence)? The structure is: the opponent concedes a point (sallamnā) but then refuses to concede the next step, ending with a direct challenge. The teacher should note that this objection presupposes a particular view on divine attributes and the scope of rational inference.

First proof: the mountain verse with objection and answer
Razi presents the first scriptural proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the mountain verse to argue for the possibility of seeing Allah and how he answers the objection that the condition was impossible.

Core Idea
The mountain verse proves seeing Allah is possible because the condition is possible, not because it actually happened.

In the debate over whether Allah can be seen in the afterlife, scriptural proofs are crucial. Razi's first proof cites the verse where Allah tells Moses, 'If the mountain settles, you will see Me.' The argument hinges on whether the condition is possible. But an objection arises: the mountain was moving, so settling was impossible. How does Razi defend the proof? Let's explore.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of proof, objection, and answer.
Notice the logical structure: proof, then 'if it is said' for objection, then 'the answer'.
2
Understand the proof's logic
Identify the core argument: vision is suspended on settling, settling is possible, so vision is possible. Why does Razi consider settling possible?
The verse mentions the mountain's essence, which can be still.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector argues that the condition was impossible because the mountain was moving at the time of speech. Trace how he uses the particle 'in' to claim the condition refers to the future, and that failure of vision implies the mountain remained moving.
The objector asserts no middle state between motion and stillness.
4
Examine Razi's answer
Razi concedes the mountain was moving but distinguishes between the mountain's essence (which can be still) and its accidental state. The verse only mentions the essence, so the condition remains possible.
This answer preserves the proof by focusing on the capacity for stillness, not the actual state.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for the broader debate on seeing Allah. It shows how Kalam scholars defend scriptural arguments against objections by analyzing language and ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتمد في المسألة الدلائل السمعية. أحدها: أن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة باستقرار الجبل وهو ممكن والمعلق على الممكن ممكن فالرؤية ممكنة. فإن قيل: لا نسلم أنه علق الرؤية على شرط ممكن بل على شرط محال، لأنه علقها على استقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا وذلك محال، وإنما قلنا أنه علقها على استقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا لأن صيغة "إن" إذا دخلت على الماضي صارت بمعنى المستقبل فقوله "إن استقر" أي لو صار مستقرا في الزمان المستقبل فسوف تراني. ثم أنه في الزمان المستقبل إما أن يقال أنه صار مستقرا أو ما صار مستقرا، فإن صار مستقرا وجب حصول الرؤية لوجوب حصول المشروط عند حصول الشرط، فلما لم تحصل الرؤية بالإجماع علمنا أن الجبل لم يستقر، وإذا لم يكن مستقرا كان متحركا ضرورة أنه لا واسطة بين الحركة والسكون، فإن الجبل حال ما علق الله الرؤية باستقراره كان متحركا، ومعلوم أن استقرار المتحرك حال كونه متحركا محال، فثبت أن الشرط ممتنع فلا يلزم القطع بجواز المشروط. والجواب: سلمنا أن الجبل في تلك الحال كان متحركا لكن الجبل بما هو جبل يصح السكون عليه والمذكور في الآية ليس إلا ذات الجبل، وأما المقتضي لامتناع السكون فهو حصول السكون، فإذا القدرة المذكورة في الآية منشأ لصحة الاستقرار وما هو المنشأ لامتناع الاستقرار فغير مذكور في الآية فوجب القطع بالصحة.
And the relied-upon in the matter are the scriptural proofs. First: that the vision of Allah the Exalted is suspended upon the settling of the mountain, which is possible, and that which is suspended upon the possible is possible, so vision is possible. If it is said: we do not concede that He suspended vision upon a possible condition, but rather upon an impossible condition, because He suspended it upon the settling of the mountain while it was moving, and that is impossible; and we only said that He suspended it upon the settling of the mountain while it was moving because the particle 'in' when it enters upon the past tense becomes in the meaning of the future, so His saying 'if it settles' means 'if it becomes settled in the future time, then you will see Me'. Then in the future time, it is either said that it became settled or it did not become settled; if it became settled, the occurrence of vision would be necessary due to the necessity of the occurrence of the conditioned upon the occurrence of the condition; and when vision did not occur by consensus, we know that the mountain did not settle; and if it was not settled, it was moving necessarily, because there is no intermediate between motion and stillness; for the mountain at the time Allah suspended vision upon its settling was moving; and it is known that the settling of a moving thing while it is moving is impossible; thus it is established that the condition is impossible, so the judgment of the permissibility of the conditioned does not follow. The answer: we concede that the mountain at that state was moving, but the mountain insofar as it is a mountain, stillness is valid for it, and what is mentioned in the verse is only the essence of the mountain; and as for that which necessitates the impossibility of stillness, it is the occurrence of stillness; so the power mentioned in the verse is the source of the validity of settling, and what is the source of the impossibility of settling is not mentioned in the verse, so the judgment of validity is necessary.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the first scriptural proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, followed by an objection and its answer. The proof is based on the verse where Allah says to Moses, 'If the mountain settles in its place, then you will see Me.' The argument is: Allah suspended vision on the settling of the mountain. Settling is possible (since mountains can be stationary), so that which is suspended on a possible thing is itself possible. Therefore, vision is possible. The objection claims that the condition was actually impossible because at the moment of the speech, the mountain was moving, and settling a moving thing is impossible. The objector argues that the particle 'in' (if) when applied to a past tense verb refers to the future, so 'if it settles' means 'if it becomes settled in the future'. Since vision did not occur (by consensus the mountain did not settle), the mountain must have remained moving, making the condition impossible. The answer distinguishes between the mountain's essence and its temporary state: the verse mentions only the mountain as such, which can be stationary; the impossibility of stillness due to motion is not mentioned in the verse. Thus, the condition remains possible, and the proof stands.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition in the verse was literally impossible, so the proof fails.
Correction: Razi's answer shows that the condition refers to the mountain's essence, which can be still, not its accidental motion, so the condition is possible.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that the mountain's settling was impossible because it was moving?
A) He argues that the mountain could have been stationary at the time of speech.
B) He concedes motion but notes the verse refers to the mountain's essence which can be still.
C) He says the condition was a test and not meant to be fulfilled.
D) He rejects that the mountain was moving.

Key Takeaway
Razi's answer saves the proof by distinguishing between the mountain's essence, which can be still, and its accidental motion, which was not mentioned in the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty that seems impossible, do you focus on the temporary circumstances (like the mountain's motion) or on the essential promise of Allah? Which perspective do you choose today?
?
Allah's condition in the verse was possible in essence, even though it was blocked temporarily. Is there an area in your life where you have given up on something Allah has made possible, because you looked at the present obstacle rather than the essential capacity? What would it take for you to revive that hope and act on it?
?
The objector argued that because the mountain was moving, settling was impossible. Razi replied by distinguishing essence from accident. In your relationship with Allah, have you ever let a temporary state (a sin, a hardship, a doubt) convince you that His mercy or guidance was impossible for you? Will you now separate who you are in essence from the accidents of your situation and return to Him?

Opponent's Arguments Against Ru'ya and Razi's First Answer
Razi presents three opponent arguments and answers the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's three arguments against the possibility of seeing Allah and how Razi answers the first by distinguishing idrak from ru'ya.

Core Idea
The verse 'No vision can grasp Him' only negates comprehensive grasping, not simple vision.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the arguments of those who deny that Allah can be seen, focusing on the Quranic verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'. He then provides a nuanced answer to the first argument by distinguishing between two terms: idrak (encompassing perception) and ru'ya (simple vision). This is a classic example of kalam dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how Razi structures the opponent's case.
The passage has three opponent arguments followed by an answer to the first.
2
Identify the three arguments
The opponent makes three distinct arguments. Find the second and third arguments and note their logic.
The first argument has two sub-parts: context of praise and universal negation.
3
Understand Razi's answer
Razi only answers the first argument here. Pay attention to how he distinguishes idrak from ru'ya.
He affirms the verse but redefines 'idrak' to mean comprehensive grasping.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for the Ash'ari position on seeing Allah in the afterlife.
This allows for vision without encompassing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم بأمور. أحدها: قوله تعالى: {لا تدركه الأبصار}، والاستدلال به من وجهين: الأول: أن ما قبل هذه الآية وما بعدها مذكور في معرض المدح فوجب أن تكون هذه الآية مدحا؛ فإن إلقاء ما ليس بمدح فيما بين المدحين ركيك، كما يقال: فلان أجل الناس وآكل الخبز وأستاذ الوقت، وإذا كان في الإدراك مدحا كان ثبوته نقصا والنقص على الله محال. الثاني: أن قوله تعالى: {لا تدركه الأبصار} يقتضي أن لا تدركه الأبصار في شيء من الأوقات، لأننا إذا قلنا: تدركه الأبصار يناقض قولنا: لا تدركه الأبصار، بدليل أنه يستعمل كل واحد من القولين في تكذيب الآخر، وإذا صدق أحد النقيضين كذب الآخر، فوجب كذلك قولنا: لا تدركه الأبصار، وإذا ثبت ذلك ثبت كذب قولنا: يدركه بصر واحد أو بصران ضرورة أن لا قائل بالفرق. وثانيها: أنه تعالى لو كان مرئيا لرأيناه الآن. وثالثها: أنه لو كان مرئيا لكان مقابلا أو في حكم المقابل، وقولنا في حكم المقابل احتراز عن رؤية الإنسان وجهه في المرآة وعن رؤية الأعراض. والجواب عن الأول: أنا نقول بموجب الآية لأن الإدراك هو رؤية الشيء من جميع جوانبه لأن أصله من الدرك وهو اللحوق والإحاطة، والرؤية لا تستلزم الإحاطة، فلا تدل الآية على نفي الرؤية.
The opponent argued with several things. First: His statement the Exalted: 'No vision can grasp Him.' The deduction from it is in two ways: First: that what comes before and after this verse is mentioned in the context of praise, so this verse must be praise; for placing what is not praise between two praises is awkward, as one says 'So-and-so is the noblest of people, and he eats bread, and he is the master of the time'—and if non-grasping is praise, then grasping would be a deficiency, and deficiency upon Allah is impossible. Second: that His statement 'Vision grasps Him not' requires that vision never grasps Him at any time, because if we say 'vision grasps Him' it contradicts our saying 'vision grasps Him not', as evidenced that each of the two statements is used to falsify the other, and if one of the two contradictories is true, the other is false; so likewise our saying 'vision grasps Him not' is true; and if that is established, then the falsity of our saying 'one sight grasps Him' or 'two sights grasp Him' is established, necessarily, because no one holds a distinction. Second: that if He were visible, we would see Him now. Third: that if He were visible, He would be either facing us or in the ruling of facing, and our saying 'in the ruling of facing' is to avoid [the case of] a person seeing his face in a mirror and the seeing of accidents. The answer to the first: we say the verse is acted upon, because 'idrak' is seeing a thing from all its sides, because its root is from 'daraka' meaning overtaking and encompassing, and vision does not necessitate encompassing, so the verse does not indicate the negation of vision.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's arguments against the possibility of seeing Allah, followed by Razi's answer to the first argument. The opponent raises three proofs: (1) the Quranic verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (No vision can grasp Him). The opponent derives two sub-arguments: (a) the context of the verse is praise, so not being grasped is a perfection; if grasping were possible, it would be a deficiency, which is impossible for Allah. (b) The verse is universal negative, so it negates all instances of grasping; if any instance of vision were true, it would contradict the verse. (2) If Allah were visible, we would see Him now (since there is no obstacle). (3) If visible, He would have to be facing the viewer or in the ruling of facing (like a reflection), which is impossible. Razi answers only the first argument: he affirms the verse but distinguishes 'idrak' from mere 'ru'ya'. 'Idrak' means comprehensive perception from all sides, which is impossible for Allah. But simple vision (ru'ya) does not require such encompassment, so the verse does not negate it. The other two objections are left unanswered in this passage.

Key Terms
الإدراك
الأبصار
المدح
النقيضين
مقابلا
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'No vision can grasp Him' proves that Allah cannot be seen at all.
Correction: Razi shows that 'idrak' means comprehensive grasping, not simple vision, so the verse does not negate vision.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the opponent's argument from the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'?
A) He says the verse is abrogated.
B) He distinguishes 'idrak' (comprehensive grasping) from mere 'ru'ya' (vision).
C) He argues the verse refers only to this world.
D) He claims the verse is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Razi preserves the verse's meaning while opening the door for seeing Allah by distinguishing idrak from ru'ya.

Razi says that the verse only negates comprehensive grasping, not simple vision. In your prayers and worship, do you sometimes try to 'grasp' Allah's essence or demand full understanding? What one thing will you do this week to practice contented humility before the incomprehensible Majesty of Allah?
?
The opponent argued that non-grasping is praise, so grasping would be deficiency. Reflect on a time when you thought that complete understanding of a religious matter was a sign of spiritual strength. How does this lesson teach you that admitting you cannot fully know Allah is actually a sign of true servitude? Will you allow yourself to be comfortable with not having all the answers?

Razi distinguishes between idrak and ru'ya—two concepts that are often confused. The Distinction Between Worldly and Divine Vision
al-Razi responds to the second objection by denying the analogy between worldly and divine vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that the conditions for seeing physical objects do not apply to seeing God.

Core Idea
Divine vision is fundamentally different from worldly sight.

Al-Razi now addresses the second objection against the beatific vision. Opponents argued that vision requires certain conditions like the absence of obstruction, which cannot apply to God. In his response, al-Razi makes a crucial distinction: the rules for seeing created things do not automatically transfer to seeing the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the response to the second objection. Notice how al-Razi concedes the condition but denies its transfer.
2
Identify the opponent's move
The opponent says: vision requires conditions, God cannot meet them, so God cannot be seen. Find where al-Razi grants the condition.
3
Understand the distinction
Al-Razi says the vision of creatures is different from the vision of God. What does that imply about the analogy?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثاني : أنا بينا أن عند حضور المرئي وحصول الشرائط لا تحجب الرؤية ، سلمنا وجوبها في المرئيات التي في الشاهد دفعا للتشنيعات التي يذكرونها فلم قلت إنها واجبة في رؤية الصانع ، وإن رؤية المخلوقات مخالفة لرؤية الله تعالى، ولا يلزم من وجوب حصول رؤية المخلوقات عند حضور الشرائط وجوب رؤية الله تعالى عند حضور الشرائط
وعن الثالث : أن قولهم المرئي يجب أن يكون مقابلا أو في حكم المقابل عين المتنازع أو تقول ثبت أنه يجب أن يكون كذلك في الشاهد ، فلم قلت إنه يجب أن يكون كذلك في الغائب وتقريره ما ذكرناه الآن
As for the second: We have shown that when the seen object is present and conditions are fulfilled, vision is not obstructed. We grant its necessity in visible things in the witnessed realm to rebut their criticisms, but why do you say it is necessary in seeing the Maker? And the vision of creatures is different from the vision of God, and the necessity of vision of creatures when conditions are present does not imply the necessity of vision of God when conditions are present.
As for the third: Their statement that the seen must be facing or in the ruling of facing is the very point of dispute, or you say it is established that it must be so in the witnessed realm, so why do you say it must be so in the absent? And its clarification is what we mentioned now.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer sequence. Al-Razi continues responding to arguments of those who deny the beatific vision. The second objection claimed that vision requires special conditions (like absence of obstruction) that cannot apply to God. Al-Razi admits the conditions in worldly sight but denies they transfer to the divine case. He points out that the vision of God is a different kind of vision, so the analogy fails. The third objection said a visible object must be facing the viewer. Al-Razi calls this the very point of contention; the opponent assumes what needs proof. He insists that the rule in the witnessed world does not necessarily apply to God.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الشرائط
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if worldly vision requires conditions, then divine vision must also require them.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the vision of God is different in kind, so the analogy is invalid.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi concede in his response to the second objection?
A) That God cannot be seen.
B) That vision of creatures requires conditions.
C) That conditions are the same for divine vision.
D) That the objection is valid.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi rejects the analogy between worldly and divine vision, insisting that the conditions for seeing physical objects do not transfer to seeing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have never seen God, yet He sees you at every moment. Al-Razi insists that God's vision is utterly different from human sight. ?
Al-Razi argues that the necessity of conditions for seeing creatures does not apply to seeing God. Have you ever caught yourself imagining God based on your experience of seeing people? In what specific ways might that be a subtle form of tashbih (likening God to creation) that you need to repent from and correct in your understanding?
?
If the necessity of divine vision does not depend on any physical conditions, then God can be seen in the afterlife without the limitations of this world. Does this hope move you to purify your heart today? What is one thing you can do right now to prepare for that vision, knowing that it is not earned by merit but given by grace?

The Fallacy of Requiring Facing for Divine Vision
al-Razi responds to the third objection by calling the premise disputed and denying transfer from witnessed to absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's critique of the claim that the seen object must be facing the viewer.

Core Idea
The requirement of facing is disputed; it cannot be assumed for God.

The third objection claimed that a visible object must be facing the observer (muqabila) or in the ruling of facing. Al-Razi points out that this is exactly the point in contention; the opponent is assuming what needs to be proven. He argues that even if facing is required in the witnessed world, it does not necessarily apply to the unseen (gha'ib).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

2
Identify the opponent's premise
The opponent says: the seen must be facing. Al-Razi says this is the disputed point.
3
Understand the argument
Al-Razi uses the same distinction between witnessed and absent: what holds in the witnessed world may not hold for God.

Denial of Vision to God: A Clarifying Gloss
A marginal note clarifying the correct meaning of God's denial of visual perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of God's denial of visual perception according to this gloss, distinguishing between perception requiring physical means and direct vision affirmed in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
God denied perception that requires physical processes, not direct vision.

This gloss addresses a potential misunderstanding about the denial of vision to Allah. The author argues that the denial itself is a form of praise, because perception — in the sense of full encompassing — implies limitation. He then refutes an incorrect interpretation and provides an accurate explanation that preserves both God's transcendence and the possibility of seeing Him in the afterlife without physical means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three parts: the initial claim, the refutation, and the correct answer.
Pay attention to the technical terms: الإدراك, الإبصار, ارتسام الشبح, خروج الشعاع.
2
Understand why denial is praise
The author says denial of perception is praise because perception here implies deficiency. Ask yourself: why would perception be a deficiency? Consider that fully encompassing something requires limitation.
This is a kalām argument about divine transcendence.
3
Analyze the refuted interpretation
The gloss refutes the idea that idrak means seeing something from all sides. The counterexample of perceiving the sun or fire shows that idrak does not require full encirclement.
This step guards against a common misinterpretation of the word idrak.
4
Grasp the correct distinction
The correct answer: God denied visual perception that requires physical conditions (imprint of image or emission of rays). But the state of being seen after these conditions occur is not denied. This allows for the possibility of seeing Allah without physical means.
This distinction is crucial in Sunni theology regarding ru'ya (vision of Allah in the afterlife).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : نفي الإدراك عنه تعالى مدح فالإدراك نقص ليس بشيء لأن المدح يكون له نفي الإدراك البصري فالنقص يكون هو الإدراك ، والله تعالى منزه عن ذلك بالاتفاق . وقوله : إدراك الشيء بمعنى الإبصار رؤيته من جميع جوانبه ليس بصحيح، لأنهم يقولون أدرك الشمس والنار ولم يريدوا به إدراكهما من جميع جوانبهما . والجواب الصحيح : أنه تعالى نفي الإدراك بالأبصار الذي من شرطه ارتسام الشبح أو خروج الشعاع وأما الحالة التي تحصل بعد حصول أحد هذين الشيئين من غير حصول أحدهما فلم ينفه
I say: The denial of perception from Him is praise, for perception is a deficiency—nothing—because praise is for Him through the denial of visual perception, so the deficiency is the perception itself, and God is exalted above that by consensus. And his statement: 'Perception of a thing in the sense of sight means seeing it from all its sides' is not correct, because they say 'he perceived the sun and fire' and do not mean thereby perceiving them from all their sides. And the correct answer: He, exalted, denied perception through the eyes whose condition is the imprint of the image or the emission of rays; as for the state that occurs after the occurrence of one of these two things without the occurrence of the other, He did not deny it.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on the interpretation of the verse or hadith that denies divine vision. The author (likely a commentator) argues that God's denial of al-idrak (perception) is praise, because idrak in the sense of full encompassing from all sides is a deficiency—only finite objects are perceived entirely. He refutes the claim that idrak means seeing from all sides, giving counterexamples (sun, fire) that are perceived without full encirclement. He concludes that the correct meaning is that God denied visual perception that requires physical processes like the imprint of the image or the emission of rays, but He did not deny the state of being seen that occurs after these processes without their actual occurrence (i.e., direct vision without intermediaries).

Key Terms
الإدراك
الإبصار
ارتسام الشبح
خروج الشعاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying idrak means Allah cannot be seen at all, even in the afterlife.
Correction: The gloss clarifies that only the kind of perception that requires physical processes (imprint of image or emission of rays) is denied; the actual vision of Allah (ru'ya) without physical means is not denied.

Checkpoint
What is the 'correct answer' given in the gloss regarding God's denial of perception?
A) God denied all forms of seeing, including direct vision.
B) God denied perception that requires imprint of the image or emission of rays, not the state of being seen after those conditions.
C) God denied perception because it implies deficiency in the perceiver.
D) The denial is only figurative.

Key Takeaway
God denied only visual perception that requires physical processes like imprint of image or emission of rays, not the direct seeing of Him affirmed in Sunni belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God denied physical perception of Himself, how do you reconcile this with your desire to 'feel' or 'sense' His closeness? Are you willing to believe His unseen reality is more real than what your senses can grasp?
?
When you pray, do you imagine God as somehow limited by your own senses? How can you train yourself to worship a God who is beyond all physical perception, yet closer to you than your jugular vein?
?
You claim to believe that you will see God in the afterlife—a vision without physical means. Is your life today governed by that unseen reality, or do you live only for what your eyes can see now? What needs to change so that your choices reflect that certain hope?

Objection and Reply on Two Wise Gods
Imam Razi replies to the objection that two wise gods would agree on the best action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that two wise gods must agree on the best action and Imam Razi's twofold reply that shows how disagreement is possible.

Core Idea
Two wise gods need not agree; their actions can differ via motives or indifference.

Imam Razi presents a common objection raised by those who argue that if there were two wise gods, they would both will the same best action, so no disagreement could occur. In response, he offers a nuanced argument distinguishing between acts that depend on a motive and those that do not. This lesson unpacks that reply and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a twofold reply.
The reply is divided into two scenarios based on whether an act depends on a motive.
2
Analyze the first scenario
Consider the case where an act depends on a motive. Understand that if God creates a motive for a bad act, the act of creating that motive is not necessarily bad according to the opponent's framework.
This shows that disagreement in motives can lead to different actions.
3
Examine the second scenario
Now consider the case where an act does not depend on a motive. In that situation, two gods can choose opposite actions of equal value, so disagreement is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال أيهما لكونهما حكيمين لا يريد وذلك الأصلح واحد فلا جرم يجب توافقهما. قلنا : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الداعى أولا يتوقف، فإن توقف على الداعى لا مجال من العبد أن يختار الفعل القبيح ألا إذا خلق الله فيه داعيا يدعوه إليه ، وإذا كان الداعي إلى القبيح موجبا للقبح كان قبحا ، وإذا كان الفاعل لذلك الداعى هو الله تعالى لم يجب أن يكون فعل الله حسنا بالتفسير الذي تريدونه ، فلم يلزم اتفاق الإلهين على الفعل الواحد فصحت المخالفة بينهما ، وإن لم يتوقف الفعل الداعى جاز في الضدين المتساويين في الحسن و القبح أن يختار أحد الإلهين إيجاد أحدهما والإله الآخر إيجاد الآخر وحينئذ تحصل المخالفة بين هما (1) أقول : قد مر امتناع وجود واجب الوجود لذا تهما وذلك يكفي في إثبات هذا المطلوب ، وأما هذا الدليل فيدل عن امتناع كون الإلهين متساويين من كل الوجوه ولا يدل على امتناع كون آلهة مترتبة يقدر العالي منها يمنع السافل مما يريده من غير عكس، ومذهب أكثر المشركين هو هذا وقوله فعل خالق الداعى إلى القبيح لا يكون حسنا ليس بشيء ، لأن القائل بالحسن و القبح لا يسلم أن خلق ما هو موجب للقبيح قبيح، فإن خلق الكفار مع قدرهم ودواعيهم خلق ما يوجب الكفر وذلك غير قبيح عندهم وذلك لأن وجوب الكفر عن القدرة والداعى معا لا ينافي الاختيار ، وإذا كان الخلوق مختارا لم يتأد قبح فعله إلى فاعله ، وباقى الكلام ظاهر ، وقد يمكن أن يتبين هذه المسألة بالسمع، لأن صحة السمع غير موقوف على القول بوتحدة الإله .
If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the two of them, because they are both wise, would not will except that which is the more beneficial (al-aslah), so necessarily they must agree? We say: The act either depends on a motive (al-da'i) or does not depend. If it depends on a motive, then the servant has no way to choose a bad act unless God creates in him a motive that calls him to it. And when the motive to the bad necessitates badness, it is bad. And when the agent of that motive is God Most High, it is not necessary that God's act be good according to the interpretation you intend. Thus agreement of the two gods on a single act is not necessitated, and disagreement between them is valid. And if the act does not depend on a motive, then regarding two opposites equal in goodness and badness, it is permissible that one of the gods choose to bring about one of them and the other god bring about the other, and then disagreement occurs between them. (1) I say: The impossibility of the existence of two necessary existents has already passed, and that suffices to prove this desired conclusion. As for this proof, it indicates the impossibility of two gods being equal in all respects, but it does not indicate the impossibility of there being a hierarchy of gods, where the higher has power to prevent the lower from what it wills, without the reverse. And this is the doctrine of most polytheists. His statement that the action of the creator of the motive to the bad is not good is worthless, because the upholder of good and bad (al-husn wa al-qubh) does not concede that creating what necessitates bad is bad. Indeed, creating disbelievers along with their power and motives is creating what necessitates disbelief, and that is not bad in their view, because the necessitation of disbelief from power and motive together does not negate choice. And when the created being is choosing, the badness of his act does not transfer to his agent. The rest of the discussion is clear. And this issue can possibly be demonstrated through transmitted evidence (al-sam'), because the validity of transmitted evidence is not dependent on the assertion of the oneness of God.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its reply regarding the possibility of two gods disagreeing. The objector argues that two wise gods would always choose the best action (al-aslah), so they must agree. The reply is twofold: (1) If acts depend on motives, then God creates the motive; the motive to a bad act is itself bad, but the objector's framework does not require God's act to be good, so disagreement can occur. (2) If acts do not depend on motives, then gods can choose opposite actions on equal grounds. The marginal note adds that the argument only disproves fully equal gods, not a hierarchy, and critiques the opponent's notion of good and bad.

Key Terms
الداعى
الأصلح
الحسن و القبح
الإلهين
المخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's theory of good and bad forces God's acts to be good, so creating a motive to bad would be bad.
Correction: Razi argues that the opponent's definition of goodness does not necessarily apply to the act of creating a motive, so the objection is not valid.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's reply, what is one scenario that allows two gods to disagree?
A) They always will the same best action.
B) When acts depend on motives, agreement is necessary.
C) When acts do not depend on motives, they can choose opposite acts.
D) They cannot disagree because God's actions are always good.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi shows that even if two wise gods exist, disagreement is logically possible through either dependence or independence of acts on motives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If two wise gods could disagree on what is best, how does that affect your confidence that God always does what is best for you? Do you truly believe that God's choices for you are the most beneficial, even when you cannot see the wisdom? Reflect on a situation where you struggled with God's decree and ask yourself: is it possible that my limited understanding of 'best' is the real issue?
?
The objection assumes that wisdom leads to agreement, but Razi shows disagreement can still be wise. In your own life, have you ever assumed that being wise means everyone would agree with you? How does this lesson correct that assumption and call you to humility in your judgments of others?
?
Razi's argument splits into two cases: acts that depend on motives and acts that don't. Which category describes your own actions more often? When you act, do you examine your motives, or do you act without clear reason? How does this lesson challenge you to be more intentional about the motives behind your choices, especially in matters of obedience to God?

Limits of the Proof: Hierarchical Gods and Critique of Good and Bad
Imam Razi critiques his own earlier proof and discusses hierarchical gods and the nature of good and bad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Razi's proof only disproves equal gods, not hierarchical gods, and also understand his critique of the opponent's concept of good and bad.

Core Idea
The proof only rejects equal gods; hierarchical polytheism remains possible in reason.

After presenting the main reply, Imam Razi adds a critical note that clarifies the limits of the argument. He observes that the reasoning only shows that two gods cannot be equal in all respects, but it does not rule out a hierarchy of gods, which is the actual belief of most polytheists. He also critiques the opponent's notion that creating a motive to a bad act is itself bad.

Guided Path
1
Read the marginal note
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. This note begins with 'I say' and contains two main points.
The note is a self-critique by Imam Razi, limiting the scope of the preceding proof.
2
Identify the limitation
Notice that Imam Razi states that the proof only shows impossibility of two equal gods, not of hierarchical gods. This is important because most polytheists believe in a hierarchy.
He refers to a previous proof that one necessary existent cannot be two.
3
Understand the critique of good and bad
Imam Razi argues that the opponent's claim—'creating a motive to a bad act is bad'—is not conceded by those who affirm objective good and bad, because the badness lies in the human's choice, not the creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال أيهما لكونهما حكيمين لا يريد وذلك الأصلح واحد فلا جرم يجب توافقهما. قلنا : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الداعى أولا يتوقف، فإن توقف على الداعى لا مجال من العبد أن يختار الفعل القبيح ألا إذا خلق الله فيه داعيا يدعوه إليه ، وإذا كان الداعي إلى القبيح موجبا للقبح كان قبحا ، وإذا كان الفاعل لذلك الداعى هو الله تعالى لم يجب أن يكون فعل الله حسنا بالتفسير الذي تريدونه ، فلم يلزم اتفاق الإلهين على الفعل الواحد فصحت المخالفة بينهما ، وإن لم يتوقف الفعل الداعى جاز في الضدين المتساويين في الحسن و القبح أن يختار أحد الإلهين إيجاد أحدهما والإله الآخر إيجاد الآخر وحينئذ تحصل المخالفة بين هما (1) أقول : قد مر امتناع وجود واجب الوجود لذا تهما وذلك يكفي في إثبات هذا المطلوب ، وأما هذا الدليل فيدل عن امتناع كون الإلهين متساويين من كل الوجوه ولا يدل على امتناع كون آلهة مترتبة يقدر العالي منها يمنع السافل مما يريده من غير عكس، ومذهب أكثر المشركين هو هذا وقوله فعل خالق الداعى إلى القبيح لا يكون حسنا ليس بشيء ، لأن القائل بالحسن و القبح لا يسلم أن خلق ما هو موجب للقبيح قبيح، فإن خلق الكفار مع قدرهم ودواعيهم خلق ما يوجب الكفر وذلك غير قبيح عندهم وذلك لأن وجوب الكفر عن القدرة والداعى معا لا ينافي الاختيار ، وإذا كان الخلوق مختارا لم يتأد قبح فعله إلى فاعله ، وباقى الكلام ظاهر ، وقد يمكن أن يتبين هذه المسألة بالسمع، لأن صحة السمع غير موقوف على القول بوتحدة الإله .
If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the two of them, because they are both wise, would not will except that which is the more beneficial (al-aslah), so necessarily they must agree? We say: The act either depends on a motive (al-da'i) or does not depend. If it depends on a motive, then the servant has no way to choose a bad act unless God creates in him a motive that calls him to it. And when the motive to the bad necessitates badness, it is bad. And when the agent of that motive is God Most High, it is not necessary that God's act be good according to the interpretation you intend. Thus agreement of the two gods on a single act is not necessitated, and disagreement between them is valid. And if the act does not depend on a motive, then regarding two opposites equal in goodness and badness, it is permissible that one of the gods choose to bring about one of them and the other god bring about the other, and then disagreement occurs between them. (1) I say: The impossibility of the existence of two necessary existents has already passed, and that suffices to prove this desired conclusion. As for this proof, it indicates the impossibility of two gods being equal in all respects, but it does not indicate the impossibility of there being a hierarchy of gods, where the higher has power to prevent the lower from what it wills, without the reverse. And this is the doctrine of most polytheists. His statement that the action of the creator of the motive to the bad is not good is worthless, because the upholder of good and bad (al-husn wa al-qubh) does not concede that creating what necessitates bad is bad. Indeed, creating disbelievers along with their power and motives is creating what necessitates disbelief, and that is not bad in their view, because the necessitation of disbelief from power and motive together does not negate choice. And when the created being is choosing, the badness of his act does not transfer to his agent. The rest of the discussion is clear. And this issue can possibly be demonstrated through transmitted evidence (al-sam'), because the validity of transmitted evidence is not dependent on the assertion of the oneness of God.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its reply regarding the possibility of two gods disagreeing. The objector argues that two wise gods would always choose the best action (al-aslah), so they must agree. The reply is twofold: (1) If acts depend on motives, then God creates the motive; the motive to a bad act is itself bad, but the objector's framework does not require God's act to be good, so disagreement can occur. (2) If acts do not depend on motives, then gods can choose opposite actions on equal grounds. The marginal note adds that the argument only disproves fully equal gods, not a hierarchy, and critiques the opponent's notion of good and bad.

Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that it only disproves equal gods, not hierarchical gods, which is the actual belief of polytheists.

What is the main limitation of the earlier proof according to Imam Razi?
A) It proves that no gods can exist.
B) It only disproves hierarchical gods.

?

?

Are there areas in your life where you unjustly attribute bad outcomes to others while excusing your own choices? How can you take more responsibility?
?
Razi acknowledges that the proof he presented is limited and that hierarchical polytheism remains rationally possible. Does this shake your certainty in the oneness of God, or does it drive you to seek stronger evidence? What steps will you take to ground your belief in both reason and transmitted truth?

Third Argument: Two Conflicting Wills
Imam Razi presents the third argument about conflicting divine and human wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's third argument that human will cannot oppose divine will because two equal independent powers cannot produce contradictory effects on the same object without an external preference.

Core Idea
Two equal independent powers cannot prefer a single outcome.

Imam Razi presents a logical argument to refute the idea that human will could effectively oppose God's will. He sets up a scenario where the servant wills one thing and God wills the opposite, and shows that no outcome is logically consistent unless one power is superior—which would contradict their assumed equality. This passage is a classic Kalam proof for the Ash'ari position that all acts are created by God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Read the scenario: the servant wills to settle a body while God wills to move it. Identify the two opposing wills.
The body is the single object of both wills.
2
Consider the logical outcomes
The passage presents two possibilities: both occur together (impossible because contradictory) or only one occurs. Examine why both occurring is impossible.
Contradictory effects cannot coexist in a real unity.
3
Analyze the equality assumption
Razi assumes the two powers are equal in independence of influence on that single object. Understand why this equality makes one occurring without the other arbitrary.
Without an external factor, no preference can be justified.
4
Conclude the impossibility of preference
Since the powers are equal, no inherent reason gives one effect priority. Therefore, preference (tarjīḥ) is impossible without an external factor.
This leads to the theological conclusion that human will cannot overcome divine will.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this argument supports the Ash'ari position that all human acts are ultimately created by God. Think about its significance in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إذا أراد العبد تسكين الجسم أو أراد الله تحريكه فإما أن لا يقعا معا وهو محال، أو يقع أحدهما دون الآخر وهو باطل، لأن القدرتين متساويتان في الاستقلال بالتأثير في ذلك المقدور الواحد والشيء الواحد وحدة حقيقية لا يقبل التفاوت، فإذا القدرتان بالنسبة إلى اقتضاء وجود هذا المقدور على السواء، إنما التفاوت في أمور أخر خارجة عن هذا المعنى، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع الترجيح.
Third: If the servant wills to settle a body or God wills to move it, then either both do not occur—which is impossible—or one occurs without the other—which is false. Because the two powers are equal in independence of influence on that one thing capable of being an object of power, and the one thing is a real unity that does not admit of variation. So the two powers are equal with respect to requiring the existence of this object of power; difference is only in other matters external to this meaning. And if that is the case, preference (tarjīḥ) is impossible.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a proof (دليل) that human power cannot effectively oppose divine power. The scenario: the servant wills to settle a body (e.g., stop it) while God wills to move it. If both occur together, the body would be simultaneously moving and still—impossible. If only one occurs, then the other will must be ineffective. But if the two powers are considered equal in their independence to produce the effect on that single object, then neither can overcome the other without some external factor—yet none is present in the pure scenario. Thus, no preferability (ترجیح) can be determined, implying that the human will cannot truly oppose the divine will. This supports the Ash'ari position that human acts are ultimately created by God.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدرة
استقلال بالتأثير
مقدور واحد
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two powers are equal and independent, they can both produce their effects on the same object simultaneously.
Correction: Razi argues that on a single real object, contradictory effects cannot coexist; thus both cannot occur. Without an external preference, neither can prevail alone either.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that if the servant wills to settle a body and God wills to move it, the occurrence of only one is false?
A) Because the two powers are equal and independent, so no preference is possible without an external factor.
B) Because God's power is obviously greater.
C) Because the body cannot be both settled and moved.
D) Because the servant's will is always ineffective.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that two equal and independent powers cannot produce contradictory effects on a single object, thereby showing that human will cannot oppose divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something and it doesn't happen, do you feel frustrated or accept that God's will is supreme? How does this argument help you surrender your desires to His decree?
?
Think of a time you strongly resisted God's decree in your heart. What would it look like to fully submit, knowing that your will cannot truly oppose His?
?
This argument shows the logical impossibility of opposing God's will. Is there an area in your life where you still act as if you can override His plan? What step can you take today to align your will with His?

The Opponent's Rational Argument and Its Response
Imam Razi presents the Mu'tazila's rational argument and his response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's rational argument from human moral responsibility and Imam Razi's universal-dilemma response.

Core Idea
The problem of determinism is universal, not unique to any school.

This passage presents a classic theological debate about human agency and divine creation. The Mu'tazila argue that if God creates all actions, human responsibility becomes impossible. Imam Razi responds by showing that the same dilemma applies to the Mu'tazila's own framework, leading to the conclusion that God's actions are beyond questioning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the argument and counterargument.
2
Analyze the Mu'tazila argument
Identify the premises: if God creates actions, the agent cannot act; thus actions become like inanimate movements; commands become absurd; therefore humans must create their actions.
The term 'badīha' (intuition) is central here.
3
Understand the response
Razi's response has two parts: first, the dilemma of divine command (equal vs. preponderant inclination); second, the problem of divine foreknowledge. Both show determinism applies to all.
4
Reflect on the final answer
The concluding statement 'God is not questioned' grounds the Ash'ari position in divine sovereignty, not human logic.
Consider how this reply resolves the earlier dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم بالمعقول والمنقول. أما المعقول فهو أن فعل العبد لو كان يخلق الله تعالى لما كان متمكنا من الفعل ألبتة، لأنه إن خلقه الله تعالى فيه كان واجب الحصول، وإن لم يخلق الله تعالى فيه كان ممتنع الحصول، ولو لم يكن العبد متمكنا من الفعل والترك لكانت أفعاله جارية مجرى حركات الجمادات، وكما أن البديهة جازمة بأنه لا يجوز أمر الجماد ونهيه ومدحه وذمه وجب أن يكون الأمر كذلك في أفعال العباد، ولما كان ذلك باطلا علمنا كون العبد موجدا. والجواب: أنه لازم عليكم، لأن الأمر إن توجه حال استواء الداعي ففي تلك الحال امتنع الترجيح، وإن توجه حال الرجحان فهناك الراجح واجب والمرجوح ممتنع، ولأن ذلك الفعل إن علم الله وجوده فهو واجب، وإن علم الله عدمه فهو ممتنع، فثبت أن الإشكال وارد على الكل، وإن الجواب هو أن الله تعالى لا يسأل عما يفعل.
The opponent argued by reason and transmitted scripture. As for reason, it is that if the servant's action were created by God Almighty, then he would never be able to perform the action at all. Because if God created it in him, it would be necessary of occurrence; and if God did not create it in him, it would be impossible of occurrence. And if the servant were unable to perform or abandon, his actions would be like the movements of inanimate objects. And just as intuition is certain that commanding and forbidding, praising and blaming an inanimate object are not permissible, so likewise it must be the case regarding the actions of servants. And since that is false, we know that the servant is a creator. The answer: That follows necessarily upon you. Because if the command is addressed during the state of equal inclination, then in that state preference (tarjīḥ) is impossible; and if addressed during the state of preponderance, then there the preponderant is necessary and the outweighed is impossible. And because that action—if God knows its existence, then it is necessary; and if God knows its non-existence, then it is impossible. So it is established that the problem applies to everyone. And the answer is that God Almighty is not questioned about what He does.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response (احتجاج وجواب). The Mu'tazila argue that if God creates all human actions, humans would have no real ability; actions would be involuntary like those of inanimate objects, making commands and moral judgments absurd. Therefore, humans must be the creators. Imam Razi responds (والجواب) by pointing out that the same problem arises for the Mu'tazila themselves: divine command presupposes either equal or preponderant inclination—in both cases, the outcome is predetermined. Also, God's foreknowledge necessitates either existence or non-existence. Thus, the difficulty is universal. The final answer is that God is not accountable for His actions (لا يسأل عما يفعل).

Key Terms
خالق
خلق
متمكن
واجب الحصول
ممتنع الحصول
بديهة
أمر ونهي
استواء الداعي
ترجيح
علم الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The problem of determinism only affects those who believe God creates all actions.
Correction: Razi shows the same dilemma arises for any view that includes divine command and foreknowledge, regardless of human creative power.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's response, what conclusion follows from both the case of equal inclination and preponderant inclination?
A) The human agent has free will.
B) The outcome is predetermined.
C) God does not know the future.
D) The Mu'tazila argument is correct.

Key Takeaway
The problem of determinism is universal because divine command and foreknowledge entail necessity; the Ash'ari response is that God's actions are beyond question.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often feel that you have the power to choose between right and wrong. But Razi argues that even your feeling of ability is part of God's creation, and that ultimately every outcome is determined by God's knowledge and will. Does this make you feel liberated or burdened? How will you respond the next time you face a moral dilemma—will you turn to God in prayer, acknowledging your complete dependence on Him, or will you rely on your own sense of agency?
?
Razi concludes that 'God is not questioned about what He does.' This is a hard saying for those who want to understand every divine decree. Is there an event in your life—a loss, a failure, a trial—that you have struggled to accept because you thought you could explain it? Will you now trust God's wisdom even when you cannot see the reason, knowing that His actions are beyond human questioning?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that if God creates all actions, then commands and moral judgments become meaningless. Yet Razi shows that the same difficulty applies to everyone. How does this affect your personal accountability before God? Do you sometimes use the excuse that 'everything is from God' to avoid taking responsibility for your sins? What would it mean for you to fully embrace both God's sovereignty and your own moral responsibility, without falling into fatalism or presumption?

The First Proof: God's Will Covers All Creation
Razi gives the first proof that God wills all things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that God wills all things because He is their Creator, establishing the universal scope of divine will.

Core Idea
Because God creates everything, He wills everything.

Imam Razi opens this issue by stating the Ash'ari position that God wills all existing things, contrary to the Mu'tazila who limit God's will to good acts. His first proof is direct and logical: since God is the Creator of all things, and the Creator of a thing must will its existence, it follows that God's will encompasses all creation. This foundational argument sets the stage for further discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the issue statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage that declares the issue: 'That He wills all existing things, contrary to the Mu'tazila.' Notice the phrase 'مريد لجميع الكائنات'.
The Mu'tazila deny that God wills evil or acts of disobedience.
2
Identify the first proof
Look for the words: 'We have shown that He is their Creator, and the Creator of a thing wills its existence.' This is the core of the argument.
The proof relies on the previously established fact that God is the Creator of all things.
3
Reflect on the logic
Understand the implication: If God creates something, He must will its existence. Since God creates everything that exists, He wills everything, including acts of obedience and disobedience.
This argument directly challenges the Mu'tazila position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات خلافا للمعتزلة. لنا أنا بينا أنه تعالى خالقها وقد تقدم أن خالق الشيء مريد لوجوده، ولأنه لما علم أن الإيمان لا يوجد من الكافر كان وجوده من الكافر محالا كما ظهر فيكون الله تعالى عالما بكونه محالا، والعالم بكون الشيء محالا لا يريده فيستحيل أن يريد الإيمان من الكافر. استجوا [؟] : أنه أمر الكافر بالإيمان والأمر يدل على الإرادة. وثانيها: أن الطاعة موافقة الإرادة فلو أراد الله تعالى كفر الكافر المكان [؟] الكافر مطيعاله [؟] بكفره. وأما جواب المعتزلة عن قوله تعالى ما علم الله وجوده فهو واجب وبوجوب الفعل عند ترجيح وامتناعه عند عدمه فقد مر الكلام فيه ولا وجه لإعادته. وقال أهل التحقيق في هذا الموضع ولا تفويض ولكن أمر بين أمرين، فهذا هو الحق ومن لا يعرف حقيقته وقع في التحير.
Issue: That He, may He be exalted, wills all existing things, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For us: We have shown that He is their Creator, and it has preceded that the Creator of a thing wills its existence. And because when He knew that faith does not occur from the disbeliever, its occurrence from the disbeliever was impossible, as appeared; so God, may He be exalted, knows it is impossible, and one who knows a thing is impossible does not will it, so it is impossible that He will faith from the disbeliever. Their argument: That He commanded the disbeliever to believe, and command indicates will. Second: That obedience is conformity to will; so if God willed the disbeliever's disbelief, then the disbeliever would be obedient to Him through his disbelief. As for the Mu'tazila's answer to His statement: 'What God knows will exist is necessary, and the necessity of the act upon the preponderance of the motive and its impossibility upon its absence'—the discussion has passed on it, and there is no point in repeating it. And the people of verification said in this place: Neither delegation nor compulsion, but a matter between two matters. This is the truth, and whoever does not know its reality falls into confusion.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents a new issue: that God wills all created things, against the Mu'tazila who hold that God does not will evil or acts of disobedience. The author gives two proofs: (1) God is the Creator of all things, and the Creator of a thing necessarily wills its existence. (2) God knows that faith will never occur from a disbeliever; therefore its occurrence is impossible, and God cannot will what is impossible, so He does not will faith from the disbeliever. The author then mentions two objections from the Mu'tazila: (a) God commanded the disbeliever to believe, and command indicates will, so God must will faith. (b) Obedience is conformity to God's will; if God willed the disbeliever's disbelief, then the disbeliever would be obedient through that disbelief. The author dismisses these by referring to earlier discussions and concludes with the position of the people of verification: it is neither absolute delegation (tafwid) nor compulsion (jabr), but a matter between the two. This is the truth, and ignorance of it leads to confusion.

خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God does not will evil or sinful acts, as the Mu'tazila claim.
Correction: According to this proof, since God creates everything, including sinful acts, He wills their existence. However, this does not mean He approves of them morally.

Checkpoint

D) The Quran states it explicitly.

Key Takeaway
The first proof: because God creates everything, He wills everything.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
How does the truth that God wills everything that exists change the way you pray? When you ask God for something, do you submit to His will, or do you try to bend His will to yours? What concrete step can you take today to align your desires with His?

Second Proof and the Middle Path
Razi gives the second proof, answers objections, and concludes with the middle path

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof from divine knowledge of impossibility, the Mu'tazila objections, and the final Ash'ari resolution that divine will and human responsibility lie between compulsion and delegation.

Core Idea
God cannot will what He knows is impossible, and the truth lies between jabr and tafwid.

Imam Razi strengthens his case with a second proof: since God knows that faith is impossible from a disbeliever, He cannot will it. The Mu'tazila counter with objections based on the command to believe and the nature of obedience. Razi dismisses these by referring to earlier discussions and then presents the balanced position of the people of verification: neither absolute delegation nor compulsion, but a matter between the two. This lesson explores these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read: 'because when He knew that faith does not occur from the disbeliever, its occurrence from the disbeliever was impossible... so it is impossible that He will faith from the disbeliever.' Understand the logic: impossible things cannot be willed.
This proof uses God's knowledge of actual outcomes.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila objections
The Mu'tazila argue: (1) God commanded belief, and command indicates will. (2) If God willed disbelief, the disbeliever would be obedient. Reflect on these objections.
These objections challenge the earlier conclusion.
3
See Razi's response and the final position
Razi says these have been answered before. Then he states the true position: 'Neither delegation nor compulsion, but a matter between the two.' This is the Ash'ari middle path.
This resolves the tension between divine will and human agency.

Source Text

مسألة: أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات خلافا للمعتزلة. لنا أنا بينا أنه تعالى خالقها وقد تقدم أن خالق الشيء مريد لوجوده، ولأنه لما علم أن الإيمان لا يوجد من الكافر كان وجوده من الكافر محالا كما ظهر فيكون الله تعالى عالما بكونه محالا، والعالم بكون الشيء محالا لا يريده فيستحيل أن يريد الإيمان من الكافر. استجوا [؟] : أنه أمر الكافر بالإيمان والأمر يدل على الإرادة. وثانيها: أن الطاعة موافقة الإرادة فلو أراد الله تعالى كفر الكافر المكان [؟] الكافر مطيعاله [؟] بكفره. وأما جواب المعتزلة عن قوله تعالى ما علم الله وجوده فهو واجب وبوجوب الفعل عند ترجيح وامتناعه عند عدمه فقد مر الكلام فيه ولا وجه لإعادته. وقال أهل التحقيق في هذا الموضع ولا تفويض ولكن أمر بين أمرين، فهذا هو الحق ومن لا يعرف حقيقته وقع في التحير.

The author gives two proofs: (1) God is the Creator of all things, and the Creator of a thing necessarily wills its existence. God Wills All Contingent Things
Razi proves God wills all things, answers Mu'tazila, and affirms the middle position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that God wills all contingent things, and how he answers Mu'tazilite objections by distinguishing between command and will, and between decree and its object.

Core Idea
God creates all things, so He wills them; objections misunderstand divine commands and decree.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi defends the Ash'ari position that God's will encompasses all contingent events, including disbelief and evil. He provides two proofs and then addresses three key objections raised by the Mu'tazila, who limit God's will to good acts. Finally, he presents the balanced position between compulsion and delegation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: proof, objections, answers, and final conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two proofs
Razi gives two arguments for God willing all things. Find them in the text: one from creation, one from knowledge of impossibility.
The second proof involves God's knowledge that faith cannot come from a disbeliever.
3
Examine the three objections
The Mu'tazila raise three objections based on command, obedience, and pleasure with decree. List each one and note the underlying assumption.
Each objection tries to show that God's will must be limited.
4
Analyze Razi's answers
For each objection, find Razi's answer. Notice how he distinguishes between command and will, between obedience to command vs. will, and between decree and its object.
These distinctions are central to Ash'ari theology.
5
Understand the final position
Razi ends by citing the people of verification who affirm 'amr bayn amrayn' (a matter between two matters). Reflect on how this resolves the apparent tension.
This is the orthodox Ash'ari position on divine will and human action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات خلافا للمعتزلة. لنا أنا بينا أنه تعالى خالقها وقد تقدم أن خالق الشيء مريد لوجوده، ولأنه لما علم أن الإيمان لا يوجد من الكافر كان وجوده من الكافر محالا كما ظهر فيكون الله تعالى عالما بكونه محالا، والعالم بكون الشيء محالا لا يريده فيستحيل أن يريد الإيمان من الكافر . احتجوا: أنه أمر الكافر بالإيمان والأمر يدل على الإرادة. وثانيها: أن الطاعة موافقة الإرادة فلو أراد الله تعالى كفر الكافر المكان الكافر مطيعاله بكفره. وثالثها: أن الرضا بقضاء الله تعالى واجب، ولو كان الكفر بقضائه لوجب الرضابه ولكن الرضا بالكفر كفر. الجواب عن الأول: لا نسلم أن الأمر يدل على الإرادة وسأبينه في كتاب أصول الفقه إن شاء الله تعالى . الثانى: الطاعة موافقة الأمر لا موافقة الإرادة . وعن الثالث: أن الكفر ليس نفس القضاء بل متعاق القضاء، فنحن نرضى بالقضاء لا بالمقضى. وقال أهل التحقيق في هذا الموضع ولا تفويض ولكن أمر بين أمرين، فهذا هو الحق ومن لا يعرف حقيقته وقع في التحير
Question: He, exalted, is willing for all contingent things, contrary to the Mu'tazila. Our proof: we have shown that He is their creator, and it has preceded that the creator of a thing is willing for its existence. And because when He knows that faith will not occur from the disbeliever, its occurrence from the disbeliever is impossible, as appeared, so God knows it is impossible, and the one who knows a thing is impossible does not will it, so it is impossible that He will faith from the disbeliever. They objected: He commanded the disbeliever to believe, and command indicates will. Second: obedience is agreement with will, so if God willed the disbeliever's disbelief, then the disbeliever would be obedient to Him by his disbelief. Third: being pleased with God's decree is obligatory, and if disbelief is by His decree, then being pleased with it is obligatory, but being pleased with disbelief is disbelief. The answer to the first: we do not concede that command indicates will, and I will explain it in the book of Usul al-Fiqh, God willing. The second: obedience is agreement with command, not agreement with will. And the third: disbelief is not the decree itself but the object of the decree, so we are pleased with the decree, not with the object of decree. And the people of verification said in this place: neither compulsion nor delegation, but a matter between the two matters. This is the truth, and whoever does not know its reality falls into confusion.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal question (mas'ala) proving that God wills all contingent things, in opposition to the Mu'tazila who limit God's will to good acts. Razi gives two proofs: first, God is the creator of everything, and the creator of a thing wills its existence; second, God knows that faith will never occur from a particular disbeliever, so its occurrence from him is impossible, and God cannot will the impossible. Then he addresses three objections from the Mu'tazila. The first objection: God's command to believe implies that He wills belief from the disbeliever. Razi denies that command implies will, deferring the explanation to his work on usul al-fiqh. The second objection: obedience is conformity to will, so if God wills disbelief, the disbeliever would be obedient. Razi distinguishes: obedience is conformity to command, not to will. The third objection: one must be pleased with God's decree, but if disbelief is decreed, being pleased with it would be disbelief. Razi answers: disbelief is not the decree itself but the object of the decree (maqdi); we are pleased with the decree, not with the object. Finally, Razi cites the 'people of verification' (ahl al-tahqiq) affirming the middle position between jabr (compulsion) and tafwid (delegation), which is the correct view.

Key Terms
إرادة
أمر
طاعة
قضاء
مقضى
تفويض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God wills all things, then He wills evil and disbelief in the same sense as good, and that His command always reflects His will.
Correction: Razi clarifies that God's command is distinct from His will; He may command what He does not will. Moreover, we must distinguish between the divine decree (qada') and its object (maqdi); we affirm the decree but not its content when it is evil.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that God's command to believe indicates He wills faith from the disbeliever?
A) He agrees that command implies will.
B) He denies that command implies will.
C) He says the command is only for those who are able.
D) He says God does not command disbelief.

Key Takeaway
God's will encompasses all things, but His command is distinct from His will, and we must distinguish between decree and its object to avoid theological confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience something painful or unjust, do you resent God's decree—or do you truly distinguish between His wise decree and the evil object? Are you willing to accept that God decreed it for a reason you may never know, while still hating the sin or harm itself?
?
You know God commands faith to all, yet He does not will it for some. Does this unsettle you? Do you trust that His wisdom in withholding faith from certain people is just, even if you cannot understand it? How can you actively submit to this in your heart without becoming indifferent to their disbelief?
?
Razi warns that confusion leads to 'falling into confusion' (التحير). Have you ever felt spiritually confused about God's will and evil? Will you commit to not forcing yourself into either extreme (jabr or tafwid) but rather seeking the middle path—acknowledging God's full will while holding people accountable for their choices?

Two Wills of God: Distinguishing Divine Will
Imam Razi refutes objections by distinguishing two divine wills and clarifying pleasure with decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians distinguish between God's will for His own act and His will for another's act, and how this distinction resolves objections about divine command and human responsibility.

Core Idea
God's will for His own act differs from His will for another's act; command only indicates the latter.

This marginal note from Imam Razi refines the Ash'ari response to objections about divine will and human acts. It introduces a crucial distinction between two types of divine will: the will by which God acts directly and the will by which He commands others. Understanding this distinction is key to resolving apparent conflicts between divine predestination and human moral responsibility. The note also addresses a subtle point about being pleased with God's decree versus being pleased with the object of decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the note is structured as a dialogue with an opponent.
2
Identify the two wills of God
Find where the author distinguishes between God's will for His own act (إرادة الأولى) and His will for another's act (إرادة الثانية). Understand how command relates only to the second will.
This distinction is central to defusing the objection that God commands what He does not will.
3
Analyze the decree vs. object of decree
Examine the objection about being pleased with disbelief as God's decree. Understand why the initial response is insufficient and what the correct answer is.
The key is that pleasure is directed at the divine attribute of decree, not the act itself.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how these distinctions protect Ash'ari theology from accusations of making God the author of evil or contradicting His commands.
This nuance preserves both divine sovereignty and human moral accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول للخصم: أن يقول أما الحجة الأولى فموقوفة على إثبات كون الله تعالى خالقا لأعمال العبادة، وأما الحجة الثانية فيقول عليها إن وجود الإيمان ليس بمحال بالنظر إلى قدرة القادر، ومحال بالنظر إلى إرادته، فيجوز أن يتعلق به إرادة الله من حيث إنه يمكن لا من حيث أنه محال. وأما احتجاج الخصم بأنه أمر الكافر بالإيمان والأمر يدل على الإرادة، فنقول في جوابه إن إرادة الفاعل لفعله غير إرادته لفعل غيره والأمر يدل على الإرادة الثانية دون الأولى ومدعانا هى الإرادة الأولى . وكذا الكلام في الحجة الثانية أعنى الطاعة موافقة الإرادة الثانية دون الأولى ، وجوابه على الثانية بأن الكفر ليس نفس القضاء إنما هو المقضى ليس بشيء فان القائل رضيت بقضاء الله تعالى لا يعنى به رضاه بصفة صفات الله تعالى إنما يريد به رضاه بما يقتضى تلك الصفة ، وهو المقضى والجواب الصحيح أن الرضا بالكفر قضاء الله طاعة ولا من من حيث هذه الحيثية كفر
I say to the opponent: he may say that the first proof depends on establishing that God is the creator of the acts of servants. As for the second proof, he may say about it that the existence of faith is not impossible with respect to the power of the Powerful, but it is impossible with respect to His will, so it is possible for God's will to attach to it in terms of its possibility, not in terms of its impossibility. As for the opponent's argument that He commanded the disbeliever to believe and command indicates will, we say in response: the agent's will for his own act is different from his will for the act of another, and command indicates the second will, not the first, and our claim is the first will. The same applies to the second argument, namely that obedience is agreement with the second will, not the first. And his response to the second—that disbelief is not the decree itself but the object of decree—is not valid, for when someone says 'I am pleased with God's decree' he does not mean his pleasure in the attribute of God's attributes, but rather his pleasure in what that attribute entails, which is the object of decree. The correct answer is that being pleased with disbelief as God's decree is obedience, not disbelief, from the perspective that it is decree.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a marginal note (beginning with 'aqul') that provides a more nuanced refutation of the objections. It addresses two points. First, the opponent may argue that the proof from divine creation is not yet established, and that faith's impossibility is only with respect to divine will, not divine power, so God could still will it in principle. The note argues that God's will for His own act differs from His will for another's act. Command indicates the second will (that the commanded person should do the act), not the first will (that God Himself brings about the act). Since the Ash'ari claim is about the first will, the objection fails. Second, regarding the answer to the third objection about being pleased with the decree versus the object, the note criticizes the distinction as insufficient because when people say 'we are pleased with God's decree' they mean the object of decree. The correct answer is that being pleased with disbelief insofar as it is God's decree is obedience, not disbelief, because the pleasure is directed at the decree, not the act of disbelief qua disbelief.

Key Terms
إرادة الفاعل
إرادة الثانية
قضاء
مقضى
رضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being pleased with disbelief as God's decree is still an act of disbelief in itself.
Correction: The note clarifies that pleasure in the decree is directed at the divine attribute (decree), not the act of disbelief, so it is obedience, not disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal note, what is the correct answer to the objection that being pleased with disbelief as God's decree would be disbelief?
A) Disbelief is not the decree but the object of decree.
B) Being pleased with disbelief as God's decree is obedience, not disbelief.
C) One should not be pleased with God's decree at all.
D) The objection is invalid because command does not indicate will.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between God's will for His own act and His will for another's act is essential to resolving objections about divine command and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity that is clearly God's decree, do you find yourself questioning God's goodness because you confuse the decree itself with the act it contains? How will you train your heart to distinguish between the two starting today?
?
The text says command indicates God's will for another's act, not His direct causing. Have you ever considered that God's commands to you are not a guarantee that He will make you obey, but a test of your free response? How will you respond differently knowing that your obedience is not compelled by God?
?
Being pleased with disbelief as God's decree is obedience, not disbelief. Where in your life do you struggle to accept a difficult divine decree because you mistake the object of the decree for the decree itself? What specific step will you take this week to cultivate that deeper pleasure?
