Philosophers' Theodicy: The Problem of Evil
Razi presents the philosophers' classification of existence into pure good and predominantly good, and their justification for evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that existence is either pure good or predominantly good, and that the presence of evil is a necessary consequence of the best possible world.

Core Idea
Evil exists because the best possible world must include some evil, but good overwhelmingly outweighs it.

The philosophers offer a theodicy to explain why a good and powerful God would allow evil. They argue that the world is predominantly good, and that creating it entirely free of evil is rationally impossible. Therefore, the presence of some evil is a necessary part of the overall good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi frames this as a 'problem' (مسألة) and presents the philosophers' view.
2
Identify the two categories of existence
The passage classifies everything into pure good (intellects and spheres) and predominantly good (this world). Find these terms in the Arabic.
خير محض and الخير غالب
3
Understand the rational argument for evil
The philosophers argue that it is rationally impossible to create a world completely free of evil. Since the good in this world outweighs the evil, the wisest option is to create it. Failing to do so would be a greater evil.
The key phrase: 'ترك الخير الكثير لأجل الشر القليل شراً كثيراً'
4
Distinguish the divine will regarding good and evil
Good and evil are both willed, but in different ways: good is pleasing (مرضى) to God, while evil is willed by necessity (بالضرورة) and hated by essence (مكروه بالذات).
This nuance is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.
5
Reflect on the broader context
Razi mentions that he discussed this principle in his commentary on Ibn Sina's al-Isharat. Consider how this argument fits into the larger debate about divine attributes and qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : قالت الفلاسفة الموجود ما خير محض كالعقول والأفلاك أو الخير غالب فيه كما فى هذا العالم فإن المرض وإن كان كثيراً لكن الصحة أكثر ، فلما امتنع عقلا إيجاد ما في هذا العالم مبرأ عن الشرور بالكلية كان ترك الخير الكثير لأجل الشر القليل شراً كثيراً وجب في الحكمة إيجاده ، فالخير والشر مرادان لكن الخير مرضى الشر مراد بالضرورة مكروه بالذات وهذه القاعدة قد تكلمنا عليها في شرح الإشارات
Problem: The philosophers said: Existence is either pure good, like the intellects and spheres, or good is predominant in it, as in this world. For even though illness is much, health is more. So when it is rationally impossible to bring what is in this world free of evils entirely, then leaving many goods for the sake of a little evil is a great evil, so wisdom necessitates bringing it into existence. So good and evil are both intended, but good is pleasing, evil is necessarily willed and hated by essence. And this principle we have discussed in our commentary on al-Isharat.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' theodicy, i.e., their justification for the existence of evil. They classify existents into pure good (intellects and spheres) and predominantly good (this world, where good outweighs evil). Since it is rationally impossible to create a world completely free of evil, the best option is to create a world where the good greatly exceeds the evil. Failing to create such a world would be a greater evil. Therefore, wisdom necessitates creating it. Both good and evil are willed by God, but good is willed for its own sake (pleasing), while evil is willed by necessity and is hated in itself. Razi mentions that he discussed this principle in his commentary on Ibn Sina's al-Isharat.

Key Terms
خير محض (pure good)
شر (evil)
حكمة (wisdom)
إرادة (will)
مكروه بالذات (hated by essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the philosophers claim that evil is good or that God directly wills evil for its own sake.
Correction: The passage clarifies that evil is willed by necessity (بالضرورة) as a consequence of the best possible world, not for itself. It is hated by essence (مكروه بالذات), indicating God's displeasure with evil.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why does evil exist in the world?
A) Because God is not all-powerful.
B) Because it is rationally impossible to create a world free of evil, and the overall good is greater.
C) Because evil is a test for humans.
D) Because evil is a necessary attribute of matter.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that evil exists because the best possible world must contain some evil, but the overwhelming good justifies its existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or witness suffering, do you genuinely believe that this world is predominantly good? How does your answer change your response to that suffering? Commit to one specific way you will remind yourself of the greater good when you next encounter evil.
?
The philosophers argue that evil is willed by necessity, not for its own sake. Reflect on a time you resented God for an evil you experienced. Knowing this teaching, can you accept that God’s wisdom required that evil? What is stopping you from surrendering to that wisdom today?
?
If you were to truly internalize that leaving much good for a little evil would be a great evil, how would that change your perspective on the suffering in your life? Write down one concrete change you will make in your relationship with God based on this truth.

An Editorial Caution on Theodicy
Editorial comment on the philosophers' theodicy premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editorial reservation about the philosophers' theodicy and why the assumption that God's acts can be rationally justified is not uncontroversial.

Core Idea
Theodicy arguments stand or fall on the premise that God's acts have reasons.

This brief editorial remark appears after a presentation of the philosophers' theodicy. The author, Imam Razi, offers a short but significant caution: the entire argument rests on an assumption that is far from universally accepted. He hints that there is 'something in that' — a subtle warning to the careful reader.

Guided Path
1
Read the source text and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the phrasing 'absolute transmission' and the qualifying remark.
2
Identify the two key elements
Identify the nature of the transmission (naql mutlaq) and the controversial premise (ta'lil af'al Allah). Understand that the transmission itself is not questioned, but the premise is.
3
Reflect on the debate
Consider why the permissibility of giving reasons for God's acts is debated in kalam. Think about the implications for theodicy.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on how this editorial note affects the overall theodicy argument. Does it strengthen or weaken the philosophers' position?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول هذا نقل مطلق ليس فيه كلام إلا أن ذلك مبنى على جواز تعليل أفعال واجب الوجود وفيه ما فيه.
I say: This is an absolute transmission (naql mutlaq). There is no discussion in it except that it is based on the permissibility of giving reasons for the acts of the Necessary Existence, and there is something in that.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This short editorial comment observes that the philosophers' theodicy (the previous passage) is a pure transmission of their view. The commentator notes that the whole argument depends on the controversial premise that the acts of the Necessary Existence (God) can be given rational justifications (ta'lil). He hints that this premise is itself problematic, with 'something in it' meaning it is not universally accepted.

Key Terms
نقل مطلق
تعليل
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the editorial reservation about the philosophers' theodicy?
A) It is not transmitted correctly.
B) It assumes that God's acts can be justified rationally, which is not self-evident.
C) It denies the existence of evil.
D) It contradicts revelation.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' theodicy is only as strong as the controversial premise that God's acts have rational explanations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever demanded to know 'why me?' in a trial, assuming God must have a reason you can grasp? How does Razi's caution—that theodicy depends on a controversial premise—challenge your attitude toward unexplainable suffering? Will you now surrender your need for an explanation?
?
When you hear a religious justification for suffering (e.g., 'it's a test'), do you accept it without examining the underlying premise that God's acts have reasons? Spend time today questioning whether you too easily assume God's actions must fit your sense of rationality.
?
If the philosophers' theodicy rests on a premise even the scholars disagree on ('there is something in that'), how does that affect your trust in human attempts to justify God's ways? Will you now hold such explanations more lightly and instead focus on submission?

Nothing is Obligatory upon God
Razi states the Sunni thesis and the opposing Mu'tazila views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that nothing is obligatory upon God and the basic argument against the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
No one judges God; divine law establishes obligation.

Imam al-Razi begins by stating the central issue: nothing is obligatory upon God. This contradicts the Mu'tazila, who argue that certain things are incumbent on God. Razi presents the Sunni proof that obligation comes only from divine law, and since there is no lawgiver above God, nothing is obligatory upon Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage stating the issue and the opposing views.
2
Identify the Sunni argument
Find the key phrase: 'الحكم لا يثبت إلا بالشرع ولا حاكم على الشرع' and understand its meaning.
This is the core proof.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument refutes the Mu'tazila position.
If there is no judge over God, nothing can be morally obligatory upon Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا يجب على الله تعالى شيء خلافا للمعتزلة فإنهم يوجبون اللطف والعوض والثواب والبغداديون يوجبون العقاب ، والأصلح في الدنيا لنا أن الحكم لا يثبت إلا بالشرع ولا حاكم على الشرع فلا يجب عليه شيء ، عليه شيء ، ولأن اللطف هو الذي يفيد ترجيح الداعية بحيث لا ينتهى إلى حد الإلجاء فالداعية الواصلة إلى ذلك الحد شيء ممكن الوجود فى نفسه ، والله تعالى قادر على الممكنات فوجب أن يكون الله تعالى قادرا على إيجاد تلك الداعية المنتهية إلى ذلك الحد من غير تلك الواسطة ، أما العوض فلو كان واجبا لسكان دفع الألم دفعا لتلك المنافع العظيمة ، وكان يجب أن يقبح دفع الألم عن الغير كما قبح المنع من الفصد ، وأما الثواب فاله تعالى من النعم إفى العبد ما يحسن معه التكليف بهذا القدر من الطاعات فوجب أن لا يوجب الطاعات الثواب كما في الشاهد ، وأما الأصلح فى الدنيا فغير واجب لأن الأصلح للكافر الفقير أن لا يخلق حتى لا يكون معذبا في الدارين ، والأصلح أن يخلق عباده فى الجنة وأن يغنيهم بالمشتهيات الحسنة القبيحة ، وأما العقوبة فلان العذاب حقه وليس له فى استيفائه نفع ولا فى إسقاطه ضرر فيحسن إسقاطه ، كما في الشاهد

مان يقبح الثانى ويلجئون إلى التعريض الثلا يرتكبون فعل القبيح الذى يضطرون إليه ، ولا يلزم من ذلك عدم القطع بقبح الكذب لأن العقل هو الحاكم بالفعل ولا يقدح حكمه بتجويز إحتمال التخلف كما مر في الأحكام المقلية الضرورية وإنها لا تلتم بالاحتمالات التي يذكرها السوفسطائيون .

وقوله على تقدير التعويض أو جواز وجود المانع من القبح لا يبقى كذب فى العالم مجاب بأن تقرير وجودهما لا يوجب ارتفاع الكذب وإنما يوجب وجوب وقوع التعويض أو المانع في جميع الأحوال .

و تفسير الفبح بحصول الملاءمة والمنافرة غير صحيح عندهم فإن كثيراً مما هو ملائم قبيح كغضب الفقير ما يحتاج إليه من الفنى فإن ذلك ملائم له وهو قبيح ، وكثيراً ما هو منافر حسن ككف الظالم عن الظلم بأنواع التأديبات فإنه منافر وحسن . والتحقيق في هذا الباب إنه متفرع على وقوع الفعل من العبد ولا وقوعه منه فإن لم يكن العبد موجداً فلا قبيح وإن كان فقبيح موجود ويدل على ذلك التأمل في حدهما عند القائلين به .

أقول : ليس هذا الوجوب بكون الحكم الشرعى كما هو المصطلح عند الفقهاء بل هذا الوجوب بمعنى كون الفعل بحيث يستحق تاركه الذم ، كما أن القبيح بمعنى كون الفعل حيث يستحق فاعله الذم ، والكلام فيه هو ا كلام فى الحسن و القبح بعينه، ويقولون إن القادر العالم الغنى لا يترك الواجب ضرورة ، واللطف عندهم عبارة عن جميع ما يقرب العبد إلى الطاعة ويبعده عن المعصية حيث لا يؤدى إلى الإلجاء وهو من أفعال الله تعالى

وهو عندهم واجب بعد ثبوت التكليف وما ذكره في خلق الداعى من غير اللطف فقد مر الكلام فيه . وأما العوض فيقولون وقوع الآلام فى الخلق واجب لوجوب وقوع أسبابها والقادر العادل الغني إذا خلق ذلك فالواجب عليه أن يعوض المتألم نظراً إلى هدله ودفع الألم يؤدى إلى مفاسد فلذلك لم يدفعه . وأما الطاعات والتكاليف فلو كانت بإزاء نعم الله تعالى لسكان المثاب هو الله تعالى وتنزه عن أن ينعم ليأخذ عوضاً عن نعمه، إنما النعم تفضل منه، والثواب جزاء التكاليف، وإبطال الثواب من غير تكليف الطاعة غير ممكن لكون الثواب مشتملا على التعظيم والإجلال ذلك في غير المستحق قبيح، والأصلح واجب عند أبي القاسم البلخى وهو لا يقول بوجوبه في جميع المواضع بل يقول في المواضع المتعلقة بإزاحة عال المكافين

محسن

وما ذكره فى العقاب فهو كلام المرجئة والوعيدية يقولون الوعيد لطف وهو واجب والوفاء بالقول واجب وإلا لمكان الكذب حسنا فهذا كلامهم في هذا الباب والجميع مبنى على كون العبد فاعلا باختيار والقوال الأفعال وقبحها ووجوبها وإذا انهدمت تلك القواعد سقط جميع استدلالاتهم
Issue: Nothing is obligatory upon God Almighty, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For they obligate lutf (grace) and 'iwad (compensation) and thawab (reward), and the Baghdadis obligate punishment, and the most beneficial in this world. For us: Judgement is not established except by divine law, and there is no judge over divine law, so nothing is obligatory upon Him. And because lutf is that which benefits the preponderance of the motive such that it does not reach the limit of compulsion, so the motive reaching that limit is something possible in itself, and God Almighty is powerful over possible things, so it must be that God Almighty is able to create that motive reaching that limit without that intermediary. As for compensation, if it were obligatory, then [the claim] that removing pain would remove those great benefits, and it would be obligatory that removing pain from another be considered ugly just as preventing phlebotomy is considered ugly. As for reward, God Almighty from the favors [gives] to the servant what makes the obligation of this amount of acts of obedience good, so it must be that acts of obedience do not obligate reward as in the witnessed realm. As for the most beneficial in this world, it is not obligatory, because the most beneficial for the poor disbeliever is that he not be created so that he is not tormented in both abodes, and the most beneficial is that He create His servants in Paradise and enrich them with the good and ugly desires. As for punishment, because torment is His right, and He has no benefit in exacting it nor harm in waiving it, so waiving it is good, as in the witnessed realm.

[Text continues: an objection and response about the second (thing) and they resort to the third conditional they commit the ugly act which they are compelled to; and it does not follow from that the absence of certainty of the ugliness of lying because reason is the judge of the act and its judgment is not invalidated by allowing the possibility of failure as mentioned in the necessary rational judgments and that they are not affected by the possibilities mentioned by the sophists.

And his statement: on the assumption of compensation or the possibility of the existence of a preventer of ugliness, no lie remains in the world, is answered by: the establishment of their existence does not necessitate the removal of lying but rather necessitates the obligation of the occurrence of compensation or the preventer in all circumstances.

And the interpretation of ugliness by the occurrence of harmony and disharmony is not correct according to them, for much of what is harmonious is ugly, like the anger of a poor person at what he needs from the [?] person, for that is harmonious for him and it is ugly; and much of what is disharmonious is good, like restraining the oppressor from oppression with various kinds of disciplinary measures, for it is disharmonious and good. And the verification in this matter is that it branches upon the occurrence of the act from the servant and its non-occurrence from him; if the servant is not the originator, then there is no ugly act, but if he is, then ugly exists, and consideration of their definitions among those who hold them indicates that.

I say: This wujub (obligation) is not the same as the legal judgment as terminologically used by the jurists, but rather this wujub means that the act is such that its abandoner deserves blame, just as qabih (ugly) means that the act is such that its doer deserves blame, and the discussion here is the same as the discussion of good and ugly. And they say that the powerful, knowing, self-sufficient one necessarily does not abandon the obligatory. And lutf according to them is an expression of everything that brings the servant closer to obedience and distances him from disobedience without reaching compulsion, and it is among the acts of God Almighty. And it is obligatory according to them after the establishment of obligation. And what he mentioned about creating the motive without lutf has already been discussed. As for compensation, they say that the occurrence of pains in creation is obligatory due to the obligation of the occurrence of their causes, and the powerful, just, self-sufficient one, if He creates that, then it is obligatory upon Him to compensate the one pained in view of His justice, and removing pain leads to evils, so He did not remove it. As for acts of obedience and obligations, if they were in exchange for the favors of God Almighty, then the rewarder would be God Almighty, and He is exalted above giving favor to take compensation for His favors; favors are only a gift from Him, and reward is the recompense of obligations. Invalidation of reward without the obligation of obedience is not possible because reward includes glorification and veneration, and that is ugly in the case of one who is not deserving. And the most beneficial is obligatory according to Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, but he does not say it is obligatory in all cases, rather he says in cases related to removing the excuses of the obligated.

[Text: 'Muhsin'? unclear]

And what he mentioned about punishment is the speech of the Murji'a and the Wa'idiyya: they say that the threat is lutf and it is obligatory, and fulfillment of the promise is obligatory, otherwise lying would be good. So this is their speech on this topic, and all of it is based on the servant being an agent by choice, and on the principles of acts and their ugliness and obligation, and if those foundations are demolished, all their deductions fall.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument against the Mu'tazila and others who hold that certain things are obligatory upon God. The author, Imam al-Razi, presents the Sunni position that nothing is obligatory upon God. He first states the issue and the opposing views: the Mu'tazila obligate lutf (grace), 'iwad (compensation), and thawab (reward), while the Baghdadis obligate punishment and the most beneficial. Then he gives the Sunni argument: judgments are based only on divine law, and there is no judge over divine law, so nothing is obligatory upon God. He then refutes each claimed obligation in turn. For lutf, he argues that if lutf is a motive that does not compel, God can create that motive directly without the intermediary of lutf, so it is not obligatory. For compensation, if it were obligatory, then preventing harm would be considered ugly, but that is not the case. For reward, God's favors make obedience possible, so reward is not obligatory. For the most beneficial, it is not obligatory because it could involve not creating a disbeliever or creating all in Paradise—these are not necessarily best. For punishment, since God has no benefit or harm from it, waiving it is good. The passage then addresses objections regarding the definition of ugliness and the possibility of lying. The commentator (likely Razi himself) adds a clarification that 'obligation' here is not legal but moral, and then elaborates on the Mu'tazila and others' views on lutf, compensation, reward, and punishment, arguing that all these positions depend on the premise that human acts are created by humans, which Razi rejects. The passage ends by asserting that if that premise is destroyed, all their deductions fall.

Key Terms
واجب
شرع
حاكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that God is obligated to do certain things because human reason can determine what is good and necessary.
Correction: Razi argues that obligation is only known through divine revelation, and no one can judge God, so nothing is obligatory upon Him.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the basis for obligation?
A) Human reason
B) Divine law
C) Social contract
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
No obligation binds God because He is the source of all law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial and feel that God should have spared you, are you setting yourself up as a judge over divine law? What would it look like to surrender that judgment today?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they could reason about what God must do. Are there areas in your life where you assume God owes you something—health, provision, or ease? How can you turn that assumption into gratitude for His unearned grace?
?
If no obligation binds God, then every command He gives you is a gift, not a right. Does your obedience reflect that reality? Take a moment to repent of any sense of entitlement toward your Creator.

Refuting the Obligation of Lutf and 'Iwad
Razi refutes the obligation of lutf and compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the Mu'tazila claims that God is obligated to provide lutf (grace) and compensation for harms.

Core Idea
God can create motives directly; compensation is not obligatory.

The Mu'tazila argue that God must do what is most beneficial for His creatures, including providing lutf (grace that inclines to obedience) and compensating for pains. Razi counters that lutf is not necessary because God can directly create the motive without an intermediary, and compensation is not obligatory because preventing harm does not always incur ugliness.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of lutf
Read the Arabic passage defining lutf and the argument against its obligation.
2
Understand the definition of lutf
Lutf is defined as that which preponderates the motive without compelling.
3
See the argument against lutf
The motive is a possible thing, and God can create it directly, so lutf is not needed.
4
Read the refutation of compensation
Read the argument comparing compensation to phlebotomy.
5
Understand the argument against compensation
If compensation were obligatory, preventing pain would be ugly, but it is not always ugly.

Source Text

مسألة : لا يجب على الله تعالى شيء خلافا للمعتزلة فإنهم يوجبون اللطف والعوض والثواب والبغداديون يوجبون العقاب ، والأصلح في الدنيا لنا أن الحكم لا يثبت إلا بالشرع ولا حاكم على الشرع فلا يجب عليه شيء ، عليه شيء ، ولأن اللطف هو الذي يفيد ترجيح الداعية بحيث لا ينتهى إلى حد الإلجاء فالداعية الواصلة إلى ذلك الحد شيء ممكن الوجود فى نفسه ، والله تعالى قادر على الممكنات فوجب أن يكون الله تعالى قادرا على إيجاد تلك الداعية المنتهية إلى ذلك الحد من غير تلك الواسطة ، أما العوض فلو كان واجبا لسكان دفع الألم دفعا لتلك المنافع العظيمة ، وكان يجب أن يقبح دفع الألم عن الغير كما قبح المنع من الفصد ، وأما الثواب فاله تعالى من النعم إفى العبد ما يحسن معه التكليف بهذا القدر من الطاعات فوجب أن لا يوجب الطاعات الثواب كما في الشاهد ، وأما الأصلح فى الدنيا فغير واجب لأن الأصلح للكافر الفقير أن لا يخلق حتى لا يكون معذبا في الدارين ، والأصلح أن يخلق عباده فى الجنة وأن يغنيهم بالمشتهيات الحسنة القبيحة ، وأما العقوبة فلان العذاب حقه وليس له فى استيفائه نفع ولا فى إسقاطه ضرر فيحسن إسقاطه ، كما في الشاهد

مان يقبح الثانى ويلجئون إلى التعريض الثلا يرتكبون فعل القبيح الذى يضطرون إليه ، ولا يلزم من ذلك عدم القطع بقبح الكذب لأن العقل هو الحاكم بالفعل ولا يقدح حكمه بتجويز إحتمال التخلف كما مر في الأحكام المقلية الضرورية وإنها لا تلتم بالاحتمالات التي يذكرها السوفسطائيون .

وقوله على تقدير التعويض أو جواز وجود المانع من القبح لا يبقى كذب فى العالم مجاب بأن تقرير وجودهما لا يوجب ارتفاع الكذب وإنما يوجب وجوب وقوع التعويض أو المانع في جميع الأحوال .

و تفسير الفبح بحصول الملاءمة والمنافرة غير صحيح عندهم فإن كثيراً مما هو ملائم قبيح كغضب الفقير ما يحتاج إليه من الفنى فإن ذلك ملائم له وهو قبيح ، وكثيراً ما هو منافر حسن ككف الظالم عن الظلم بأنواع التأديبات فإنه منافر وحسن . والتحقيق في هذا الباب إنه متفرع على وقوع الفعل من العبد ولا وقوعه منه فإن لم يكن العبد موجداً فلا قبيح وإن كان فقبيح موجود ويدل على ذلك التأمل في حدهما عند القائلين به .

أقول : ليس هذا الوجوب بكون الحكم الشرعى كما هو المصطلح عند الفقهاء بل هذا الوجوب بمعنى كون الفعل بحيث يستحق تاركه الذم ، كما أن القبيح بمعنى كون الفعل حيث يستحق فاعله الذم ، والكلام فيه هو ا كلام فى الحسن و القبح بعينه، ويقولون إن القادر العالم الغنى لا يترك الواجب ضرورة ، واللطف عندهم عبارة عن جميع ما يقرب العبد إلى الطاعة ويبعده عن المعصية حيث لا يؤدى إلى الإلجاء وهو من أفعال الله تعالى

وهو عندهم واجب بعد ثبوت التكليف وما ذكره في خلق الداعى من غير اللطف فقد مر الكلام فيه . وأما العوض فيقولون وقوع الآلام فى الخلق واجب لوجوب وقوع أسبابها والقادر العادل الغني إذا خلق ذلك فالواجب عليه أن يعوض المتألم نظراً إلى هدله ودفع الألم يؤدى إلى مفاسد فلذلك لم يدفعه . وأما الطاعات والتكاليف فلو كانت بإزاء نعم الله تعالى لسكان المثاب هو الله تعالى وتنزه عن أن ينعم ليأخذ عوضاً عن نعمه، إنما النعم تفضل منه، والثواب جزاء التكاليف، وإبطال الثواب من غير تكليف الطاعة غير ممكن لكون الثواب مشتملا على التعظيم والإجلال ذلك في غير المستحق قبيح، والأصلح واجب عند أبي القاسم البلخى وهو لا يقول بوجوبه في جميع المواضع بل يقول في المواضع المتعلقة بإزاحة عال المكافين

محسن

وما ذكره فى العقاب فهو كلام المرجئة والوعيدية يقولون الوعيد لطف وهو واجب والوفاء بالقول واجب وإلا لمكان الكذب حسنا فهذا كلامهم في هذا الباب والجميع مبنى على كون العبد فاعلا باختيار والقوال الأفعال وقبحها ووجوبها وإذا انهدمت تلك القواعد سقط جميع استدلالاتهم
Issue: Nothing is obligatory upon God Almighty, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For they obligate lutf (grace) and 'iwad (compensation) and thawab (reward), and the Baghdadis obligate punishment, and the most beneficial in this world. For us: Judgement is not established except by divine law, and there is no judge over divine law, so nothing is obligatory upon Him. And because lutf is that which benefits the preponderance of the motive such that it does not reach the limit of compulsion, so the motive reaching that limit is something possible in itself, and God Almighty is powerful over possible things, so it must be that God Almighty is able to create that motive reaching that limit without that intermediary. As for compensation, if it were obligatory, then [the claim] that removing pain would remove those great benefits, and it would be obligatory that removing pain from another be considered ugly just as preventing phlebotomy is considered ugly. As for reward, God Almighty from the favors [gives] to the servant what makes the obligation of this amount of acts of obedience good, so it must be that acts of obedience do not obligate reward as in the witnessed realm. As for the most beneficial in this world, it is not obligatory, because the most beneficial for the poor disbeliever is that he not be created so that he is not tormented in both abodes, and the most beneficial is that He create His servants in Paradise and enrich them with the good and ugly desires. As for punishment, because torment is His right, and He has no benefit in exacting it nor harm in waiving it, so waiving it is good, as in the witnessed realm.

[Text continues: an objection and response about the second (thing) and they resort to the third conditional they commit the ugly act which they are compelled to; and it does not follow from that the absence of certainty of the ugliness of lying because reason is the judge of the act and its judgment is not invalidated by allowing the possibility of failure as mentioned in the necessary rational judgments and that they are not affected by the possibilities mentioned by the sophists.

And his statement: on the assumption of compensation or the possibility of the existence of a preventer of ugliness, no lie remains in the world, is answered by: the establishment of their existence does not necessitate the removal of lying but rather necessitates the obligation of the occurrence of compensation or the preventer in all circumstances.

And the interpretation of ugliness by the occurrence of harmony and disharmony is not correct according to them, for much of what is harmonious is ugly, like the anger of a poor person at what he needs from the [?] person, for that is harmonious for him and it is ugly; and much of what is disharmonious is good, like restraining the oppressor from oppression with various kinds of disciplinary measures, for it is disharmonious and good. And the verification in this matter is that it branches upon the occurrence of the act from the servant and its non-occurrence from him; if the servant is not the originator, then there is no ugly act, but if he is, then ugly exists, and consideration of their definitions among those who hold them indicates that.

I say: This wujub (obligation) is not the same as the legal judgment as terminologically used by the jurists, but rather this wujub means that the act is such that its abandoner deserves blame, just as qabih (ugly) means that the act is such that its doer deserves blame, and the discussion here is the same as the discussion of good and ugly. And they say that the powerful, knowing, self-sufficient one necessarily does not abandon the obligatory. And lutf according to them is an expression of everything that brings the servant closer to obedience and distances him from disobedience without reaching compulsion, and it is among the acts of God Almighty. And it is obligatory according to them after the establishment of obligation. And what he mentioned about creating the motive without lutf has already been discussed. As for compensation, they say that the occurrence of pains in creation is obligatory due to the obligation of the occurrence of their causes, and the powerful, just, self-sufficient one, if He creates that, then it is obligatory upon Him to compensate the one pained in view of His justice, and removing pain leads to evils, so He did not remove it. As for acts of obedience and obligations, if they were in exchange for the favors of God Almighty, then the rewarder would be God Almighty, and He is exalted above giving favor to take compensation for His favors; favors are only a gift from Him, and reward is the recompense of obligations. Invalidation of reward without the obligation of obedience is not possible because reward includes glorification and veneration, and that is ugly in the case of one who is not deserving. And the most beneficial is obligatory according to Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, but he does not say it is obligatory in all cases, rather he says in cases related to removing the excuses of the obligated.

[Text: 'Muhsin'? unclear]

And what he mentioned about punishment is the speech of the Murji'a and the Wa'idiyya: they say that the threat is lutf and it is obligatory, and fulfillment of the promise is obligatory, otherwise lying would be good. So this is their speech on this topic, and all of it is based on the servant being an agent by choice, and on the principles of acts and their ugliness and obligation, and if those foundations are demolished, all their deductions fall.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument against the Mu'tazila and others who hold that certain things are obligatory upon God. The author, Imam al-Razi, presents the Sunni position that nothing is obligatory upon God. He first states the issue and the opposing views: the Mu'tazila obligate lutf (grace), 'iwad (compensation), and thawab (reward), while the Baghdadis obligate punishment and the most beneficial. Then he gives the Sunni argument: judgments are based only on divine law, and there is no judge over divine law, so nothing is obligatory upon God. He then refutes each claimed obligation in turn. For lutf, he argues that if lutf is a motive that does not compel, God can create that motive directly without the intermediary of lutf, so it is not obligatory. For compensation, if it were obligatory, then preventing harm would be considered ugly, but that is not the case. For reward, God's favors make obedience possible, so reward is not obligatory. For the most beneficial, it is not obligatory because it could involve not creating a disbeliever or creating all in Paradise—these are not necessarily best. For punishment, since God has no benefit or harm from it, waiving it is good. The passage then addresses objections regarding the definition of ugliness and the possibility of lying. The commentator (likely Razi himself) adds a clarification that 'obligation' here is not legal but moral, and then elaborates on the Mu'tazila and others' views on lutf, compensation, reward, and punishment, arguing that all these positions depend on the premise that human acts are created by humans, which Razi rejects. The passage ends by asserting that if that premise is destroyed, all their deductions fall.

Key Terms
لطف
عوض
الداعية
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that lutf is necessary because without it, humans would not be inclined to obedience.
Correction: Razi shows that God can directly create the motive, so lutf is not needed as an intermediary.

Checkpoint
How does Razi argue that lutf is not obligatory?
A) Lutf is impossible for God to create.
B) The motive lutf produces is possible and God can create it directly.
C) Lutf is only for angels.
D) Lutf would contradict God's freedom.

Key Takeaway
Lutf is not necessary because God's power is direct; compensation is not obligatory because preventing harm can be good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God can create faith directly in your heart without any worldly means? If so, why do you still rely on sermons, books, or scholars to strengthen your iman? Will you today ask Allah directly for that motive, without intermediaries?
?
When you suffer a loss or pain, do you secretly feel that Allah owes you compensation? This lesson says compensation is not obligatory. Does that disturb you? Can you accept that your trials may be for a greater benefit without expecting repayment?
?
If lutf is not necessary, then you are fully responsible for your response to God's call. Are you making excuses for your sins by blaming lack of divine grace? What will you change today to take full responsibility for your faith and actions?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
Refuting Obligations of Reward, Most Beneficial, and Punishment
Razi refutes obligations of reward, most beneficial, and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's arguments against the Mu'tazila obligations of reward, the most beneficial, and punishment.

Core Idea
Reward is not obligatory; most beneficial is subjective; God can waive punishment.

The Mu'tazila also claim that God must reward obedience, always do what is most beneficial, and punish evil. Razi counters that reward is not a necessary compensation, the most beneficial is not universally defined, and punishment is not a required act since God suffers no loss from waiving it.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of reward
Read the passage about reward and God's favors.
2
Understand the argument against reward
God's favors are free, so reward is not a necessary consequence.
3
Read the refutation of the most beneficial
Read the passage about what is best for a disbeliever.
4
Understand the argument against most beneficial
What is most beneficial varies; for a disbeliever, non-existence might be best.
5
Read the refutation of punishment
Read the passage about punishment being God's right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا يجب على الله تعالى شيء خلافا للمعتزلة فإنهم يوجبون اللطف والعوض والثواب والبغداديون يوجبون العقاب ، والأصلح في الدنيا لنا أن الحكم لا يثبت إلا بالشرع ولا حاكم على الشرع فلا يجب عليه شيء ، عليه شيء ، ولأن اللطف هو الذي يفيد ترجيح الداعية بحيث لا ينتهى إلى حد الإلجاء فالداعية الواصلة إلى ذلك الحد شيء ممكن الوجود فى نفسه ، والله تعالى قادر على الممكنات فوجب أن يكون الله تعالى قادرا على إيجاد تلك الداعية المنتهية إلى ذلك الحد من غير تلك الواسطة ، أما العوض فلو كان واجبا لسكان دفع الألم دفعا لتلك المنافع العظيمة ، وكان يجب أن يقبح دفع الألم عن الغير كما قبح المنع من الفصد ، وأما الثواب فاله تعالى من النعم إفى العبد ما يحسن معه التكليف بهذا القدر من الطاعات فوجب أن لا يوجب الطاعات الثواب كما في الشاهد ، وأما الأصلح فى الدنيا فغير واجب لأن الأصلح للكافر الفقير أن لا يخلق حتى لا يكون معذبا في الدارين ، والأصلح أن يخلق عباده فى الجنة وأن يغنيهم بالمشتهيات الحسنة القبيحة ، وأما العقوبة فلان العذاب حقه وليس له فى استيفائه نفع ولا فى إسقاطه ضرر فيحسن إسقاطه ، كما في الشاهد

مان يقبح الثانى ويلجئون إلى التعريض الثلا يرتكبون فعل القبيح الذى يضطرون إليه ، ولا يلزم من ذلك عدم القطع بقبح الكذب لأن العقل هو الحاكم بالفعل ولا يقدح حكمه بتجويز إحتمال التخلف كما مر في الأحكام المقلية الضرورية وإنها لا تلتم بالاحتمالات التي يذكرها السوفسطائيون .

وقوله على تقدير التعويض أو جواز وجود المانع من القبح لا يبقى كذب فى العالم مجاب بأن تقرير وجودهما لا يوجب ارتفاع الكذب وإنما يوجب وجوب وقوع التعويض أو المانع في جميع الأحوال .

و تفسير الفبح بحصول الملاءمة والمنافرة غير صحيح عندهم فإن كثيراً مما هو ملائم قبيح كغضب الفقير ما يحتاج إليه من الفنى فإن ذلك ملائم له وهو قبيح ، وكثيراً ما هو منافر حسن ككف الظالم عن الظلم بأنواع التأديبات فإنه منافر وحسن . والتحقيق في هذا الباب إنه متفرع على وقوع الفعل من العبد ولا وقوعه منه فإن لم يكن العبد موجداً فلا قبيح وإن كان فقبيح موجود ويدل على ذلك التأمل في حدهما عند القائلين به .

أقول : ليس هذا الوجوب بكون الحكم الشرعى كما هو المصطلح عند الفقهاء بل هذا الوجوب بمعنى كون الفعل بحيث يستحق تاركه الذم ، كما أن القبيح بمعنى كون الفعل حيث يستحق فاعله الذم ، والكلام فيه هو ا كلام فى الحسن و القبح بعينه، ويقولون إن القادر العالم الغنى لا يترك الواجب ضرورة ، واللطف عندهم عبارة عن جميع ما يقرب العبد إلى الطاعة ويبعده عن المعصية حيث لا يؤدى إلى الإلجاء وهو من أفعال الله تعالى

وهو عندهم واجب بعد ثبوت التكليف وما ذكره في خلق الداعى من غير اللطف فقد مر الكلام فيه . وأما العوض فيقولون وقوع الآلام فى الخلق واجب لوجوب وقوع أسبابها والقادر العادل الغني إذا خلق ذلك فالواجب عليه أن يعوض المتألم نظراً إلى هدله ودفع الألم يؤدى إلى مفاسد فلذلك لم يدفعه . وأما الطاعات والتكاليف فلو كانت بإزاء نعم الله تعالى لسكان المثاب هو الله تعالى وتنزه عن أن ينعم ليأخذ عوضاً عن نعمه، إنما النعم تفضل منه، والثواب جزاء التكاليف، وإبطال الثواب من غير تكليف الطاعة غير ممكن لكون الثواب مشتملا على التعظيم والإجلال ذلك في غير المستحق قبيح، والأصلح واجب عند أبي القاسم البلخى وهو لا يقول بوجوبه في جميع المواضع بل يقول في المواضع المتعلقة بإزاحة عال المكافين

محسن

وما ذكره فى العقاب فهو كلام المرجئة والوعيدية يقولون الوعيد لطف وهو واجب والوفاء بالقول واجب وإلا لمكان الكذب حسنا فهذا كلامهم في هذا الباب والجميع مبنى على كون العبد فاعلا باختيار والقوال الأفعال وقبحها ووجوبها وإذا انهدمت تلك القواعد سقط جميع استدلالاتهم
Issue: Nothing is obligatory upon God Almighty, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For they obligate lutf (grace) and 'iwad (compensation) and thawab (reward), and the Baghdadis obligate punishment, and the most beneficial in this world. For us: Judgement is not established except by divine law, and there is no judge over divine law, so nothing is obligatory upon Him. And because lutf is that which benefits the preponderance of the motive such that it does not reach the limit of compulsion, so the motive reaching that limit is something possible in itself, and God Almighty is powerful over possible things, so it must be that God Almighty is able to create that motive reaching that limit without that intermediary. As for compensation, if it were obligatory, then [the claim] that removing pain would remove those great benefits, and it would be obligatory that removing pain from another be considered ugly just as preventing phlebotomy is considered ugly. As for reward, God Almighty from the favors [gives] to the servant what makes the obligation of this amount of acts of obedience good, so it must be that acts of obedience do not obligate reward as in the witnessed realm. As for the most beneficial in this world, it is not obligatory, because the most beneficial for the poor disbeliever is that he not be created so that he is not tormented in both abodes, and the most beneficial is that He create His servants in Paradise and enrich them with the good and ugly desires. As for punishment, because torment is His right, and He has no benefit in exacting it nor harm in waiving it, so waiving it is good, as in the witnessed realm.

And his statement: on the assumption of compensation or the possibility of the existence of a preventer of ugliness, no lie remains in the world, is answered by: the establishment of their existence does not necessitate the removal of lying but rather necessitates the obligation of the occurrence of compensation or the preventer in all circumstances.

And what he mentioned about punishment is the speech of the Murji'a and the Wa'idiyya: they say that the threat is lutf and it is obligatory, and fulfillment of the promise is obligatory, otherwise lying would be good. Commentary
This passage is a complex argument against the Mu'tazila and others who hold that certain things are obligatory upon God. The author, Imam al-Razi, presents the Sunni position that nothing is obligatory upon God. He first states the issue and the opposing views: the Mu'tazila obligate lutf (grace), 'iwad (compensation), and thawab (reward), while the Baghdadis obligate punishment and the most beneficial. Then he gives the Sunni argument: judgments are based only on divine law, and there is no judge over divine law, so nothing is obligatory upon God. He then refutes each claimed obligation in turn. For lutf, he argues that if lutf is a motive that does not compel, God can create that motive directly without the intermediary of lutf, so it is not obligatory. For compensation, if it were obligatory, then preventing harm would be considered ugly, but that is not the case. For reward, God's favors make obedience possible, so reward is not obligatory. For the most beneficial, it is not obligatory because it could involve not creating a disbeliever or creating all in Paradise—these are not necessarily best. For punishment, since God has no benefit or harm from it, waiving it is good. The passage then addresses objections regarding the definition of ugliness and the possibility of lying. The commentator (likely Razi himself) adds a clarification that 'obligation' here is not legal but moral, and then elaborates on the Mu'tazila and others' views on lutf, compensation, reward, and punishment, arguing that all these positions depend on the premise that human acts are created by humans, which Razi rejects. The passage ends by asserting that if that premise is destroyed, all their deductions fall.

Key Terms
ثواب
أصلح
عقاب
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that God must reward good deeds because it is just.
Correction: Razi argues that reward is not a necessary obligation; God's favors are free and not contingent on human obedience.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is punishment not obligatory upon God?
A) Punishment is always evil.
B) God has no benefit in exacting punishment nor harm in waiving it.
C) The Mu'tazila do not believe in punishment.
D) Punishment is only for angels.

Key Takeaway
Reward, most beneficial, and punishment are not obligatory because God's actions are not bound by human notions of necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If reward for your obedience is not a debt God owes you but a pure gift, how does that change the attitude with which you perform salah or give charity? Will you still pray if you feel no immediate reward?
?
Razi says that what is 'most beneficial' for a disbeliever might be non-existence. Have you ever wished you were not created because of hardship? How does this passage challenge your understanding of God's wisdom in creating you?
?
Since punishment is God’s right and He can waive it, are you living as if you expect God to forgive you regardless, or are you taking His mercy seriously? What would you change today to rely on His mercy without presuming upon it?

Divine Attributes

Understanding Obligation and Ugliness in Divine Acts
Commentator clarifies meaning of obligation and ugliness and sums up the debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deeper discussions about the meaning of obligation and ugliness as applied to God, and how they relate to human agency.

Core Idea
Obligation and ugliness depend on human free will.

After refuting specific Mu'tazila claims, Razi and the commentator delve into the conceptual foundations. They clarify that 'obligation' here means deserving blame for omission, and 'ugliness' means deserving blame for commission. They also engage with objections about the definition of ugliness and the issue of lying. The commentator adds that these debates presuppose human freedom, and if that is denied, the entire Mu'tazila edifice falls.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's clarification
Read the passage starting with 'أقول : ليس هذا الوجوب...'
2
Understand the definitions
Note that 'obligation' here is not legal but moral: deserving blame for omission.
3
Understand the foundations
The Mu'tazila arguments are based on human free will.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
If human agency is denied, the whole Mu'tazila system collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا يجب على الله تعالى شيء خلافا للمعتزلة فإنهم يوجبون اللطف والعوض والثواب والبغداديون يوجبون العقاب ، والأصلح في الدنيا لنا أن الحكم لا يثبت إلا بالشرع ولا حاكم على الشرع فلا يجب عليه شيء ، عليه شيء ، ولأن اللطف هو الذي يفيد ترجيح الداعية بحيث لا ينتهى إلى حد الإلجاء فالداعية الواصلة إلى ذلك الحد شيء ممكن الوجود فى نفسه ، والله تعالى قادر على الممكنات فوجب أن يكون الله تعالى قادرا على إيجاد تلك الداعية المنتهية إلى ذلك الحد من غير تلك الواسطة ، أما العوض فلو كان واجبا لسكان دفع الألم دفعا لتلك المنافع العظيمة ، وكان يجب أن يقبح دفع الألم عن الغير كما قبح المنع من الفصد ، وأما الثواب فاله تعالى من النعم إفى العبد ما يحسن معه التكليف بهذا القدر من الطاعات فوجب أن لا يوجب الطاعات الثواب كما في الشاهد ، وأما الأصلح فى الدنيا فغير واجب لأن الأصلح للكافر الفقير أن لا يخلق حتى لا يكون معذبا في الدارين ، والأصلح أن يخلق عباده فى الجنة وأن يغنيهم بالمشتهيات الحسنة القبيحة ، وأما العقوبة فلان العذاب حقه وليس له فى استيفائه نفع ولا فى إسقاطه ضرر فيحسن إسقاطه ، كما في الشاهد

مان يقبح الثانى ويلجئون إلى التعريض الثلا يرتكبون فعل القبيح الذى يضطرون إليه ، ولا يلزم من ذلك عدم القطع بقبح الكذب لأن العقل هو الحاكم بالفعل ولا يقدح حكمه بتجويز إحتمال التخلف كما مر في الأحكام المقلية الضرورية وإنها لا تلتم بالاحتمالات التي يذكرها السوفسطائيون .

وقوله على تقدير التعويض أو جواز وجود المانع من القبح لا يبقى كذب فى العالم مجاب بأن تقرير وجودهما لا يوجب ارتفاع الكذب وإنما يوجب وجوب وقوع التعويض أو المانع في جميع الأحوال .

و تفسير الفبح بحصول الملاءمة والمنافرة غير صحيح عندهم فإن كثيراً مما هو ملائم قبيح كغضب الفقير ما يحتاج إليه من الفنى فإن ذلك ملائم له وهو قبيح ، وكثيراً ما هو منافر حسن ككف الظالم عن الظلم بأنواع التأديبات فإنه منافر وحسن . والتحقيق في هذا الباب إنه متفرع على وقوع الفعل من العبد ولا وقوعه منه فإن لم يكن العبد موجداً فلا قبيح وإن كان فقبيح موجود ويدل على ذلك التأمل في حدهما عند القائلين به .

أقول : ليس هذا الوجوب بكون الحكم الشرعى كما هو المصطلح عند الفقهاء بل هذا الوجوب بمعنى كون الفعل بحيث يستحق تاركه الذم ، كما أن القبيح بمعنى كون الفعل حيث يستحق فاعله الذم ، والكلام فيه هو ا كلام فى الحسن و القبح بعينه، ويقولون إن القادر العالم الغنى لا يترك الواجب ضرورة ، واللطف عندهم عبارة عن جميع ما يقرب العبد إلى الطاعة ويبعده عن المعصية حيث لا يؤدى إلى الإلجاء وهو من أفعال الله تعالى

وهو عندهم واجب بعد ثبوت التكليف وما ذكره في خلق الداعى من غير اللطف فقد مر الكلام فيه . وأما العوض فيقولون وقوع الآلام فى الخلق واجب لوجوب وقوع أسبابها والقادر العادل الغني إذا خلق ذلك فالواجب عليه أن يعوض المتألم نظراً إلى هدله ودفع الألم يؤدى إلى مفاسد فلذلك لم يدفعه . وأما الطاعات والتكاليف فلو كانت بإزاء نعم الله تعالى لسكان المثاب هو الله تعالى وتنزه عن أن ينعم ليأخذ عوضاً عن نعمه، إنما النعم تفضل منه، والثواب جزاء التكاليف، وإبطال الثواب من غير تكليف الطاعة غير ممكن لكون الثواب مشتملا على التعظيم والإجلال ذلك في غير المستحق قبيح، والأصلح واجب عند أبي القاسم البلخى وهو لا يقول بوجوبه في جميع المواضع بل يقول في المواضع المتعلقة بإزاحة عال المكافين

محسن

وما ذكره فى العقاب فهو كلام المرجئة والوعيدية يقولون الوعيد لطف وهو واجب والوفاء بالقول واجب وإلا لمكان الكذب حسنا فهذا كلامهم في هذا الباب والجميع مبنى على كون العبد فاعلا باختيار والقوال الأفعال وقبحها ووجوبها وإذا انهدمت تلك القواعد سقط جميع استدلالاتهم
Issue: Nothing is obligatory upon God Almighty, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For they obligate lutf (grace) and 'iwad (compensation) and thawab (reward), and the Baghdadis obligate punishment, and the most beneficial in this world. For us: Judgement is not established except by divine law, and there is no judge over divine law, so nothing is obligatory upon Him. And because lutf is that which benefits the preponderance of the motive such that it does not reach the limit of compulsion, so the motive reaching that limit is something possible in itself, and God Almighty is powerful over possible things, so it must be that God Almighty is able to create that motive reaching that limit without that intermediary. As for compensation, if it were obligatory, then [the claim] that removing pain would remove those great benefits, and it would be obligatory that removing pain from another be considered ugly just as preventing phlebotomy is considered ugly. As for reward, God Almighty from the favors [gives] to the servant what makes the obligation of this amount of acts of obedience good, so it must be that acts of obedience do not obligate reward as in the witnessed realm. As for the most beneficial in this world, it is not obligatory, because the most beneficial for the poor disbeliever is that he not be created so that he is not tormented in both abodes, and the most beneficial is that He create His servants in Paradise and enrich them with the good and ugly desires. As for punishment, because torment is His right, and He has no benefit in exacting it nor harm in waiving it, so waiving it is good, as in the witnessed realm.

[Text continues: an objection and response about the second (thing) and they resort to the third conditional they commit the ugly act which they are compelled to; and it does not follow from that the absence of certainty of the ugliness of lying because reason is the judge of the act and its judgment is not invalidated by allowing the possibility of failure as mentioned in the necessary rational judgments and that they are not affected by the possibilities mentioned by the sophists.

And his statement: on the assumption of compensation or the possibility of the existence of a preventer of ugliness, no lie remains in the world, is answered by: the establishment of their existence does not necessitate the removal of lying but rather necessitates the obligation of the occurrence of compensation or the preventer in all circumstances.

And the interpretation of ugliness by the occurrence of harmony and disharmony is not correct according to them, for much of what is harmonious is ugly, like the anger of a poor person at what he needs from the [?] person, for that is harmonious for him and it is ugly; and much of what is disharmonious is good, like restraining the oppressor from oppression with various kinds of disciplinary measures, for it is disharmonious and good. And the verification in this matter is that it branches upon the occurrence of the act from the servant and its non-occurrence from him; if the servant is not the originator, then there is no ugly act, but if he is, then ugly exists, and consideration of their definitions among those who hold them indicates that.

I say: This wujub (obligation) is not the same as the legal judgment as terminologically used by the jurists, but rather this wujub means that the act is such that its abandoner deserves blame, just as qabih (ugly) means that the act is such that its doer deserves blame, and the discussion here is the same as the discussion of good and ugly. And they say that the powerful, knowing, self-sufficient one necessarily does not abandon the obligatory. And lutf according to them is an expression of everything that brings the servant closer to obedience and distances him from disobedience without reaching compulsion, and it is among the acts of God Almighty. And it is obligatory according to them after the establishment of obligation. And what he mentioned about creating the motive without lutf has already been discussed. As for compensation, they say that the occurrence of pains in creation is obligatory due to the obligation of the occurrence of their causes, and the powerful, just, self-sufficient one, if He creates that, then it is obligatory upon Him to compensate the one pained in view of His justice, and removing pain leads to evils, so He did not remove it. As for acts of obedience and obligations, if they were in exchange for the favors of God Almighty, then the rewarder would be God Almighty, and He is exalted above giving favor to take compensation for His favors; favors are only a gift from Him, and reward is the recompense of obligations. Invalidation of reward without the obligation of obedience is not possible because reward includes glorification and veneration, and that is ugly in the case of one who is not deserving. And the most beneficial is obligatory according to Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, but he does not say it is obligatory in all cases, rather he says in cases related to removing the excuses of the obligated.

[Text: 'Muhsin'? unclear]

And what he mentioned about punishment is the speech of the Murji'a and the Wa'idiyya: they say that the threat is lutf and it is obligatory, and fulfillment of the promise is obligatory, otherwise lying would be good. So this is their speech on this topic, and all of it is based on the servant being an agent by choice, and on the principles of acts and their ugliness and obligation, and if those foundations are demolished, all their deductions fall.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument against the Mu'tazila and others who hold that certain things are obligatory upon God. The author, Imam al-Razi, presents the Sunni position that nothing is obligatory upon God. He first states the issue and the opposing views: the Mu'tazila obligate lutf (grace), 'iwad (compensation), and thawab (reward), while the Baghdadis obligate punishment and the most beneficial. Then he gives the Sunni argument: judgments are based only on divine law, and there is no judge over divine law, so nothing is obligatory upon God. He then refutes each claimed obligation in turn. For lutf, he argues that if lutf is a motive that does not compel, God can create that motive directly without the intermediary of lutf, so it is not obligatory. For compensation, if it were obligatory, then preventing harm would be considered ugly, but that is not the case. For reward, God's favors make obedience possible, so reward is not obligatory. For the most beneficial, it is not obligatory because it could involve not creating a disbeliever or creating all in Paradise—these are not necessarily best. For punishment, since God has no benefit or harm from it, waiving it is good. The passage then addresses objections regarding the definition of ugliness and the possibility of lying. The commentator (likely Razi himself) adds a clarification that 'obligation' here is not legal but moral, and then elaborates on the Mu'tazila and others' views on lutf, compensation, reward, and punishment, arguing that all these positions depend on the premise that human acts are created by humans, which Razi rejects. The passage ends by asserting that if that premise is destroyed, all their deductions fall.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'obligation' here is the same as legal obligation (wajib shar'i).
Correction: The commentator clarifies that here 'wujub' means moral blameworthiness, not legal obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the foundation of all the Mu'tazila arguments about what is obligatory upon God?
A) The belief that God is just.
B) The belief that humans are free agents.
C) The belief that reward is necessary.
D) The belief that punishment is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila obligations on God rest on the premise of human free will; if that is denied, their whole system falls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that God owes you something—health, wealth, or understanding? In that moment, are you imposing your own sense of obligation on the Creator? How does this lesson challenge you to surrender that entitlement and accept that God is never in debt to anyone?
?
If moral obligation does not ultimately rest on human free will, what is the foundation for your own sense of right and wrong? Are you willing to let God define what is good and evil for you, even when it conflicts with your desires?
?
The Mu'tazila built their entire system on the premise that you create your own acts. If that premise is false, then your belief that you 'deserve' certain outcomes from God is built on sand. What would it cost you to abandon that belief today and submit fully to God's will, trusting that He is never unjust?

The Proof that God Acts Without Purpose
Razi gives two proofs and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that God cannot act for a purpose because it would imply self-perfection and deficiency.

Core Idea
If God acted for a purpose, He would be completing Himself—contradicting His perfection.

This passage presents al-Razi's initial argument against the Mu'tazila who claim that God acts for a purpose. Razi argues that such a view implies that God is perfecting Himself, which would mean He was deficient without that act. He also argues that any purpose is a contingent thing that God can create directly, making the act pointless. The passage also addresses an objection that some purposes require intermediaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Understand the first proof
The passage presents a proof: if God acts for a purpose, He is perfecting Himself, which implies deficiency. Understand why this is impossible for God.
3
Understand the second proof
Any purpose is a possible thing; God can create it directly, so mediating the act would be futile. Grasp the logic of this second argument.
This proof anticipates the objection about intermediaries.
4
Consider the objection and response
The opponent says some purposes require intermediaries. Razi responds that the only relevant purpose is the servant's pleasure, which God can grant directly. Note how this rebuttal strengthens the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا يجوز أن يفعل الله شيئاً لغرض خلافا للمعتزلة ولأكثر الفقهاء

لنا أن كل من كان كذلك كان مستكملا يفعل ذلك الشيء والمستكمل غيره ناقص لذاته ، ولأن كل غرض يفرض فهو من الممكنات فيكون الله تعالى قادراً على إيجاده ابتداء فيكون توسيط ذلك الفعل عبثاً . لا يقال لا يمكن تحصيله إلا بتلك الواسطة لانا نقول الذى يصلح أنه يكون لفرضنا ليس إلا اتصال اللذة إلى العبد وهو مقدور الله تعالى من غير شيء من الوسائط .

احتجوا بأن ما يفعل لا لغرض فهو عبث والعبث على الحكيم غير جائز .

قلنا إن أردت بالبث الخالى عن الغرض فهذا استدلال بالشيء على نفسه ، وإن أردت غيره فبينه

عوضاً عن نعمه، إنما النعم تفضل منه، والثواب جزاء التكاليف، وإبطال الثواب من غير تكليف الطاعة غير ممكن لكون الثواب مشتملا على التعظيم والإجلال ذلك في غير المستحق قبيح، والأصلح واجب عند أبي القاسم البلخى وهو لا يقول بوجوبه في جميع المواضع بل يقول في المواضع المتعلقة بإزاحة عال المكافين

محسن

وما ذكره فى العقاب فهو كلام المرجئة والوعيدية يقولون الوعيد لطف وهو واجب والوفاء بالقول واجب وإلا لمكان الكذب حسنا فهذا كلامهم في هذا الباب والجميع مبنى على كون العبد فاعلا باختيار والقوال الأفعال وقبحها ووجوبها وإذا انهدمت تلك القواعد سقط جميع استدلالاتهم

أقول المعتزلة يقولون فعل الحكيم لا يخلو عن غرض هو الداعى إلى ذلك الفعلى والالزم ترجيح من مرجح ، والفتهاء يقولون الحكم بالقصاص إنما ورد من الشارع ايزجر الناس عن القتل فهذا هو الفرض منه ، ثم إن المجتهدين فرعوا على ذلك الإذن والمنع فيما لم يصرح الشارع حكمه فيه على وجه يوافق الغرض ، وبعض القائلين بالأغراض يقولون المراد من الغرض سوق الاشياء الناقصة إلى كالاتها فمن الكمالات ما لا يحصل إلا بذلك السوق ، كما أن الجسم لا يمكن اتصاله من مكان إلى مكان إلا تحريكه وهو الغرض من تحريكه فتحصيل بعض الأغراض من غير توسيط الأفعال الخاصة بها محال والمحال غير مقدور عليه .

وقوله الصالح لكونه غرضا ليس إلا إيصال اللذة إلى العبد وهو مقدور من غير واسطة ليس بحكم كلى فإن لذة أحد أخره الكسب من غير الكسب ليس بمقدور والعبث ليس هو الفعل الخالي عن الغرض مطلقا بل يجب أن يراد فيه بشرط أن يكون من شأن ذلك الفعل أن يصدر عن فاعله المختار الغرض .

وأما قوله : الفاعل بغرض مستكمل بالغرض حكم أخذه من الحكماء استعمله في غير موضعه فإنهم لا ينفون سوق الأشياء إلى كمالاتها وإلا لبطل علم منافع الأعضاء وقواعد العلوم الحكمية من الطبيعيات وعلم الهيئة وغيرها وسقطت الملل الغائية بأسرها من الاعتبار ، بل يقولون إفاضة الموجودات عن مبدأها يكون على أكمل ما يمكن لا أن يخلق ناقصا ثم يكمله بقصد ثان بل خلقه مساقا إلى كماله باستئناف تدبير ، ويعنون بالغرض استئناف ذلك التدبير فى الإكمال بالقصد الثانى .

أما أهل السنة فيقولون إنه تعالى فعال لما يريد ليس من شأن فعله أن يوصف بحسن وقبح ، فكثير من الناقصين يعدمهم قبل استكمالهم وكثير من المتحركين يحركهم إلى غير غايات حركاتهم ولا يسئل في أفعاله بلم وكيف ؟
Issue: It is not permissible that God do anything for a purpose, contrary to the Mu'tazila and most jurists.

For us: Whoever is like that is perfecting himself by doing that thing, and the one who perfects something else is deficient in himself. And because every purpose that is supposed is among possible things, so God Almighty is able to create it initially, so the mediation of that act is futile. It should not be said that it cannot be obtained except by that intermediary, for we say: that which is suitable to be our purpose is nothing but the connection of pleasure to the servant, and that is within God's power without any intermediaries.

They argued: that which is done without a purpose is futile, and futility is not permissible for the wise.

We said: If you mean by futility that which is devoid of purpose, then this is arguing from the thing upon itself; but if you mean something else, then clarify it.

[Text appears: 'as compensation for His favors, favors are only a gift from Him, and reward is the recompense of obligations...' – possibly a misplaced continuation from earlier discussion]

[Text: 'Muhsin'?]

And what he mentioned about punishment is the speech of the Murji'a and the Wa'idiyya: they say that the threat is lutf and it is obligatory, and fulfillment of the promise is obligatory, otherwise lying would be good. So this is their speech on this topic, and all of it is based on the servant being an agent by choice, and on the principles of acts and their ugliness and obligation, and if those foundations are demolished, all their deductions fall.

I say: The Mu'tazila say that the action of the wise is not free from a purpose which is the motive for that action, and the necessary consequence is preponderance from a preponderator. And the jurists say that the ruling of retaliation came from the Lawgiver to deter people from killing, so this is the purpose of it. Then the mujtahids branched upon that permission and prohibition in matters where the Lawgiver has not explicitly stated His ruling, in a manner consistent with the purpose. And some of those who affirm purposes say that the meaning of purpose is the driving of imperfect things to their perfections; for some perfections are not attained except by that driving, just as a body cannot be moved from place to place except by moving it, and that is the purpose of moving it. So the attainment of some purposes without the mediation of specific acts is impossible, and the impossible is not within power.

And his statement that 'that which is suitable to be a purpose is nothing but the connection of pleasure to the servant, and that is within power without intermediary' is not a universal judgment, for the pleasure of one's afterlife from earning without earning is not within power. And futility is not the act devoid of purpose absolutely, rather it must be intended with the condition that it is in the nature of that act to issue from its agent with purpose.

And as for his statement: 'the agent with purpose is perfecting by the purpose' – a judgment he took from the philosophers and used in the wrong place. For they do not negate the driving of things to their perfections; otherwise the science of the benefits of organs and the principles of the philosophical sciences from physics and astronomy and others would be invalid, and all teleological sciences would fall from consideration. Rather, they say that the emanation of existents from their principle is in the most perfect way possible, not that He creates imperfect then completes it with a second intention, but rather He creates it driven to its perfection without renewal of management. And by 'purpose' they mean the renewal of that management in completion with a second intention.

As for the Ahl al-Sunna, they say that He, exalted, is a doer of what He wills; it is not in the nature of His act to be described as good or ugly. So He destroys many imperfect beings before their perfection, and moves many moving things to other than the goals of their movements, and He is not asked about His acts: 'Why?' and 'How?'.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument against the idea that God acts for any purpose, a position held by the Mu'tazila and many jurists. Al-Razi first states the Sunni position: it is not permissible for God to act for a purpose. He gives two proofs: (1) If God acted for a purpose, He would be perfecting Himself by that act, implying that He is deficient without it, which is impossible. (2) Every conceivable purpose is a possible thing, and God can create it directly without mediating acts; thus, the act would be futile. He anticipates an objection that some purposes cannot be obtained except through certain means, but responds that the only suitable purpose in this context is the connection of pleasure to the servant, and that is within God's power without any intermediary. The opponent then objects that an act without purpose is futile (عبث), and futility is not worthy of the wise. Al-Razi answers by distinguishing two meanings of 'futility': if it means 'devoid of purpose', then the objection is circular; if it means something else, it must be clarified. The passage then includes a discussion of related concepts like compensation and reward, before delving into an extensive commentary (أقول) that presents the views of the Mu'tazila, jurists, and philosophers on purposes, and finally reaffirms the Ash'ari position that God's acts are not subject to moral categories of good and ugly. The commentary explains that the Mu'tazila require a purpose as a motive; the jurists take the retribution ruling as having a purpose; some philosophers see purpose as driving imperfect things to perfection; and the Ash'aris maintain that God does what He wills and is not questioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must have a purpose for His actions, otherwise they are meaningless.
Correction: Razi shows that having a purpose implies imperfection in God, so the opposite is true.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first proof, why is it problematic for God to act for a purpose?
A) Because it would mean God is perfecting Himself, implying prior deficiency.
B) Because purposes are impossible for God to have.
C) Because the Mu'tazila do not believe in purposes.
D) Because purposes would limit God's power.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not for any purpose because that would imply self-perfection and deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says that if God acted for a purpose, He would be deficient without that purpose. Do you ever treat your acts of worship as if God needs them to be completed? How does that reveal a hidden assumption about God's lack? Repent of that mindset now, and commit to seeing your worship as purely a gift from Him.
?
The passage shows that any purpose God might have would be a possible thing He can create directly. Do you sometimes believe that certain blessings must come through specific means (a job, a person) rather than directly from God? This lesson calls you to rely on God's direct power. Is there a need you are clinging to a created means for? Surrender it to God right now.
?
Razi refutes the objection by saying the only relevant purpose is the servant's pleasure, which God can give without intermediaries. How does this challenge your impatience in dua? Do you think God needs your repeated actions to grant your request? Ask Him directly, with full trust that He can give you what you need without any intermediate cause.
