The Permissible Sins of Prophets Before Prophethood
Razi enumerates three permissible sinful acts and surveys sectarian views on infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three permissible ways prophets can sin before prophethood and the spectrum of scholarly views on the timing and nature of infallibility.

Core Idea
Prophetic sin before prophethood is restricted to three exceptional ways and requires repentance.

Razi tackles a delicate theological question: can prophets sin before receiving revelation? He clarifies that any wrongdoing must fall into one of three categories and then surveys the divergent opinions among early sects, from the extreme Fudayliyya to the majority Ahl al-Sunna. This passage is essential for understanding how Islamic orthodoxy balances prophetic infallibility with human fallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice the structure: first a general rule, then a list of three ways, then a series of sectarian positions, and finally a qualification about major sins.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail on first reading.
2
Identify the three permissible ways
Locate the three ways listed: inadvertence/forgetfulness, leaving what is better, and confusing the forbidden with the permissible. Understand why these are exceptions.
These three categories cover cognitive or moral errors, not deliberate rebellion.
3
Map the differing views on infallibility timing
Trace the views of al-Fudayliyya, Ibn Furak, al-Hashwiyya, and the investigators. Note what each says about the possibility of a prophet being a disbeliever before prophethood.
The Hashwiyya misinterpreted Quranic verses to claim the Prophet was once a disbeliever.
4
Understand the extension to imams
Notice how some scholars applied the same infallibility requirement to imams, and how this was used to criticize the first two caliphs.
This shows the political dimension of theological debates.
5
Grasp the majority position on major sins
Read the final section: most Ahl al-Sunna permit major sins before prophethood but only rarely and with repentance. Persistence in sin would defeat the purpose of prophethood.
The evidence from Joseph's brothers is used to support this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإقدام على المعصية مقيدا سواء كانت صغيرة أو كبيرة ، بل يجوز صدورها منهم على أحد وجوه ثلاثة أحدها السهو والنسيان ، والثاني ترك الأولى ، والثالث اشتباه المنهي بالمباح . واختلفوا في الوقت الذي تعتبر فيه العصمة ، أما الفضيلية من الخوارج فقد جوزوا بعثة من يعلم الله تعالى منه أنه يكفر ، ومنهم من لم يجوز ذلك [؟] جوز بعثة من كان كافرا قبل الرسالة ، وهو قول ابن فورك لكنه زعم أن هذا الجائز لم يقع، ومن الحشوية من زعم أن الرسول عليه السلام كان كافرا قبل البعثة لقوله: "ووجدك ضالا فهدى"، ولقوله: "وما كنت تدري ما الكتاب ولا الإيمان". واتفق المحصلون على فساد ذلك . ومن الناس من طرد هذا الحكم في الأئمة ، وقال : كما لا يجوز كون الرسول كافرا قبل البعثة لا يجوز أيضا أن يكون الإمام كافرا قبل الإمامة ، ولذلك يقدحون في إمامة الشيخين . فأما أنه هل يجوز فعل الكبيرة على الأنبياء قبل البعثة فالأكثرون من أهل السنة جوزوا ذلك مستدلين بأفعال إخوة يوسف ، ومنهم من لم يقل به ولم يقل بموتهم . ثم الذين جوزوا ذلك قالوا : منهم من فعل الكبيرة قبل البعثة ، لكنهم إنما جوزوا ذلك على سبيل الندرة يتوبون عنه ويستر حالهم فيما بين الخلق بالصلاح ، فأما لو أصرُّوا على الكبائر بحيث يصيرون مشهورين بالخلاعة فذلك غير جائز ، لأن المقصود من بعثتهم يفوت على ذلك التقدير.
Undertaking disobedience is restricted, whether it is minor or major; rather, it is permissible for it to occur from them in one of three ways: first, inadvertence and forgetfulness; second, leaving what is better; third, confusing the forbidden with the permissible. And they differed about the time in which infallibility is considered. As for al-Fudayliyya from the Khawarij, they permitted the sending of one whom Allah knows will disbelieve; and some of them did not permit that, [?] they permitted the sending of one who was a disbeliever before the message, and this is the saying of Ibn Furak, but he claimed that this permissible did not occur. And from the Hashwiyya are those who claimed that the Messenger, peace be upon him, was a disbeliever before the mission, due to His saying: 'And He found you lost and guided [you]' and His saying: 'And you did not know what the Book is, nor faith.' And the investigators agreed on the corruption of that. And among the people are those who applied this ruling to the imams, saying: Just as it is not permissible for the messenger to be a disbeliever before the mission, so it is also not permissible for the imam to be a disbeliever before the imamate; therefore they impugn the imamate of the two shaykhs. As for whether committing a major sin is permissible for prophets before the mission, the majority of Ahl al-Sunna permitted that, using as evidence the actions of the brothers of Joseph; and some did not affirm it and did not affirm their death. Then those who permitted it said: Among them are those who committed a major sin before the mission, but they only permitted that on rare occasions, and they repent from it and conceal their state among the people by righteousness. As for if they persist in major sins such that they become notorious for dissoluteness, that is not permissible, because the purpose of their mission would be lost on that estimation.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines the permissible ways in which prophets may commit sins before prophethood and discusses the timing of infallibility. The author begins by stating that any disobedience, whether minor or major, can only occur from prophets in one of three ways: (1) inadvertence and forgetfulness, (2) leaving what is better (ترك الأولى), and (3) confusing the forbidden with the permissible. Then he notes that scholars differ about when infallibility is considered. He cites the extreme view of al-Fudayliyya, who permit sending a prophet whom Allah knows will eventually disbelieve, and a similar view attributed to Ibn Furak (though he says it never occurred). He then refutes the Hashwiyya claim that the Prophet Muhammad was a disbeliever before prophethood, using Qur'anic verses out of context; the majority (المحصلون) agree this is false. The argument is extended to imams by some, who say that just as a prophet cannot be a disbeliever before prophethood, an imam cannot be a disbeliever before the imamate—this is used to impugn the imamate of Abu Bakr and Umar. Finally, the author discusses major sins before prophethood: most Ahl al-Sunna permit them, citing the sins of Joseph's brothers. However, they restrict this to rare occasions with repentance, and if a prophet became notorious for sin, it would defeat the purpose of his mission.

Key Terms
السهو والنسيان
ترك الأولى
اشتباه المنهي بالمباح
العصمة
الفضيلية
ابن فورك
الحشوية
المحصلون
الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets can commit any sin freely before prophethood, and infallibility only begins after the mission.
Correction: The passage restricts any sin to three specific categories (inadvertence, leaving the better, confusion) and requires repentance. Moreover, the majority of Ahl al-Sunna permit major sins only rarely and with repentance, not persistently.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three permissible ways a prophet can sin before prophethood?
A) Inadvertence and forgetfulness
B) Leaving what is better
C) Intentional rebellion (ma'siya muta'ammada)
D) Confusing the forbidden with the permissible

Key Takeaway
Prophetic sin before prophethood is neither unrestricted nor non-existent; it is limited to three exceptional categories, and even major sins are rare, repented, and concealed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you tend to excuse yourself by saying 'I forgot' or 'I didn't know,' even when you are not genuinely in that state? How does Razi's restriction of prophetic errors to only three specific categories challenge you to stop making excuses for deliberate disobedience?
?
Razi mentions that prophets repent from their pre-prophethood sins and conceal them among people. Are you quick to repent when you sin, or do you delay? Do you prefer to keep your sins hidden out of humility, or do you sometimes boast about them? What change must you make today?
?
If prophets had been notorious sinners, the purpose of their mission would be lost. You are not a prophet, but as a Muslim, you represent Islam to others. Is your public behavior such that it draws people to the faith or drives them away? What specific habit will you change this week to better reflect the character of the Prophet?

The Issue of Annihilation and Recreation: Not Decisively Proven
Razi states the issue and presents a scriptural proof for annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God annihilates and recreates parts is not established by decisive proof, and how the verse 'He is the First and the Last' is used as a proof by those who affirm it.

Core Idea
The decisive proof for annihilation is not decisive.

Imam Razi raises a critical subtle point: while many affirm that God will annihilate the universe and then recreate it, this specific sequence is not established by a definitive rational or scriptural proof. Yet, some scholars (al-qati'un) have argued from the Quranic verse 'He is the First and the Last' to support it. Let's examine Razi's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the issue statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Razi's opening claim: that the annihilation and recreation is not proven by a decisive proof.
Notice that Razi does not reject it outright; he says it is not decisively proven.
2
Identify the proof from al-qati'un
Identify the verse they cite and how they interpret 'Awwal' and 'Akhir'. Understand the logical connection: Last implies existence after all things, so things must become non-existent.
This proof is scriptural, not rational.
3
Reflect on Razi's stance
Consider why Razi might have included this proof even though he says it is not decisive. Is he showing the weakness of the proof or just reporting?
Razi often reports views without endorsing them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لم يثبت بدليل قاطع أن الله تعالى يعدم الأجزاء ثم يعيدها واحتج القاطعون عليه بآيات . أحدها : قوله تعالى وهو الأول والآخر، وإنما كان أولا لأنه كان موجودا قبل وجودها فكذا إنما يكون آخر لو كان موجودا بعد وجودها
Issue: It is not proven by decisive proof that God Almighty annihilates the parts and then recreates them. And those who assert that have argued with verses. One of them: His saying: He is the First and the Last. He is First because He existed before their existence, so likewise He would be Last only if He existed after their existence.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage introduces a new issue (mas'ala) that the specific sequence of annihilation and recreation is not established by a demonstrative proof. Razi then presents an argument from those who affirm it (al-qati'un): they cite the verse 'He is the First and the Last' (Quran 57:3). The reasoning is that just as God being 'First' means He existed before all things, 'Last' must mean He exists after all things, implying that things do become non-existent, otherwise there would be no 'after'. Thus, the verse supports the idea that God annihilates parts and then recreates them.

Key Terms
دليل قاطع
يعدم الأجزاء
الأول والآخر
القاطعون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the proof presented for God annihilating and recreating parts?
A) The world is created.
B) The verse 'He is the First and the Last' implies God exists after all things.
C) The resurrection itself.
D) The eternity of the world.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'He is the First and the Last' is used as a scriptural proof for annihilation, but Razi notes it is not a decisive proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'He is the First and the Last,' do you truly feel that God will outlast everything you hold dear—your loved ones, your possessions, your very self? What would shift in your heart if you lived with that certainty today?
?
Razi says this proof is not decisive. Are you quick to accept arguments that support what you want to believe? What is one belief about God that you hold without having critically examined it? Will you challenge it today, even if it's uncomfortable?
?
The verse is used to argue for annihilation, but you likely don't feel mortal. If you truly accepted that every part of you could be annihilated, what habit or attachment would you be forced to change? Name one change you will make this week to prepare for that reality.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Response to Objections on God's Eternity and Resurrection
Razi answers three objections regarding divine attributes and resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends theological doctrines against scriptural objections by reinterpreting key terms and limiting the scope of analogies.

Core Idea
Scriptural objections can be resolved by proper interpretation of terms and analogies.

Razi presents three objections based on Quranic verses that seem to contradict divine eternity and resurrection from non-existence. He responds by clarifying the meaning of 'first' and 'last', redefining 'perishing', and limiting the scope of analogical reasoning. This lesson walks through his responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the passage and note the three objections: the first (implied) about 'First and Last', the second about 'everything perishing', and the third about the repetition of creation.
The first objection is not explicitly stated but is inferred from the answer.
2
Understand Razi's reinterpretation of 'first' and 'last'
Notice how Razi shifts the meaning from temporal to entitlement-based, showing that divine attributes are not bound by time.
This avoids implying a beginning or end for God.
3
Analyze his redefinition of 'perishing'
Razi argues that 'perishing' does not mean non-existence but loss of benefit, and even if it meant non-existence, the verse requires interpretation.
He contrasts his interpretation with that of his opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : قوله تعالى وكل شيء هالك إلا وجهه ، والهلاك هو الفناء .
وثالثها : قوله تعالى كما بدأنا أول خلق نعيده بين أن الإعادة كالابتداء ، وكان الابتداء عن العدم فوجب أن تكون الإعادة أيضا عن العدم .
والجواب عن الأول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال هو الأول والآخر بحسب الاستحقاق لا بحسب الزمان .
الثاني : لا نسلم أن الهالك هو المعدوم بل هو الذي خرج عن حد الانتفاع ، والأجسام بعد تفرقها وهي تصير كذلك .
سلمنا أنه المعدوم لكن الآية على هذا التقدير لا يمكن إجراؤها على ظاهرها ، لأن وصفها مكونها هالكة يقتضي أن تكون معدومة في الحال وهو بالاتفاق باطل فوجب تأويلها ، فإنهم حملوها على أن ما لها إلى الهلاك ونحن حملناها على أنها قابلة للهلاك فلم يكن تأويلكم أولى من تأويلنا .
وعن الثالث أن تشبيه الشيء بغيره لا يقتضي مشابهتهما في كل الأمور
And the second: His statement 'Everything is perishing except His face', and perishing is annihilation. And the third: His statement 'Just as We began the first creation, We will repeat it' - it indicates that the repetition is like the beginning, and the beginning was from non-existence, so it is necessary that the repetition also be from non-existence. The answer to the first: Why is it not permissible to say that He is the First and the Last according to entitlement, not according to time? The second: We do not accept that the perishing is the non-existent, but rather that which has left the limit of benefit, and bodies after their disintegration and they become like that. We grant that it is the non-existent, but the verse on this estimation cannot be applied literarily, because describing them as perishing requires that they be non-existent at the present moment, and that is false by consensus, so it must be interpreted. For they interpreted it as what is heading towards perishing, while we interpreted it as what is susceptible to perishing; so your interpretation is not more worthy than ours. And regarding the third: That likening a thing to another does not require their similarity in all matters.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to theological positions and their answers. The first objection cites the verse 'Everything is perishing except His face' to argue that only Allah remains, implying temporal termination. The answer explains that 'First' and 'Last' refer to entitlement (استحقاق) not time. The second objection says the perishing mentioned means annihilation. The answer differentiates between non-existence and being useless; bodies after disintegration become useless but not non-existent. They further argue that even if perishing means non-existence, the verse cannot be literal since things would be non-existent now, which is false; so interpretation is needed, and their interpretation (heading towards perishing) is not better than Razi's (susceptible to perishing). The third objection uses the analogy of beginning and repetition to argue repetition from non-existence. The answer states that analogy does not require similarity in all aspects.

Key Terms
وجهه
هالك
فناء
الإعادة
الابتداء
الاستحقاق
الزمان
حد الانتفاع
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'first' and 'last' must be interpreted temporally.
Correction: Razi shows they can refer to entitlement, avoiding temporal implications for God.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that God being 'First and Last' implies a temporal beginning and end?
A) By saying it refers to entitlement, not time.
B) By denying the verse's authenticity.
C) By accepting the temporal interpretation.
D) By saying God is not first or last.

Key Takeaway
Razi resolves scriptural objections by reinterpreting divine attributes as relating to entitlement, redefining perishing as loss of benefit, and limiting analogies between creation and resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God is the First and the Last, do you instinctively think of your own beginning and end? How does Razi's reinterpretation challenge you to think of God's eternity as independent of time? Now, reflect: Are there areas in your life where you limit God by your own temporal understanding? What commitment can you make today to trust in God's timeless nature?
?
The verse says everything perishes except His face. Razi says perishing means becoming useless. Consider: What in your life has 'perished'? What have you held onto that has lost its benefit? Are you still clinging to things that have 'left the limit of benefit' in your spiritual life? What do you need to let go of to focus on God's face?
?
Razi argues that analogies between creation and resurrection are limited. In your own journey, do you assume that because something happened one way in the past, it must always happen that way? Are there patterns in your life where you limit God's power by focusing on the 'beginning'? How does this lesson free you to trust God's ability to do something new in your life?
